section. An implementation designed for use with a specific application
protocol might provide only those operations that are used by that
protocol.[¶](#section-5.3-1){.pilcrow}

When implementing the client role, an application protocol
can:[¶](#section-5.3-2){.pilcrow}

-   [open a connection, which begins the exchange described in [Section
    7](#handshake){.xref};[¶](#section-5.3-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-3.1}
-   [enable Early Data when available;
    and[¶](#section-5.3-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-3.2}
-   [be informed when Early Data has been accepted or rejected by a
    server.[¶](#section-5.3-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-3.3}

When implementing the server role, an application protocol
can:[¶](#section-5.3-4){.pilcrow}

-   [listen for incoming connections, which prepares for the exchange
    described in [Section
    7](#handshake){.xref};[¶](#section-5.3-5.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-5.1}
-   [if Early Data is supported, embed application-controlled data in
    the TLS resumption ticket sent to the client;
    and[¶](#section-5.3-5.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-5.2}
-   [if Early Data is supported, retrieve application-controlled data
    from the client\'s resumption ticket and accept or reject Early Data
    based on that
    information.[¶](#section-5.3-5.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-5.3}

In either role, an application protocol
can:[¶](#section-5.3-6){.pilcrow}

-   [configure minimum values for the initial number of permitted
    streams of each type, as communicated in the transport parameters
    ([Section
    7.4](#transport-parameters){.xref});[¶](#section-5.3-7.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-7.1}
-   [control resource allocation for receive buffers by setting flow
    control limits both for streams and for the
    connection;[¶](#section-5.3-7.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-7.2}
-   [identify whether the handshake has completed successfully or is
    still ongoing;[¶](#section-5.3-7.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-7.3}
-   [keep a connection from silently closing, by either generating PING
    frames ([Section 19.2](#frame-ping){.xref}) or requesting that the
    transport send additional frames before the idle timeout expires
    ([Section 10.1](#idle-timeout){.xref});
    and[¶](#section-5.3-7.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-7.4}
-   [immediately close ([Section 10.2](#immediate-close){.xref}) the
    connection.[¶](#section-5.3-7.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-5.3-7.5}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#version-negotiation}
::: {#section-6 .section}
## [6.](#section-6){.section-number .selfRef} [Version Negotiation](#name-version-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-version-negotiation}

Version negotiation allows a server to indicate that it does not support
the version the client used. A server sends a Version Negotiation packet
in response to each packet that might initiate a new connection; see
[Section 5.2](#packet-handling){.xref} for
details.[¶](#section-6-1){.pilcrow}

The size of the first packet sent by a client will determine whether a
server sends a Version Negotiation packet. Clients that support multiple
QUIC versions [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ensure that the first UDP datagram they
send is sized to the largest of the minimum datagram sizes from all
versions they support, using PADDING frames ([Section
19.1](#frame-padding){.xref}) as necessary. This ensures that the server
responds if there is a mutually supported version. A server might not
send a Version Negotiation packet if the datagram it receives is smaller
than the minimum size specified in a different version; see [Section
14.1](#initial-size){.xref}.[¶](#section-6-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#send-vn}
::: {#section-6.1 .section}
### [6.1.](#section-6.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Sending Version Negotiation Packets](#name-sending-version-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sending-version-negotiation}

If the version selected by the client is not acceptable to the server,
the server responds with a Version Negotiation packet; see [Section
17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}. This includes a list of versions that
the server will accept. An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a Version
Negotiation packet in response to receiving a Version Negotiation
packet.[¶](#section-6.1-1){.pilcrow}

This system allows a server to process packets with unsupported versions
without retaining state. Though either the Initial packet or the Version
Negotiation packet that is sent in response could be lost, the client
will send new packets until it successfully receives a response or it
abandons the connection attempt.[¶](#section-6.1-2){.pilcrow}

A server [MAY]{.bcp14} limit the number of Version Negotiation packets
it sends. For instance, a server that is able to recognize packets as
0-RTT might choose not to send Version Negotiation packets in response
to 0-RTT packets with the expectation that it will eventually receive an
Initial packet.[¶](#section-6.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#handle-vn}
::: {#section-6.2 .section}
### [6.2.](#section-6.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Handling Version Negotiation Packets](#name-handling-version-negotiatio){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handling-version-negotiatio}

Version Negotiation packets are designed to allow for functionality to
be defined in the future that allows QUIC to negotiate the version of
QUIC to use for a connection. Future Standards Track specifications
might change how implementations that support multiple versions of QUIC
react to Version Negotiation packets received in response to an attempt
to establish a connection using this
version.[¶](#section-6.2-1){.pilcrow}

A client that supports only this version of QUIC [MUST]{.bcp14} abandon
the current connection attempt if it receives a Version Negotiation
packet, with the following two exceptions. A client [MUST]{.bcp14}
discard any Version Negotiation packet if it has received and
successfully processed any other packet, including an earlier Version
Negotiation packet. A client [MUST]{.bcp14} discard a Version
Negotiation packet that lists the QUIC version selected by the
client.[¶](#section-6.2-2){.pilcrow}

How to perform version negotiation is left as future work defined by
future Standards Track specifications. In particular, that future work
will ensure robustness against version downgrade attacks; see [Section
21.12](#version-downgrade){.xref}.[¶](#section-6.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#using-reserved-versions}
::: {#section-6.3 .section}
### [6.3.](#section-6.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Using Reserved Versions](#name-using-reserved-versions){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-using-reserved-versions}

For a server to use a new version in the future, clients need to
correctly handle unsupported versions. Some version numbers (0x?a?a?a?a,
as defined in [Section 15](#versions){.xref}) are reserved for inclusion
in fields that contain version numbers.[¶](#section-6.3-1){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} add reserved versions to any field where unknown
or unsupported versions are ignored to test that a peer correctly
ignores the value. For instance, an endpoint could include a reserved
version in a Version Negotiation packet; see [Section
17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}. Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} send packets
with a reserved version to test that a peer correctly discards the
packet.[¶](#section-6.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#handshake}
::: {#section-7 .section}
## [7.](#section-7){.section-number .selfRef} [Cryptographic and Transport Handshake](#name-cryptographic-and-transport){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-cryptographic-and-transport}

QUIC relies on a combined cryptographic and transport handshake to
minimize connection establishment latency. QUIC uses the CRYPTO frame
([Section 19.6](#frame-crypto){.xref}) to transmit the cryptographic
handshake. The version of QUIC defined in this document is identified as
0x00000001 and uses TLS as described in
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]; a different QUIC version could
indicate that a different cryptographic handshake protocol is in
use.[¶](#section-7-1){.pilcrow}

QUIC provides reliable, ordered delivery of the cryptographic handshake
data. QUIC packet protection is used to encrypt as much of the handshake
protocol as possible. The cryptographic handshake [MUST]{.bcp14} provide
the following properties:[¶](#section-7-2){.pilcrow}

-   ::: {#section-7-3.1}
    authenticated key exchange, where[¶](#section-7-3.1.1){.pilcrow}

    -   [a server is always
        authenticated,[¶](#section-7-3.1.2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.1.2.1}
    -   [a client is optionally
        authenticated,[¶](#section-7-3.1.2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.1.2.2}
    -   [every connection produces distinct and unrelated keys,
        and[¶](#section-7-3.1.2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.1.2.3}
    -   [keying material is usable for packet protection for both 0-RTT
        and 1-RTT
        packets.[¶](#section-7-3.1.2.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.1.2.4}
    :::

-   [authenticated exchange of values for transport parameters of both
    endpoints, and confidentiality protection for server transport
    parameters (see [Section
    7.4](#transport-parameters){.xref}).[¶](#section-7-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.2}

-   [authenticated negotiation of an application protocol (TLS uses
    Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation (ALPN)
    \[[ALPN](#ALPN){.xref}\] for this
    purpose).[¶](#section-7-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7-3.3}

The CRYPTO frame can be sent in different packet number spaces ([Section
12.3](#packet-numbers){.xref}). The offsets used by CRYPTO frames to
ensure ordered delivery of cryptographic handshake data start from zero
in each packet number space.[¶](#section-7-4){.pilcrow}

[Figure 4](#fig-hs){.xref} shows a simplified handshake and the exchange
of packets and frames that are used to advance the handshake. Exchange
of application data during the handshake is enabled where possible,
shown with an asterisk (\"\*\"). Once the handshake is complete,
endpoints are able to exchange application data
freely.[¶](#section-7-5){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-simplified-quic-handshake}

::: {#fig-hs}
::: {#section-7-6.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                               Server

    Initial (CRYPTO)
    0-RTT (*)              ---------->
                                               Initial (CRYPTO)
                                             Handshake (CRYPTO)
                           <----------                1-RTT (*)
    Handshake (CRYPTO)
    1-RTT (*)              ---------->
                           <----------   1-RTT (HANDSHAKE_DONE)

    1-RTT                  <=========>                    1-RTT
:::

[Figure 4](#figure-4){.selfRef}: [Simplified QUIC
Handshake](#name-simplified-quic-handshake){.selfRef}
:::

Endpoints can use packets sent during the handshake to test for Explicit
Congestion Notification (ECN) support; see [Section 13.4](#ecn){.xref}.
An endpoint validates support for ECN by observing whether the ACK
frames acknowledging the first packets it sends carry ECN counts, as
described in [Section
13.4.2](#ecn-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-7-7){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} explicitly negotiate an application protocol.
This avoids situations where there is a disagreement about the protocol
that is in use.[¶](#section-7-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#example-handshake-flows}
::: {#section-7.1 .section}
### [7.1.](#section-7.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Example Handshake Flows](#name-example-handshake-flows){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-example-handshake-flows}

Details of how TLS is integrated with QUIC are provided in
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\], but some examples are provided here.
An extension of this exchange to support client address validation is
shown in [Section
8.1.2](#validate-retry){.xref}.[¶](#section-7.1-1){.pilcrow}

Once any address validation exchanges are complete, the cryptographic
handshake is used to agree on cryptographic keys. The cryptographic
handshake is carried in Initial ([Section
17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}) and Handshake ([Section
17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref}) packets.[¶](#section-7.1-2){.pilcrow}

[Figure 5](#tls-1rtt-handshake){.xref} provides an overview of the 1-RTT
handshake. Each line shows a QUIC packet with the packet type and packet
number shown first, followed by the frames that are typically contained
in those packets. For instance, the first packet is of type Initial,
with packet number 0, and contains a CRYPTO frame carrying the
ClientHello.[¶](#section-7.1-3){.pilcrow}

Multiple QUIC packets \-- even of different packet types \-- can be
coalesced into a single UDP datagram; see [Section
12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}. As a result, this handshake could
consist of as few as four UDP datagrams, or any number more (subject to
limits inherent to the protocol, such as congestion control and
anti-amplification). For instance, the server\'s first flight contains
Initial packets, Handshake packets, and \"0.5-RTT data\" in 1-RTT
packets.[¶](#section-7.1-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-example-1-rtt-handshake}

::: {#tls-1rtt-handshake}
::: {#section-7.1-5.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                                  Server

    Initial[0]: CRYPTO[CH] ->

                                     Initial[0]: CRYPTO[SH] ACK[0]
                           Handshake[0]: CRYPTO[EE, CERT, CV, FIN]
                                     <- 1-RTT[0]: STREAM[1, "..."]

    Initial[1]: ACK[0]
    Handshake[0]: CRYPTO[FIN], ACK[0]
    1-RTT[0]: STREAM[0, "..."], ACK[0] ->

                                              Handshake[1]: ACK[0]
             <- 1-RTT[1]: HANDSHAKE_DONE, STREAM[3, "..."], ACK[0]
:::

[Figure 5](#figure-5){.selfRef}: [Example 1-RTT
Handshake](#name-example-1-rtt-handshake){.selfRef}
:::

[Figure 6](#tls-0rtt-handshake){.xref} shows an example of a connection
with a 0-RTT handshake and a single packet of 0-RTT data. Note that as
described in [Section 12.3](#packet-numbers){.xref}, the server
acknowledges 0-RTT data in 1-RTT packets, and the client sends 1-RTT
packets in the same packet number space.[¶](#section-7.1-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-example-0-rtt-handshake}

::: {#tls-0rtt-handshake}
::: {#section-7.1-7.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                                  Server

    Initial[0]: CRYPTO[CH]
    0-RTT[0]: STREAM[0, "..."] ->

                                     Initial[0]: CRYPTO[SH] ACK[0]
                                      Handshake[0] CRYPTO[EE, FIN]
                              <- 1-RTT[0]: STREAM[1, "..."] ACK[0]

    Initial[1]: ACK[0]
    Handshake[0]: CRYPTO[FIN], ACK[0]
    1-RTT[1]: STREAM[0, "..."] ACK[0] ->

                                              Handshake[1]: ACK[0]
             <- 1-RTT[1]: HANDSHAKE_DONE, STREAM[3, "..."], ACK[1]
:::

[Figure 6](#figure-6){.selfRef}: [Example 0-RTT
Handshake](#name-example-0-rtt-handshake){.selfRef}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#negotiating-connection-ids}
::: {#section-7.2 .section}
### [7.2.](#section-7.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Negotiating Connection IDs](#name-negotiating-connection-ids){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-negotiating-connection-ids}

A connection ID is used to ensure consistent routing of packets, as
described in [Section 5.1](#connection-id){.xref}. The long header
contains two connection IDs: the Destination Connection ID is chosen by
the recipient of the packet and is used to provide consistent routing;
the Source Connection ID is used to set the Destination Connection ID
used by the peer.[¶](#section-7.2-1){.pilcrow}

During the handshake, packets with the long header ([Section
17.2](#long-header){.xref}) are used to establish the connection IDs
used by both endpoints. Each endpoint uses the Source Connection ID
field to specify the connection ID that is used in the Destination
Connection ID field of packets being sent to them. After processing the
first Initial packet, each endpoint sets the Destination Connection ID
field in subsequent packets it sends to the value of the Source
Connection ID field that it received.[¶](#section-7.2-2){.pilcrow}

When an Initial packet is sent by a client that has not previously
received an Initial or Retry packet from the server, the client
populates the Destination Connection ID field with an unpredictable
value. This Destination Connection ID [MUST]{.bcp14} be at least 8 bytes
in length. Until a packet is received from the server, the client
[MUST]{.bcp14} use the same Destination Connection ID value on all
packets in this connection.[¶](#section-7.2-3){.pilcrow}

The Destination Connection ID field from the first Initial packet sent
by a client is used to determine packet protection keys for Initial
packets. These keys change after receiving a Retry packet; see [Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-7.2-4){.pilcrow}

The client populates the Source Connection ID field with a value of its
choosing and sets the Source Connection ID Length field to indicate the
length.[¶](#section-7.2-5){.pilcrow}

0-RTT packets in the first flight use the same Destination Connection ID
and Source Connection ID values as the client\'s first Initial
packet.[¶](#section-7.2-6){.pilcrow}

Upon first receiving an Initial or Retry packet from the server, the
client uses the Source Connection ID supplied by the server as the
Destination Connection ID for subsequent packets, including any 0-RTT
packets. This means that a client might have to change the connection ID
it sets in the Destination Connection ID field twice during connection
establishment: once in response to a Retry packet and once in response
to an Initial packet from the server. Once a client has received a valid
Initial packet from the server, it [MUST]{.bcp14} discard any subsequent
packet it receives on that connection with a different Source Connection
ID.[¶](#section-7.2-7){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST]{.bcp14} change the Destination Connection ID it uses for
sending packets in response to only the first received Initial or Retry
packet. A server [MUST]{.bcp14} set the Destination Connection ID it
uses for sending packets based on the first received Initial packet. Any
further changes to the Destination Connection ID are only permitted if
the values are taken from NEW_CONNECTION_ID frames; if subsequent
Initial packets include a different Source Connection ID, they
[MUST]{.bcp14} be discarded. This avoids unpredictable outcomes that
might otherwise result from stateless processing of multiple Initial
packets with different Source Connection
IDs.[¶](#section-7.2-8){.pilcrow}

The Destination Connection ID that an endpoint sends can change over the
lifetime of a connection, especially in response to connection migration
([Section 9](#migration){.xref}); see [Section 5.1.1](#issue-cid){.xref}
for details.[¶](#section-7.2-9){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#cid-auth}
::: {#section-7.3 .section}
### [7.3.](#section-7.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Authenticating Connection IDs](#name-authenticating-connection-i){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authenticating-connection-i}

The choice each endpoint makes about connection IDs during the handshake
is authenticated by including all values in transport parameters; see
[Section 7.4](#transport-parameters){.xref}. This ensures that all
connection IDs used for the handshake are also authenticated by the
cryptographic handshake.[¶](#section-7.3-1){.pilcrow}

Each endpoint includes the value of the Source Connection ID field from
the first Initial packet it sent in the initial_source_connection_id
transport parameter; see [Section
18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}. A server includes the
Destination Connection ID field from the first Initial packet it
received from the client in the original_destination_connection_id
transport parameter; if the server sent a Retry packet, this refers to
the first Initial packet received before sending the Retry packet. If it
sends a Retry packet, a server also includes the Source Connection ID
field from the Retry packet in the retry_source_connection_id transport
parameter.[¶](#section-7.3-2){.pilcrow}

The values provided by a peer for these transport parameters
[MUST]{.bcp14} match the values that an endpoint used in the Destination
and Source Connection ID fields of Initial packets that it sent (and
received, for servers). Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} validate that received
transport parameters match received connection ID values. Including
connection ID values in transport parameters and verifying them ensures
that an attacker cannot influence the choice of connection ID for a
successful connection by injecting packets carrying attacker-chosen
connection IDs during the handshake.[¶](#section-7.3-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat the absence of the
initial_source_connection_id transport parameter from either endpoint or
the absence of the original_destination_connection_id transport
parameter from the server as a connection error of type
TRANSPORT_PARAMETER_ERROR.[¶](#section-7.3-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat the following as a connection error of
type TRANSPORT_PARAMETER_ERROR or
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION:[¶](#section-7.3-5){.pilcrow}

-   [absence of the retry_source_connection_id transport parameter from
    the server after receiving a Retry
    packet,[¶](#section-7.3-6.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-6.1}
-   [presence of the retry_source_connection_id transport parameter when
    no Retry packet was received,
    or[¶](#section-7.3-6.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-6.2}
-   [a mismatch between values received from a peer in these transport
    parameters and the value sent in the corresponding Destination or
    Source Connection ID fields of Initial
    packets.[¶](#section-7.3-6.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3-6.3}

If a zero-length connection ID is selected, the corresponding transport
parameter is included with a zero-length
value.[¶](#section-7.3-7){.pilcrow}

[Figure 7](#fig-auth-cid){.xref} shows the connection IDs (with
DCID=Destination Connection ID, SCID=Source Connection ID) that are used
in a complete handshake. The exchange of Initial packets is shown, plus
the later exchange of 1-RTT packets that includes the connection ID
established during the handshake.[¶](#section-7.3-8){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-use-of-connection-ids-in-a-}

::: {#fig-auth-cid}
::: {#section-7.3-9.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                                  Server

    Initial: DCID=S1, SCID=C1 ->
                                      <- Initial: DCID=C1, SCID=S3
                                 ...
    1-RTT: DCID=S3 ->
                                                 <- 1-RTT: DCID=C1
:::

[Figure 7](#figure-7){.selfRef}: [Use of Connection IDs in a
Handshake](#name-use-of-connection-ids-in-a-){.selfRef}
:::

[Figure 8](#fig-auth-cid-retry){.xref} shows a similar handshake that
includes a Retry packet.[¶](#section-7.3-10){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-use-of-connection-ids-in-a-h}

::: {#fig-auth-cid-retry}
::: {#section-7.3-11.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                                  Server

    Initial: DCID=S1, SCID=C1 ->
                                        <- Retry: DCID=C1, SCID=S2
    Initial: DCID=S2, SCID=C1 ->
                                      <- Initial: DCID=C1, SCID=S3
                                 ...
    1-RTT: DCID=S3 ->
                                                 <- 1-RTT: DCID=C1
:::

[Figure 8](#figure-8){.selfRef}: [Use of Connection IDs in a Handshake
with Retry](#name-use-of-connection-ids-in-a-h){.selfRef}
:::

In both cases (Figures [7](#fig-auth-cid){.xref} and
[8](#fig-auth-cid-retry){.xref}), the client sets the value of the
initial_source_connection_id transport parameter to
`C1`.[¶](#section-7.3-12){.pilcrow}

When the handshake does not include a Retry ([Figure
7](#fig-auth-cid){.xref}), the server sets
original_destination_connection_id to `S1` (note that this value is
chosen by the client) and initial_source_connection_id to `S3`. In this
case, the server does not include a retry_source_connection_id transport
parameter.[¶](#section-7.3-13){.pilcrow}

When the handshake includes a Retry ([Figure
8](#fig-auth-cid-retry){.xref}), the server sets
original_destination_connection_id to `S1`, retry_source_connection_id
to `S2`, and initial_source_connection_id to
`S3`.[¶](#section-7.3-14){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#transport-parameters}
::: {#section-7.4 .section}
### [7.4.](#section-7.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Transport Parameters](#name-transport-parameters){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-transport-parameters}

During connection establishment, both endpoints make authenticated
declarations of their transport parameters. Endpoints are required to
comply with the restrictions that each parameter defines; the
description of each parameter includes rules for its
handling.[¶](#section-7.4-1){.pilcrow}

Transport parameters are declarations that are made unilaterally by each
endpoint. Each endpoint can choose values for transport parameters
independent of the values chosen by its
peer.[¶](#section-7.4-2){.pilcrow}

The encoding of the transport parameters is detailed in [Section
18](#transport-parameter-encoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-7.4-3){.pilcrow}

QUIC includes the encoded transport parameters in the cryptographic
handshake. Once the handshake completes, the transport parameters
declared by the peer are available. Each endpoint validates the values
provided by its peer.[¶](#section-7.4-4){.pilcrow}

Definitions for each of the defined transport parameters are included in
[Section
18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}.[¶](#section-7.4-5){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat receipt of a transport parameter with
an invalid value as a connection error of type
TRANSPORT_PARAMETER_ERROR.[¶](#section-7.4-6){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a parameter more than once in a
given transport parameters extension. An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} treat
receipt of duplicate transport parameters as a connection error of type
TRANSPORT_PARAMETER_ERROR.[¶](#section-7.4-7){.pilcrow}

Endpoints use transport parameters to authenticate the negotiation of
connection IDs during the handshake; see [Section
7.3](#cid-auth){.xref}.[¶](#section-7.4-8){.pilcrow}

ALPN (see \[[ALPN](#ALPN){.xref}\]) allows clients to offer multiple
application protocols during connection establishment. The transport
parameters that a client includes during the handshake apply to all
application protocols that the client offers. Application protocols can
recommend values for transport parameters, such as the initial flow
control limits. However, application protocols that set constraints on
values for transport parameters could make it impossible for a client to
offer multiple application protocols if these constraints
conflict.[¶](#section-7.4-9){.pilcrow}

::: {#zerortt-parameters}
::: {#section-7.4.1 .section}
#### [7.4.1.](#section-7.4.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Values of Transport Parameters for 0-RTT](#name-values-of-transport-paramet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-values-of-transport-paramet}

Using 0-RTT depends on both client and server using protocol parameters
that were negotiated from a previous connection. To enable 0-RTT,
endpoints store the values of the server transport parameters with any
session tickets it receives on the connection. Endpoints also store any
information required by the application protocol or cryptographic
handshake; see [Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]. The values of stored transport
parameters are used when attempting 0-RTT using the session
tickets.[¶](#section-7.4.1-1){.pilcrow}

Remembered transport parameters apply to the new connection until the
handshake completes and the client starts sending 1-RTT packets. Once
the handshake completes, the client uses the transport parameters
established in the handshake. Not all transport parameters are
remembered, as some do not apply to future connections or they have no
effect on the use of 0-RTT.[¶](#section-7.4.1-2){.pilcrow}

The definition of a new transport parameter ([Section
7.4.2](#new-transport-parameters){.xref}) [MUST]{.bcp14} specify whether
storing the transport parameter for 0-RTT is mandatory, optional, or
prohibited. A client need not store a transport parameter it cannot
process.[¶](#section-7.4.1-3){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} use remembered values for the following
parameters: ack_delay_exponent, max_ack_delay,
initial_source_connection_id, original_destination_connection_id,
preferred_address, retry_source_connection_id, and
stateless_reset_token. The client [MUST]{.bcp14} use the server\'s new
values in the handshake instead; if the server does not provide new
values, the default values are used.[¶](#section-7.4.1-4){.pilcrow}

A client that attempts to send 0-RTT data [MUST]{.bcp14} remember all
other transport parameters used by the server that it is able to
process. The server can remember these transport parameters or can store
an integrity-protected copy of the values in the ticket and recover the
information when accepting 0-RTT data. A server uses the transport
parameters in determining whether to accept 0-RTT
data.[¶](#section-7.4.1-5){.pilcrow}

If 0-RTT data is accepted by the server, the server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14}
reduce any limits or alter any values that might be violated by the
client with its 0-RTT data. In particular, a server that accepts 0-RTT
data [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} set values for the following parameters
([Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}) that are
smaller than the remembered values of the
parameters.[¶](#section-7.4.1-6){.pilcrow}

-   [active_connection_id_limit[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.1}
-   [initial_max_data[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.2}
-   [initial_max_stream_data_bidi_local[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.3}
-   [initial_max_stream_data_bidi_remote[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.4}
-   [initial_max_stream_data_uni[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.5}
-   [initial_max_streams_bidi[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.6){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.6}
-   [initial_max_streams_uni[¶](#section-7.4.1-7.7){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.4.1-7.7}

Omitting or setting a zero value for certain transport parameters can
result in 0-RTT data being enabled but not usable. The applicable subset
of transport parameters that permit the sending of application data
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be set to non-zero values for 0-RTT. This includes
initial_max_data and either (1) initial_max_streams_bidi and
initial_max_stream_data_bidi_remote or (2) initial_max_streams_uni and
initial_max_stream_data_uni.[¶](#section-7.4.1-8){.pilcrow}

A server might provide larger initial stream flow control limits for
streams than the remembered values that a client applies when sending
0-RTT. Once the handshake completes, the client updates the flow control
limits on all sending streams using the updated values of
initial_max_stream_data_bidi_remote and
initial_max_stream_data_uni.[¶](#section-7.4.1-9){.pilcrow}

A server [MAY]{.bcp14} store and recover the previously sent values of
the max_idle_timeout, max_udp_payload_size, and disable_active_migration
parameters and reject 0-RTT if it selects smaller values. Lowering the
values of these parameters while also accepting 0-RTT data could degrade
the performance of the connection. Specifically, lowering the
max_udp_payload_size could result in dropped packets, leading to worse
performance compared to rejecting 0-RTT data
outright.[¶](#section-7.4.1-10){.pilcrow}

A server [MUST]{.bcp14} reject 0-RTT data if the restored values for
transport parameters cannot be
supported.[¶](#section-7.4.1-11){.pilcrow}

When sending frames in 0-RTT packets, a client [MUST]{.bcp14} only use
remembered transport parameters; importantly, it [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} use
updated values that it learns from the server\'s updated transport
parameters or from frames received in 1-RTT packets. Updated values of
transport parameters from the handshake apply only to 1-RTT packets. For
instance, flow control limits from remembered transport parameters apply
to all 0-RTT packets even if those values are increased by the handshake
or by frames sent in 1-RTT packets. A server [MAY]{.bcp14} treat the use
of updated transport parameters in 0-RTT as a connection error of type
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-7.4.1-12){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#new-transport-parameters}
::: {#section-7.4.2 .section}
#### [7.4.2.](#section-7.4.2){.section-number .selfRef} [New Transport Parameters](#name-new-transport-parameters){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-new-transport-parameters}

New transport parameters can be used to negotiate new protocol behavior.
An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} ignore transport parameters that it does not
support. The absence of a transport parameter therefore disables any
optional protocol feature that is negotiated using the parameter. As
described in [Section 18.1](#transport-parameter-grease){.xref}, some
identifiers are reserved in order to exercise this
requirement.[¶](#section-7.4.2-1){.pilcrow}

A client that does not understand a transport parameter can discard it
and attempt 0-RTT on subsequent connections. However, if the client adds
support for a discarded transport parameter, it risks violating the
constraints that the transport parameter establishes if it attempts
0-RTT. New transport parameters can avoid this problem by setting a
default of the most conservative value. Clients can avoid this problem
by remembering all parameters, even those not currently
supported.[¶](#section-7.4.2-2){.pilcrow}

New transport parameters can be registered according to the rules in
[Section
22.3](#iana-transport-parameters){.xref}.[¶](#section-7.4.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#cryptographic-message-buffering}
::: {#section-7.5 .section}
### [7.5.](#section-7.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Cryptographic Message Buffering](#name-cryptographic-message-buffe){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-cryptographic-message-buffe}

Implementations need to maintain a buffer of CRYPTO data received out of
order. Because there is no flow control of CRYPTO frames, an endpoint
could potentially force its peer to buffer an unbounded amount of
data.[¶](#section-7.5-1){.pilcrow}

Implementations [MUST]{.bcp14} support buffering at least 4096 bytes of
data received in out-of-order CRYPTO frames. Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14}
choose to allow more data to be buffered during the handshake. A larger
limit during the handshake could allow for larger keys or credentials to
be exchanged. An endpoint\'s buffer size does not need to remain
constant during the life of the connection.[¶](#section-7.5-2){.pilcrow}

Being unable to buffer CRYPTO frames during the handshake can lead to a
connection failure. If an endpoint\'s buffer is exceeded during the
handshake, it can expand its buffer temporarily to complete the
handshake. If an endpoint does not expand its buffer, it [MUST]{.bcp14}
close the connection with a CRYPTO_BUFFER_EXCEEDED error
code.[¶](#section-7.5-3){.pilcrow}

Once the handshake completes, if an endpoint is unable to buffer all
data in a CRYPTO frame, it [MAY]{.bcp14} discard that CRYPTO frame and
all CRYPTO frames received in the future, or it [MAY]{.bcp14} close the
connection with a CRYPTO_BUFFER_EXCEEDED error code. Packets containing
discarded CRYPTO frames [MUST]{.bcp14} be acknowledged because the
packet has been received and processed by the transport even though the
CRYPTO frame was discarded.[¶](#section-7.5-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#address-validation}
::: {#section-8 .section}
## [8.](#section-8){.section-number .selfRef} [Address Validation](#name-address-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-address-validation}

Address validation ensures that an endpoint cannot be used for a traffic
amplification attack. In such an attack, a packet is sent to a server
with spoofed source address information that identifies a victim. If a
server generates more or larger packets in response to that packet, the
attacker can use the server to send more data toward the victim than it
would be able to send on its own.[¶](#section-8-1){.pilcrow}

The primary defense against amplification attacks is verifying that a
peer is able to receive packets at the transport address that it claims.
Therefore, after receiving packets from an address that is not yet
validated, an endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} limit the amount of data it sends
to the unvalidated address to three times the amount of data received
from that address. This limit on the size of responses is known as the
anti-amplification limit.[¶](#section-8-2){.pilcrow}

Address validation is performed both during connection establishment
(see [Section 8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref}) and during connection
migration (see [Section
8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}).[¶](#section-8-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#validate-handshake}
::: {#section-8.1 .section}
### [8.1.](#section-8.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Address Validation during Connection Establishment](#name-address-validation-during-c){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-address-validation-during-c}

Connection establishment implicitly provides address validation for both
endpoints. In particular, receipt of a packet protected with Handshake
keys confirms that the peer successfully processed an Initial packet.
Once an endpoint has successfully processed a Handshake packet from the
peer, it can consider the peer address to have been
validated.[¶](#section-8.1-1){.pilcrow}

Additionally, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} consider the peer address
validated if the peer uses a connection ID chosen by the endpoint and
the connection ID contains at least 64 bits of
entropy.[¶](#section-8.1-2){.pilcrow}

For the client, the value of the Destination Connection ID field in its
first Initial packet allows it to validate the server address as a part
of successfully processing any packet. Initial packets from the server
are protected with keys that are derived from this value (see [Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]). Alternatively, the value is echoed by
the server in Version Negotiation packets ([Section
6](#version-negotiation){.xref}) or included in the Integrity Tag in
Retry packets ([Section
5.8](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.8){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-8.1-3){.pilcrow}

Prior to validating the client address, servers [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send
more than three times as many bytes as the number of bytes they have
received. This limits the magnitude of any amplification attack that can
be mounted using spoofed source addresses. For the purposes of avoiding
amplification prior to address validation, servers [MUST]{.bcp14} count
all of the payload bytes received in datagrams that are uniquely
attributed to a single connection. This includes datagrams that contain
packets that are successfully processed and datagrams that contain
packets that are all discarded.[¶](#section-8.1-4){.pilcrow}

Clients [MUST]{.bcp14} ensure that UDP datagrams containing Initial
packets have UDP payloads of at least 1200 bytes, adding PADDING frames
as necessary. A client that sends padded datagrams allows the server to
send more data prior to completing address
validation.[¶](#section-8.1-5){.pilcrow}

Loss of an Initial or Handshake packet from the server can cause a
deadlock if the client does not send additional Initial or Handshake
packets. A deadlock could occur when the server reaches its
anti-amplification limit and the client has received acknowledgments for
all the data it has sent. In this case, when the client has no reason to
send additional packets, the server will be unable to send more data
because it has not validated the client\'s address. To prevent this
deadlock, clients [MUST]{.bcp14} send a packet on a Probe Timeout (PTO);
see [Section
6.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]. Specifically, the client
[MUST]{.bcp14} send an Initial packet in a UDP datagram that contains at
least 1200 bytes if it does not have Handshake keys, and otherwise send
a Handshake packet.[¶](#section-8.1-6){.pilcrow}

A server might wish to validate the client address before starting the
cryptographic handshake. QUIC uses a token in the Initial packet to
provide address validation prior to completing the handshake. This token
is delivered to the client during connection establishment with a Retry
packet (see [Section 8.1.2](#validate-retry){.xref}) or in a previous
connection using the NEW_TOKEN frame (see [Section
8.1.3](#validate-future){.xref}).[¶](#section-8.1-7){.pilcrow}

In addition to sending limits imposed prior to address validation,
servers are also constrained in what they can send by the limits set by
the congestion controller. Clients are only constrained by the
congestion controller.[¶](#section-8.1-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#token-differentiation}
::: {#section-8.1.1 .section}
#### [8.1.1.](#section-8.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Token Construction](#name-token-construction){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-token-construction}

A token sent in a NEW_TOKEN frame or a Retry packet [MUST]{.bcp14} be
constructed in a way that allows the server to identify how it was
provided to a client. These tokens are carried in the same field but
require different handling from servers.[¶](#section-8.1.1-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#validate-retry}
::: {#section-8.1.2 .section}
#### [8.1.2.](#section-8.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Address Validation Using Retry Packets](#name-address-validation-using-re){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-address-validation-using-re}

Upon receiving the client\'s Initial packet, the server can request
address validation by sending a Retry packet ([Section
17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}) containing a token. This token
[MUST]{.bcp14} be repeated by the client in all Initial packets it sends
for that connection after it receives the Retry
packet.[¶](#section-8.1.2-1){.pilcrow}

In response to processing an Initial packet containing a token that was
provided in a Retry packet, a server cannot send another Retry packet;
it can only refuse the connection or permit it to
proceed.[¶](#section-8.1.2-2){.pilcrow}

As long as it is not possible for an attacker to generate a valid token
for its own address (see [Section 8.1.4](#token-integrity){.xref}) and
the client is able to return that token, it proves to the server that it
received the token.[¶](#section-8.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

A server can also use a Retry packet to defer the state and processing
costs of connection establishment. Requiring the server to provide a
different connection ID, along with the
original_destination_connection_id transport parameter defined in
[Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}, forces the
server to demonstrate that it, or an entity it cooperates with, received
the original Initial packet from the client. Providing a different
connection ID also grants a server some control over how subsequent
packets are routed. This can be used to direct connections to a
different server instance.[¶](#section-8.1.2-4){.pilcrow}

If a server receives a client Initial that contains an invalid Retry
token but is otherwise valid, it knows the client will not accept
another Retry token. The server can discard such a packet and allow the
client to time out to detect handshake failure, but that could impose a
significant latency penalty on the client. Instead, the server
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} immediately close ([Section
10.2](#immediate-close){.xref}) the connection with an INVALID_TOKEN
error. Note that a server has not established any state for the
connection at this point and so does not enter the closing
period.[¶](#section-8.1.2-5){.pilcrow}

A flow showing the use of a Retry packet is shown in [Figure
9](#fig-retry){.xref}.[¶](#section-8.1.2-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-example-handshake-with-retr}

::: {#fig-retry}
::: {#section-8.1.2-7.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Client                                                  Server

    Initial[0]: CRYPTO[CH] ->

                                                    <- Retry+Token

    Initial+Token[1]: CRYPTO[CH] ->

                                     Initial[0]: CRYPTO[SH] ACK[1]
                           Handshake[0]: CRYPTO[EE, CERT, CV, FIN]
                                     <- 1-RTT[0]: STREAM[1, "..."]
:::

[Figure 9](#figure-9){.selfRef}: [Example Handshake with
Retry](#name-example-handshake-with-retr){.selfRef}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#validate-future}
::: {#section-8.1.3 .section}
#### [8.1.3.](#section-8.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Address Validation for Future Connections](#name-address-validation-for-futu){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-address-validation-for-futu}

A server [MAY]{.bcp14} provide clients with an address validation token
during one connection that can be used on a subsequent connection.
Address validation is especially important with 0-RTT because a server
potentially sends a significant amount of data to a client in response
to 0-RTT data.[¶](#section-8.1.3-1){.pilcrow}

The server uses the NEW_TOKEN frame ([Section
19.7](#frame-new-token){.xref}) to provide the client with an address
validation token that can be used to validate future connections. In a
future connection, the client includes this token in Initial packets to
provide address validation. The client [MUST]{.bcp14} include the token
in all Initial packets it sends, unless a Retry replaces the token with
a newer one. The client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} use the token provided in a
Retry for future connections. Servers [MAY]{.bcp14} discard any Initial
packet that does not carry the expected
token.[¶](#section-8.1.3-2){.pilcrow}

Unlike the token that is created for a Retry packet, which is used
immediately, the token sent in the NEW_TOKEN frame can be used after
some period of time has passed. Thus, a token [SHOULD]{.bcp14} have an
expiration time, which could be either an explicit expiration time or an
issued timestamp that can be used to dynamically calculate the
expiration time. A server can store the expiration time or include it in
an encrypted form in the token.[¶](#section-8.1.3-3){.pilcrow}

A token issued with NEW_TOKEN [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} include information
that would allow values to be linked by an observer to the connection on
which it was issued. For example, it cannot include the previous
connection ID or addressing information, unless the values are
encrypted. A server [MUST]{.bcp14} ensure that every NEW_TOKEN frame it
sends is unique across all clients, with the exception of those sent to
repair losses of previously sent NEW_TOKEN frames. Information that
allows the server to distinguish between tokens from Retry and NEW_TOKEN
[MAY]{.bcp14} be accessible to entities other than the
server.[¶](#section-8.1.3-4){.pilcrow}

It is unlikely that the client port number is the same on two different
connections; validating the port is therefore unlikely to be
successful.[¶](#section-8.1.3-5){.pilcrow}

A token received in a NEW_TOKEN frame is applicable to any server that
the connection is considered authoritative for (e.g., server names
included in the certificate). When connecting to a server for which the
client retains an applicable and unused token, it [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
include that token in the Token field of its Initial packet. Including a
token might allow the server to validate the client address without an
additional round trip. A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} include a token that
is not applicable to the server that it is connecting to, unless the
client has the knowledge that the server that issued the token and the
server the client is connecting to are jointly managing the tokens. A
client [MAY]{.bcp14} use a token from any previous connection to that
server.[¶](#section-8.1.3-6){.pilcrow}

A token allows a server to correlate activity between the connection
where the token was issued and any connection where it is used. Clients
that want to break continuity of identity with a server can discard
tokens provided using the NEW_TOKEN frame. In comparison, a token
obtained in a Retry packet [MUST]{.bcp14} be used immediately during the
connection attempt and cannot be used in subsequent connection
attempts.[¶](#section-8.1.3-7){.pilcrow}

A client [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} reuse a token from a NEW_TOKEN frame for
different connection attempts. Reusing a token allows connections to be
linked by entities on the network path; see [Section
9.5](#migration-linkability){.xref}.[¶](#section-8.1.3-8){.pilcrow}

Clients might receive multiple tokens on a single connection. Aside from
preventing linkability, any token can be used in any connection attempt.
Servers can send additional tokens to either enable address validation
for multiple connection attempts or replace older tokens that might
become invalid. For a client, this ambiguity means that sending the most
recent unused token is most likely to be effective. Though saving and
using older tokens have no negative consequences, clients can regard
older tokens as being less likely to be useful to the server for address
validation.[¶](#section-8.1.3-9){.pilcrow}

When a server receives an Initial packet with an address validation
token, it [MUST]{.bcp14} attempt to validate the token, unless it has
already completed address validation. If the token is invalid, then the
server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} proceed as if the client did not have a
validated address, including potentially sending a Retry packet. Tokens
provided with NEW_TOKEN frames and Retry packets can be distinguished by
servers (see [Section 8.1.1](#token-differentiation){.xref}), and the
latter can be validated more strictly. If the validation succeeds, the
server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} then allow the handshake to
proceed.[¶](#section-8.1.3-10){.pilcrow}

Note: The rationale for treating the client as unvalidated rather than
discarding the packet is that the client might have received the token
in a previous connection using the NEW_TOKEN frame, and if the server
has lost state, it might be unable to validate the token at all, leading
to connection failure if the packet is
discarded.[¶](#section-8.1.3-11.1){.pilcrow}

In a stateless design, a server can use encrypted and authenticated
tokens to pass information to clients that the server can later recover
and use to validate a client address. Tokens are not integrated into the
cryptographic handshake, and so they are not authenticated. For
instance, a client might be able to reuse a token. To avoid attacks that
exploit this property, a server can limit its use of tokens to only the
information needed to validate client
addresses.[¶](#section-8.1.3-12){.pilcrow}

Clients [MAY]{.bcp14} use tokens obtained on one connection for any
connection attempt using the same version. When selecting a token to
use, clients do not need to consider other properties of the connection
that is being attempted, including the choice of possible application
protocols, session tickets, or other connection
properties.[¶](#section-8.1.3-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#token-integrity}
::: {#section-8.1.4 .section}
#### [8.1.4.](#section-8.1.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Address Validation Token Integrity](#name-address-validation-token-in){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-address-validation-token-in}

An address validation token [MUST]{.bcp14} be difficult to guess.
Including a random value with at least 128 bits of entropy in the token
would be sufficient, but this depends on the server remembering the
value it sends to clients.[¶](#section-8.1.4-1){.pilcrow}

A token-based scheme allows the server to offload any state associated
with validation to the client. For this design to work, the token
[MUST]{.bcp14} be covered by integrity protection against modification
or falsification by clients. Without integrity protection, malicious
clients could generate or guess values for tokens that would be accepted
by the server. Only the server requires access to the integrity
protection key for tokens.[¶](#section-8.1.4-2){.pilcrow}

There is no need for a single well-defined format for the token because
the server that generates the token also consumes it. Tokens sent in
Retry packets [SHOULD]{.bcp14} include information that allows the
server to verify that the source IP address and port in client packets
remain constant.[¶](#section-8.1.4-3){.pilcrow}

Tokens sent in NEW_TOKEN frames [MUST]{.bcp14} include information that
allows the server to verify that the client IP address has not changed
from when the token was issued. Servers can use tokens from NEW_TOKEN
frames in deciding not to send a Retry packet, even if the client
address has changed. If the client IP address has changed, the server
[MUST]{.bcp14} adhere to the anti-amplification limit; see [Section
8](#address-validation){.xref}. Note that in the presence of NAT, this
requirement might be insufficient to protect other hosts that share the
NAT from amplification attacks.[¶](#section-8.1.4-4){.pilcrow}

Attackers could replay tokens to use servers as amplifiers in DDoS
attacks. To protect against such attacks, servers [MUST]{.bcp14} ensure
that replay of tokens is prevented or limited. Servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
ensure that tokens sent in Retry packets are only accepted for a short
time, as they are returned immediately by clients. Tokens that are
provided in NEW_TOKEN frames ([Section 19.7](#frame-new-token){.xref})
need to be valid for longer but [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} be accepted
multiple times. Servers are encouraged to allow tokens to be used only
once, if possible; tokens [MAY]{.bcp14} include additional information
about clients to further narrow applicability or
reuse.[¶](#section-8.1.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#migrate-validate}
::: {#section-8.2 .section}
### [8.2.](#section-8.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Path Validation](#name-path-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-path-validation}

Path validation is used by both peers during connection migration (see
[Section 9](#migration){.xref}) to verify reachability after a change of
address. In path validation, endpoints test reachability between a
specific local address and a specific peer address, where an address is
the 2-tuple of IP address and port.[¶](#section-8.2-1){.pilcrow}

Path validation tests that packets sent on a path to a peer are received
by that peer. Path validation is used to ensure that packets received
from a migrating peer do not carry a spoofed source
address.[¶](#section-8.2-2){.pilcrow}

Path validation does not validate that a peer can send in the return
direction. Acknowledgments cannot be used for return path validation
because they contain insufficient entropy and might be spoofed.
Endpoints independently determine reachability on each direction of a
path, and therefore return reachability can only be established by the
peer.[¶](#section-8.2-3){.pilcrow}

Path validation can be used at any time by either endpoint. For
instance, an endpoint might check that a peer is still in possession of
its address after a period of quiescence.[¶](#section-8.2-4){.pilcrow}

Path validation is not designed as a NAT traversal mechanism. Though the
mechanism described here might be effective for the creation of NAT
bindings that support NAT traversal, the expectation is that one
endpoint is able to receive packets without first having sent a packet
on that path. Effective NAT traversal needs additional synchronization
mechanisms that are not provided here.[¶](#section-8.2-5){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} include other frames with the PATH_CHALLENGE
and PATH_RESPONSE frames used for path validation. In particular, an
endpoint can include PADDING frames with a PATH_CHALLENGE frame for Path
Maximum Transmission Unit Discovery (PMTUD); see [Section
14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref}. An endpoint can also include its own
PATH_CHALLENGE frame when sending a PATH_RESPONSE
frame.[¶](#section-8.2-6){.pilcrow}

An endpoint uses a new connection ID for probes sent from a new local
address; see [Section 9.5](#migration-linkability){.xref}. When probing
a new path, an endpoint can ensure that its peer has an unused
connection ID available for responses. Sending NEW_CONNECTION_ID and
PATH_CHALLENGE frames in the same packet, if the peer\'s
active_connection_id_limit permits, ensures that an unused connection ID
will be available to the peer when sending a
response.[¶](#section-8.2-7){.pilcrow}

An endpoint can choose to simultaneously probe multiple paths. The
number of simultaneous paths used for probes is limited by the number of
extra connection IDs its peer has previously supplied, since each new
local address used for a probe requires a previously unused connection
ID.[¶](#section-8.2-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#initiating-path-validation}
::: {#section-8.2.1 .section}
#### [8.2.1.](#section-8.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Initiating Path Validation](#name-initiating-path-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-initiating-path-validation}

To initiate path validation, an endpoint sends a PATH_CHALLENGE frame
containing an unpredictable payload on the path to be
validated.[¶](#section-8.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} send multiple PATH_CHALLENGE frames to guard
against packet loss. However, an endpoint [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} send
multiple PATH_CHALLENGE frames in a single
packet.[¶](#section-8.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} probe a new path with packets
containing a PATH_CHALLENGE frame more frequently than it would send an
Initial packet. This ensures that connection migration is no more load
on a new path than establishing a new
connection.[¶](#section-8.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

The endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} use unpredictable data in every
PATH_CHALLENGE frame so that it can associate the peer\'s response with
the corresponding PATH_CHALLENGE.[¶](#section-8.2.1-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} expand datagrams that contain a
PATH_CHALLENGE frame to at least the smallest allowed maximum datagram
size of 1200 bytes, unless the anti-amplification limit for the path
does not permit sending a datagram of this size. Sending UDP datagrams
of this size ensures that the network path from the endpoint to the peer
can be used for QUIC; see [Section
14](#datagram-size){.xref}.[¶](#section-8.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

When an endpoint is unable to expand the datagram size to 1200 bytes due
to the anti-amplification limit, the path MTU will not be validated. To
ensure that the path MTU is large enough, the endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14}
perform a second path validation by sending a PATH_CHALLENGE frame in a
datagram of at least 1200 bytes. This additional validation can be
performed after a PATH_RESPONSE is successfully received or when enough
bytes have been received on the path that sending the larger datagram
will not result in exceeding the anti-amplification
limit.[¶](#section-8.2.1-6){.pilcrow}

Unlike other cases where datagrams are expanded, endpoints [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} discard datagrams that appear to be too small when they
contain PATH_CHALLENGE or PATH_RESPONSE.[¶](#section-8.2.1-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#path-validation-responses}
::: {#section-8.2.2 .section}
#### [8.2.2.](#section-8.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Path Validation Responses](#name-path-validation-responses){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-path-validation-responses}

On receiving a PATH_CHALLENGE frame, an endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} respond
by echoing the data contained in the PATH_CHALLENGE frame in a
PATH_RESPONSE frame. An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} delay transmission
of a packet containing a PATH_RESPONSE frame unless constrained by
congestion control.[¶](#section-8.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

A PATH_RESPONSE frame [MUST]{.bcp14} be sent on the network path where
the PATH_CHALLENGE frame was received. This ensures that path validation
by a peer only succeeds if the path is functional in both directions.
This requirement [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be enforced by the endpoint that
initiates path validation, as that would enable an attack on migration;
see [Section
9.3.3](#off-path-forward){.xref}.[¶](#section-8.2.2-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} expand datagrams that contain a PATH_RESPONSE
frame to at least the smallest allowed maximum datagram size of 1200
bytes. This verifies that the path is able to carry datagrams of this
size in both directions. However, an endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} expand
the datagram containing the PATH_RESPONSE if the resulting data exceeds
the anti-amplification limit. This is expected to only occur if the
received PATH_CHALLENGE was not sent in an expanded
datagram.[¶](#section-8.2.2-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send more than one PATH_RESPONSE frame in
response to one PATH_CHALLENGE frame; see [Section
13.3](#retransmission-of-information){.xref}. The peer is expected to
send more PATH_CHALLENGE frames as necessary to evoke additional
PATH_RESPONSE frames.[¶](#section-8.2.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#successful-path-validation}
::: {#section-8.2.3 .section}
#### [8.2.3.](#section-8.2.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Successful Path Validation](#name-successful-path-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-successful-path-validation}

Path validation succeeds when a PATH_RESPONSE frame is received that
contains the data that was sent in a previous PATH_CHALLENGE frame. A
PATH_RESPONSE frame received on any network path validates the path on
which the PATH_CHALLENGE was sent.[¶](#section-8.2.3-1){.pilcrow}

If an endpoint sends a PATH_CHALLENGE frame in a datagram that is not
expanded to at least 1200 bytes and if the response to it validates the
peer address, the path is validated but not the path MTU. As a result,
the endpoint can now send more than three times the amount of data that
has been received. However, the endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} initiate another
path validation with an expanded datagram to verify that the path
supports the required MTU.[¶](#section-8.2.3-2){.pilcrow}

Receipt of an acknowledgment for a packet containing a PATH_CHALLENGE
frame is not adequate validation, since the acknowledgment can be
spoofed by a malicious peer.[¶](#section-8.2.3-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#failed-path-validation}
::: {#section-8.2.4 .section}
#### [8.2.4.](#section-8.2.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Failed Path Validation](#name-failed-path-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-failed-path-validation}

Path validation only fails when the endpoint attempting to validate the
path abandons its attempt to validate the
path.[¶](#section-8.2.4-1){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [SHOULD]{.bcp14} abandon path validation based on a timer.
When setting this timer, implementations are cautioned that the new path
could have a longer round-trip time than the original. A value of three
times the larger of the current PTO or the PTO for the new path (using
kInitialRtt, as defined in \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\])
is [RECOMMENDED]{.bcp14}.[¶](#section-8.2.4-2){.pilcrow}

This timeout allows for multiple PTOs to expire prior to failing path
validation, so that loss of a single PATH_CHALLENGE or PATH_RESPONSE
frame does not cause path validation
failure.[¶](#section-8.2.4-3){.pilcrow}

Note that the endpoint might receive packets containing other frames on
the new path, but a PATH_RESPONSE frame with appropriate data is
required for path validation to succeed.[¶](#section-8.2.4-4){.pilcrow}

When an endpoint abandons path validation, it determines that the path
is unusable. This does not necessarily imply a failure of the connection
\-- endpoints can continue sending packets over other paths as
appropriate. If no paths are available, an endpoint can wait for a new
path to become available or close the connection. An endpoint that has
no valid network path to its peer [MAY]{.bcp14} signal this using the
NO_VIABLE_PATH connection error, noting that this is only possible if
the network path exists but does not support the required MTU ([Section
14](#datagram-size){.xref}).[¶](#section-8.2.4-5){.pilcrow}

A path validation might be abandoned for other reasons besides failure.
Primarily, this happens if a connection migration to a new path is
initiated while a path validation on the old path is in
progress.[¶](#section-8.2.4-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#migration}
::: {#section-9 .section}
## [9.](#section-9){.section-number .selfRef} [Connection Migration](#name-connection-migration){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-connection-migration}

The use of a connection ID allows connections to survive changes to
endpoint addresses (IP address and port), such as those caused by an
endpoint migrating to a new network. This section describes the process
by which an endpoint migrates to a new
address.[¶](#section-9-1){.pilcrow}

The design of QUIC relies on endpoints retaining a stable address for
the duration of the handshake. An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} initiate
connection migration before the handshake is confirmed, as defined in
[Section
4.1.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.1.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-9-2){.pilcrow}

If the peer sent the disable_active_migration transport parameter, an
endpoint also [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send packets (including probing
packets; see [Section 9.1](#probing){.xref}) from a different local
address to the address the peer used during the handshake, unless the
endpoint has acted on a preferred_address transport parameter from the
peer. If the peer violates this requirement, the endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14}
either drop the incoming packets on that path without generating a
Stateless Reset or proceed with path validation and allow the peer to
migrate. Generating a Stateless Reset or closing the connection would
allow third parties in the network to cause connections to close by
spoofing or otherwise manipulating observed
traffic.[¶](#section-9-3){.pilcrow}

Not all changes of peer address are intentional, or active, migrations.
The peer could experience NAT rebinding: a change of address due to a
middlebox, usually a NAT, allocating a new outgoing port or even a new
outgoing IP address for a flow. An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} perform path
validation ([Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}) if it detects any
change to a peer\'s address, unless it has previously validated that
address.[¶](#section-9-4){.pilcrow}

When an endpoint has no validated path on which to send packets, it
[MAY]{.bcp14} discard connection state. An endpoint capable of
connection migration [MAY]{.bcp14} wait for a new path to become
available before discarding connection state.[¶](#section-9-5){.pilcrow}

This document limits migration of connections to new client addresses,
except as described in [Section 9.6](#preferred-address){.xref}. Clients
are responsible for initiating all migrations. Servers do not send
non-probing packets (see [Section 9.1](#probing){.xref}) toward a client
address until they see a non-probing packet from that address. If a
client receives packets from an unknown server address, the client
[MUST]{.bcp14} discard these packets.[¶](#section-9-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#probing}
::: {#section-9.1 .section}
### [9.1.](#section-9.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Probing a New Path](#name-probing-a-new-path){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-probing-a-new-path}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} probe for peer reachability from a new local
address using path validation ([Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref})
prior to migrating the connection to the new local address. Failure of
path validation simply means that the new path is not usable for this
connection. Failure to validate a path does not cause the connection to
end unless there are no valid alternative paths
available.[¶](#section-9.1-1){.pilcrow}

PATH_CHALLENGE, PATH_RESPONSE, NEW_CONNECTION_ID, and PADDING frames are
\"probing frames\", and all other frames are \"non-probing frames\". A
packet containing only probing frames is a \"probing packet\", and a
packet containing any other frame is a \"non-probing
packet\".[¶](#section-9.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#initiating-migration}
::: {#section-9.2 .section}
### [9.2.](#section-9.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Initiating Connection Migration](#name-initiating-connection-migra){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-initiating-connection-migra}

An endpoint can migrate a connection to a new local address by sending
packets containing non-probing frames from that
address.[¶](#section-9.2-1){.pilcrow}

Each endpoint validates its peer\'s address during connection
establishment. Therefore, a migrating endpoint can send to its peer
knowing that the peer is willing to receive at the peer\'s current
address. Thus, an endpoint can migrate to a new local address without
first validating the peer\'s address.[¶](#section-9.2-2){.pilcrow}

To establish reachability on the new path, an endpoint initiates path
validation ([Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}) on the new path. An
endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} defer path validation until after a peer sends
the next non-probing frame to its new
address.[¶](#section-9.2-3){.pilcrow}

When migrating, the new path might not support the endpoint\'s current
sending rate. Therefore, the endpoint resets its congestion controller
and RTT estimate, as described in [Section
9.4](#migration-cc){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.2-4){.pilcrow}

The new path might not have the same ECN capability. Therefore, the
endpoint validates ECN capability as described in [Section
13.4](#ecn){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#migration-response}
::: {#section-9.3 .section}
### [9.3.](#section-9.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Responding to Connection Migration](#name-responding-to-connection-mi){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-responding-to-connection-mi}

Receiving a packet from a new peer address containing a non-probing
frame indicates that the peer has migrated to that
address.[¶](#section-9.3-1){.pilcrow}

If the recipient permits the migration, it [MUST]{.bcp14} send
subsequent packets to the new peer address and [MUST]{.bcp14} initiate
path validation ([Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}) to verify the
peer\'s ownership of the address if validation is not already underway.
If the recipient has no unused connection IDs from the peer, it will not
be able to send anything on the new path until the peer provides one;
see [Section
9.5](#migration-linkability){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.3-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint only changes the address to which it sends packets in
response to the highest-numbered non-probing packet. This ensures that
an endpoint does not send packets to an old peer address in the case
that it receives reordered packets.[¶](#section-9.3-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} send data to an unvalidated peer address, but
it [MUST]{.bcp14} protect against potential attacks as described in
Sections [9.3.1](#address-spoofing){.xref} and
[9.3.2](#on-path-spoofing){.xref}. An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} skip
validation of a peer address if that address has been seen recently. In
particular, if an endpoint returns to a previously validated path after
detecting some form of spurious migration, skipping address validation
and restoring loss detection and congestion state can reduce the
performance impact of the attack.[¶](#section-9.3-4){.pilcrow}

After changing the address to which it sends non-probing packets, an
endpoint can abandon any path validation for other
addresses.[¶](#section-9.3-5){.pilcrow}

Receiving a packet from a new peer address could be the result of a NAT
rebinding at the peer.[¶](#section-9.3-6){.pilcrow}

After verifying a new client address, the server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send
new address validation tokens ([Section 8](#address-validation){.xref})
to the client.[¶](#section-9.3-7){.pilcrow}

::: {#address-spoofing}
::: {#section-9.3.1 .section}
#### [9.3.1.](#section-9.3.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Peer Address Spoofing](#name-peer-address-spoofing){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-peer-address-spoofing}

It is possible that a peer is spoofing its source address to cause an
endpoint to send excessive amounts of data to an unwilling host. If the
endpoint sends significantly more data than the spoofing peer,
connection migration might be used to amplify the volume of data that an
attacker can generate toward a victim.[¶](#section-9.3.1-1){.pilcrow}

As described in [Section 9.3](#migration-response){.xref}, an endpoint
is required to validate a peer\'s new address to confirm the peer\'s
possession of the new address. Until a peer\'s address is deemed valid,
an endpoint limits the amount of data it sends to that address; see
[Section 8](#address-validation){.xref}. In the absence of this limit,
an endpoint risks being used for a denial-of-service attack against an
unsuspecting victim.[¶](#section-9.3.1-2){.pilcrow}

If an endpoint skips validation of a peer address as described above, it
does not need to limit its sending rate.[¶](#section-9.3.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#on-path-spoofing}
::: {#section-9.3.2 .section}
#### [9.3.2.](#section-9.3.2){.section-number .selfRef} [On-Path Address Spoofing](#name-on-path-address-spoofing){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-on-path-address-spoofing}

An on-path attacker could cause a spurious connection migration by
copying and forwarding a packet with a spoofed address such that it
arrives before the original packet. The packet with the spoofed address
will be seen to come from a migrating connection, and the original
packet will be seen as a duplicate and dropped. After a spurious
migration, validation of the source address will fail because the entity
at the source address does not have the necessary cryptographic keys to
read or respond to the PATH_CHALLENGE frame that is sent to it even if
it wanted to.[¶](#section-9.3.2-1){.pilcrow}

To protect the connection from failing due to such a spurious migration,
an endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} revert to using the last validated peer
address when validation of a new peer address fails. Additionally,
receipt of packets with higher packet numbers from the legitimate peer
address will trigger another connection migration. This will cause the
validation of the address of the spurious migration to be abandoned,
thus containing migrations initiated by the attacker injecting a single
packet.[¶](#section-9.3.2-2){.pilcrow}

If an endpoint has no state about the last validated peer address, it
[MUST]{.bcp14} close the connection silently by discarding all
connection state. This results in new packets on the connection being
handled generically. For instance, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} send a
Stateless Reset in response to any further incoming
packets.[¶](#section-9.3.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#off-path-forward}
::: {#section-9.3.3 .section}
#### [9.3.3.](#section-9.3.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Off-Path Packet Forwarding](#name-off-path-packet-forwarding){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-off-path-packet-forwarding}

An off-path attacker that can observe packets might forward copies of
genuine packets to endpoints. If the copied packet arrives before the
genuine packet, this will appear as a NAT rebinding. Any genuine packet
will be discarded as a duplicate. If the attacker is able to continue
forwarding packets, it might be able to cause migration to a path via
the attacker. This places the attacker on-path, giving it the ability to
observe or drop all subsequent packets.[¶](#section-9.3.3-1){.pilcrow}

This style of attack relies on the attacker using a path that has
approximately the same characteristics as the direct path between
endpoints. The attack is more reliable if relatively few packets are
sent or if packet loss coincides with the attempted
attack.[¶](#section-9.3.3-2){.pilcrow}

A non-probing packet received on the original path that increases the
maximum received packet number will cause the endpoint to move back to
that path. Eliciting packets on this path increases the likelihood that
the attack is unsuccessful. Therefore, mitigation of this attack relies
on triggering the exchange of packets.[¶](#section-9.3.3-3){.pilcrow}

In response to an apparent migration, endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} validate
the previously active path using a PATH_CHALLENGE frame. This induces
the sending of new packets on that path. If the path is no longer
viable, the validation attempt will time out and fail; if the path is
viable but no longer desired, the validation will succeed but only
results in probing packets being sent on the
path.[¶](#section-9.3.3-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that receives a PATH_CHALLENGE on an active path
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a non-probing packet in response. If the
non-probing packet arrives before any copy made by an attacker, this
results in the connection being migrated back to the original path. Any
subsequent migration to another path restarts this entire
process.[¶](#section-9.3.3-5){.pilcrow}

This defense is imperfect, but this is not considered a serious problem.
If the path via the attack is reliably faster than the original path
despite multiple attempts to use that original path, it is not possible
to distinguish between an attack and an improvement in
routing.[¶](#section-9.3.3-6){.pilcrow}

An endpoint could also use heuristics to improve detection of this style
of attack. For instance, NAT rebinding is improbable if packets were
recently received on the old path; similarly, rebinding is rare on IPv6
paths. Endpoints can also look for duplicated packets. Conversely, a
change in connection ID is more likely to indicate an intentional
migration rather than an attack.[¶](#section-9.3.3-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#migration-cc}
::: {#section-9.4 .section}
### [9.4.](#section-9.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Loss Detection and Congestion Control](#name-loss-detection-and-congesti){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-loss-detection-and-congesti}

The capacity available on the new path might not be the same as the old
path. Packets sent on the old path [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} contribute to
congestion control or RTT estimation for the new
path.[¶](#section-9.4-1){.pilcrow}

On confirming a peer\'s ownership of its new address, an endpoint
[MUST]{.bcp14} immediately reset the congestion controller and
round-trip time estimator for the new path to initial values (see
Appendices
[A.3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#appendix-A.3){.relref} and
[B.3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#appendix-B.3){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]) unless the only change in
the peer\'s address is its port number. Because port-only changes are
commonly the result of NAT rebinding or other middlebox activity, the
endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} instead retain its congestion control state and
round-trip estimate in those cases instead of reverting to initial
values. In cases where congestion control state retained from an old
path is used on a new path with substantially different characteristics,
a sender could transmit too aggressively until the congestion controller
and the RTT estimator have adapted. Generally, implementations are
advised to be cautious when using previous values on a new
path.[¶](#section-9.4-2){.pilcrow}

There could be apparent reordering at the receiver when an endpoint
sends data and probes from/to multiple addresses during the migration
period, since the two resulting paths could have different round-trip
times. A receiver of packets on multiple paths will still send ACK
frames covering all received packets.[¶](#section-9.4-3){.pilcrow}

While multiple paths might be used during connection migration, a single
congestion control context and a single loss recovery context (as
described in \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]) could be
adequate. For instance, an endpoint might delay switching to a new
