congestion control context until it is confirmed that an old path is no
longer needed (such as the case described in [Section
9.3.3](#off-path-forward){.xref}).[¶](#section-9.4-4){.pilcrow}

A sender can make exceptions for probe packets so that their loss
detection is independent and does not unduly cause the congestion
controller to reduce its sending rate. An endpoint might set a separate
timer when a PATH_CHALLENGE is sent, which is canceled if the
corresponding PATH_RESPONSE is received. If the timer fires before the
PATH_RESPONSE is received, the endpoint might send a new PATH_CHALLENGE
and restart the timer for a longer period of time. This timer
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be set as described in [Section
6.2.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2.1){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\] and [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be
more aggressive.[¶](#section-9.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#migration-linkability}
::: {#section-9.5 .section}
### [9.5.](#section-9.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Privacy Implications of Connection Migration](#name-privacy-implications-of-con){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-privacy-implications-of-con}

Using a stable connection ID on multiple network paths would allow a
passive observer to correlate activity between those paths. An endpoint
that moves between networks might not wish to have their activity
correlated by any entity other than their peer, so different connection
IDs are used when sending from different local addresses, as discussed
in [Section 5.1](#connection-id){.xref}. For this to be effective,
endpoints need to ensure that connection IDs they provide cannot be
linked by any other entity.[¶](#section-9.5-1){.pilcrow}

At any time, endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} change the Destination Connection
ID they transmit with to a value that has not been used on another
path.[¶](#section-9.5-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} reuse a connection ID when sending from
more than one local address \-- for example, when initiating connection
migration as described in [Section 9.2](#initiating-migration){.xref} or
when probing a new network path as described in [Section
9.1](#probing){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.5-3){.pilcrow}

Similarly, an endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} reuse a connection ID when
sending to more than one destination address. Due to network changes
outside the control of its peer, an endpoint might receive packets from
a new source address with the same Destination Connection ID field
value, in which case it [MAY]{.bcp14} continue to use the current
connection ID with the new remote address while still sending from the
same local address.[¶](#section-9.5-4){.pilcrow}

These requirements regarding connection ID reuse apply only to the
sending of packets, as unintentional changes in path without a change in
connection ID are possible. For example, after a period of network
inactivity, NAT rebinding might cause packets to be sent on a new path
when the client resumes sending. An endpoint responds to such an event
as described in [Section
9.3](#migration-response){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.5-5){.pilcrow}

Using different connection IDs for packets sent in both directions on
each new network path eliminates the use of the connection ID for
linking packets from the same connection across different network paths.
Header protection ensures that packet numbers cannot be used to
correlate activity. This does not prevent other properties of packets,
such as timing and size, from being used to correlate
activity.[¶](#section-9.5-6){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} initiate migration with a peer that has
requested a zero-length connection ID, because traffic over the new path
might be trivially linkable to traffic over the old one. If the server
is able to associate packets with a zero-length connection ID to the
right connection, it means that the server is using other information to
demultiplex packets. For example, a server might provide a unique
address to every client \-- for instance, using HTTP alternative
services \[[ALTSVC](#ALTSVC){.xref}\]. Information that might allow
correct routing of packets across multiple network paths will also allow
activity on those paths to be linked by entities other than the
peer.[¶](#section-9.5-7){.pilcrow}

A client might wish to reduce linkability by switching to a new
connection ID, source UDP port, or IP address (see
\[[RFC8981](#RFC8981){.xref}\]) when sending traffic after a period of
inactivity. Changing the address from which it sends packets at the same
time might cause the server to detect a connection migration. This
ensures that the mechanisms that support migration are exercised even
for clients that do not experience NAT rebindings or genuine migrations.
Changing address can cause a peer to reset its congestion control state
(see [Section 9.4](#migration-cc){.xref}), so addresses [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
only be changed infrequently.[¶](#section-9.5-8){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that exhausts available connection IDs cannot probe new
paths or initiate migration, nor can it respond to probes or attempts by
its peer to migrate. To ensure that migration is possible and packets
sent on different paths cannot be correlated, endpoints [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
provide new connection IDs before peers migrate; see [Section
5.1.1](#issue-cid){.xref}. If a peer might have exhausted available
connection IDs, a migrating endpoint could include a NEW_CONNECTION_ID
frame in all packets sent on a new network
path.[¶](#section-9.5-9){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#preferred-address}
::: {#section-9.6 .section}
### [9.6.](#section-9.6){.section-number .selfRef} [Server\'s Preferred Address](#name-servers-preferred-address){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-servers-preferred-address}

QUIC allows servers to accept connections on one IP address and attempt
to transfer these connections to a more preferred address shortly after
the handshake. This is particularly useful when clients initially
connect to an address shared by multiple servers but would prefer to use
a unicast address to ensure connection stability. This section describes
the protocol for migrating a connection to a preferred server
address.[¶](#section-9.6-1){.pilcrow}

Migrating a connection to a new server address mid-connection is not
supported by the version of QUIC specified in this document. If a client
receives packets from a new server address when the client has not
initiated a migration to that address, the client [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
discard these packets.[¶](#section-9.6-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#communicating-a-preferred-address}
::: {#section-9.6.1 .section}
#### [9.6.1.](#section-9.6.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Communicating a Preferred Address](#name-communicating-a-preferred-a){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-communicating-a-preferred-a}

A server conveys a preferred address by including the preferred_address
transport parameter in the TLS handshake.[¶](#section-9.6.1-1){.pilcrow}

Servers [MAY]{.bcp14} communicate a preferred address of each address
family (IPv4 and IPv6) to allow clients to pick the one most suited to
their network attachment.[¶](#section-9.6.1-2){.pilcrow}

Once the handshake is confirmed, the client [SHOULD]{.bcp14} select one
of the two addresses provided by the server and initiate path validation
(see [Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}). A client constructs
packets using any previously unused active connection ID, taken from
either the preferred_address transport parameter or a NEW_CONNECTION_ID
frame.[¶](#section-9.6.1-3){.pilcrow}

As soon as path validation succeeds, the client [SHOULD]{.bcp14} begin
sending all future packets to the new server address using the new
connection ID and discontinue use of the old server address. If path
validation fails, the client [MUST]{.bcp14} continue sending all future
packets to the server\'s original IP
address.[¶](#section-9.6.1-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#migration-to-a-preferred-address}
::: {#section-9.6.2 .section}
#### [9.6.2.](#section-9.6.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Migration to a Preferred Address](#name-migration-to-a-preferred-ad){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-migration-to-a-preferred-ad}

A client that migrates to a preferred address [MUST]{.bcp14} validate
the address it chooses before migrating; see [Section
21.5.3](#forgery-spa){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.6.2-1){.pilcrow}

A server might receive a packet addressed to its preferred IP address at
any time after it accepts a connection. If this packet contains a
PATH_CHALLENGE frame, the server sends a packet containing a
PATH_RESPONSE frame as per [Section 8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}. The
server [MUST]{.bcp14} send non-probing packets from its original address
until it receives a non-probing packet from the client at its preferred
address and until the server has validated the new
path.[¶](#section-9.6.2-2){.pilcrow}

The server [MUST]{.bcp14} probe on the path toward the client from its
preferred address. This helps to guard against spurious migration
initiated by an attacker.[¶](#section-9.6.2-3){.pilcrow}

Once the server has completed its path validation and has received a
non-probing packet with a new largest packet number on its preferred
address, the server begins sending non-probing packets to the client
exclusively from its preferred IP address. The server [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
drop newer packets for this connection that are received on the old IP
address. The server [MAY]{.bcp14} continue to process delayed packets
that are received on the old IP address.[¶](#section-9.6.2-4){.pilcrow}

The addresses that a server provides in the preferred_address transport
parameter are only valid for the connection in which they are provided.
A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} use these for other connections, including
connections that are resumed from the current
connection.[¶](#section-9.6.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#interaction-of-client-migration-and-preferred-address}
::: {#section-9.6.3 .section}
#### [9.6.3.](#section-9.6.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Interaction of Client Migration and Preferred Address](#name-interaction-of-client-migra){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-interaction-of-client-migra}

A client might need to perform a connection migration before it has
migrated to the server\'s preferred address. In this case, the client
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} perform path validation to both the original and
preferred server address from the client\'s new address
concurrently.[¶](#section-9.6.3-1){.pilcrow}

If path validation of the server\'s preferred address succeeds, the
client [MUST]{.bcp14} abandon validation of the original address and
migrate to using the server\'s preferred address. If path validation of
the server\'s preferred address fails but validation of the server\'s
original address succeeds, the client [MAY]{.bcp14} migrate to its new
address and continue sending to the server\'s original
address.[¶](#section-9.6.3-2){.pilcrow}

If packets received at the server\'s preferred address have a different
source address than observed from the client during the handshake, the
server [MUST]{.bcp14} protect against potential attacks as described in
Sections [9.3.1](#address-spoofing){.xref} and
[9.3.2](#on-path-spoofing){.xref}. In addition to intentional
simultaneous migration, this might also occur because the client\'s
access network used a different NAT binding for the server\'s preferred
address.[¶](#section-9.6.3-3){.pilcrow}

Servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} initiate path validation to the client\'s new
address upon receiving a probe packet from a different address; see
[Section 8](#address-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.6.3-4){.pilcrow}

A client that migrates to a new address [SHOULD]{.bcp14} use a preferred
address from the same address family for the
server.[¶](#section-9.6.3-5){.pilcrow}

The connection ID provided in the preferred_address transport parameter
is not specific to the addresses that are provided. This connection ID
is provided to ensure that the client has a connection ID available for
migration, but the client [MAY]{.bcp14} use this connection ID on any
path.[¶](#section-9.6.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#ipv6-flow-label}
::: {#section-9.7 .section}
### [9.7.](#section-9.7){.section-number .selfRef} [Use of IPv6 Flow Label and Migration](#name-use-of-ipv6-flow-label-and-){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-use-of-ipv6-flow-label-and-}

Endpoints that send data using IPv6 [SHOULD]{.bcp14} apply an IPv6 flow
label in compliance with \[[RFC6437](#RFC6437){.xref}\], unless the
local API does not allow setting IPv6 flow
labels.[¶](#section-9.7-1){.pilcrow}

The flow label generation [MUST]{.bcp14} be designed to minimize the
chances of linkability with a previously used flow label, as a stable
flow label would enable correlating activity on multiple paths; see
[Section
9.5](#migration-linkability){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.7-2){.pilcrow}

\[[RFC6437](#RFC6437){.xref}\] suggests deriving values using a
pseudorandom function to generate flow labels. Including the Destination
Connection ID field in addition to source and destination addresses when
generating flow labels ensures that changes are synchronized with
changes in other observable identifiers. A cryptographic hash function
that combines these inputs with a local secret is one way this might be
implemented.[¶](#section-9.7-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#termination}
::: {#section-10 .section}
## [10.](#section-10){.section-number .selfRef} [Connection Termination](#name-connection-termination){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-connection-termination}

An established QUIC connection can be terminated in one of three
ways:[¶](#section-10-1){.pilcrow}

-   [idle timeout ([Section
    10.1](#idle-timeout){.xref})[¶](#section-10-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10-2.1}
-   [immediate close ([Section
    10.2](#immediate-close){.xref})[¶](#section-10-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10-2.2}
-   [stateless reset ([Section
    10.3](#stateless-reset){.xref})[¶](#section-10-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-10-2.3}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} discard connection state if it does not have a
validated path on which it can send packets; see [Section
8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}.[¶](#section-10-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#idle-timeout}
::: {#section-10.1 .section}
### [10.1.](#section-10.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Idle Timeout](#name-idle-timeout){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-idle-timeout}

If a max_idle_timeout is specified by either endpoint in its transport
parameters ([Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}),
the connection is silently closed and its state is discarded when it
remains idle for longer than the minimum of the max_idle_timeout value
advertised by both endpoints.[¶](#section-10.1-1){.pilcrow}

Each endpoint advertises a max_idle_timeout, but the effective value at
an endpoint is computed as the minimum of the two advertised values (or
the sole advertised value, if only one endpoint advertises a non-zero
value). By announcing a max_idle_timeout, an endpoint commits to
initiating an immediate close ([Section 10.2](#immediate-close){.xref})
if it abandons the connection prior to the effective
value.[¶](#section-10.1-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint restarts its idle timer when a packet from its peer is
received and processed successfully. An endpoint also restarts its idle
timer when sending an ack-eliciting packet if no other ack-eliciting
packets have been sent since last receiving and processing a packet.
Restarting this timer when sending a packet ensures that connections are
not closed after new activity is
initiated.[¶](#section-10.1-3){.pilcrow}

To avoid excessively small idle timeout periods, endpoints
[MUST]{.bcp14} increase the idle timeout period to be at least three
times the current Probe Timeout (PTO). This allows for multiple PTOs to
expire, and therefore multiple probes to be sent and lost, prior to idle
timeout.[¶](#section-10.1-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#liveness-testing}
::: {#section-10.1.1 .section}
#### [10.1.1.](#section-10.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Liveness Testing](#name-liveness-testing){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-liveness-testing}

An endpoint that sends packets close to the effective timeout risks
having them be discarded at the peer, since the idle timeout period
might have expired at the peer before these packets
arrive.[¶](#section-10.1.1-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint can send a PING or another ack-eliciting frame to test the
connection for liveness if the peer could time out soon, such as within
a PTO; see [Section
6.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]. This is especially useful if
any available application data cannot be safely retried. Note that the
application determines what data is safe to
retry.[¶](#section-10.1.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#defer-idle}
::: {#section-10.1.2 .section}
#### [10.1.2.](#section-10.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Deferring Idle Timeout](#name-deferring-idle-timeout){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-deferring-idle-timeout}

An endpoint might need to send ack-eliciting packets to avoid an idle
timeout if it is expecting response data but does not have or is unable
to send application data.[¶](#section-10.1.2-1){.pilcrow}

An implementation of QUIC might provide applications with an option to
defer an idle timeout. This facility could be used when the application
wishes to avoid losing state that has been associated with an open
connection but does not expect to exchange application data for some
time. With this option, an endpoint could send a PING frame ([Section
19.2](#frame-ping){.xref}) periodically, which will cause the peer to
restart its idle timeout period. Sending a packet containing a PING
frame restarts the idle timeout for this endpoint also if this is the
first ack-eliciting packet sent since receiving a packet. Sending a PING
frame causes the peer to respond with an acknowledgment, which also
restarts the idle timeout for the
endpoint.[¶](#section-10.1.2-2){.pilcrow}

Application protocols that use QUIC [SHOULD]{.bcp14} provide guidance on
when deferring an idle timeout is appropriate. Unnecessary sending of
PING frames could have a detrimental effect on
performance.[¶](#section-10.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

A connection will time out if no packets are sent or received for a
period longer than the time negotiated using the max_idle_timeout
transport parameter; see [Section 10](#termination){.xref}. However,
state in middleboxes might time out earlier than that. Though REQ-5 in
\[[RFC4787](#RFC4787){.xref}\] recommends a 2-minute timeout interval,
experience shows that sending packets every 30 seconds is necessary to
prevent the majority of middleboxes from losing state for UDP flows
\[[GATEWAY](#GATEWAY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-10.1.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#immediate-close}
::: {#section-10.2 .section}
### [10.2.](#section-10.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Immediate Close](#name-immediate-close){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-immediate-close}

An endpoint sends a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame ([Section
19.19](#frame-connection-close){.xref}) to terminate the connection
immediately. A CONNECTION_CLOSE frame causes all streams to immediately
become closed; open streams can be assumed to be implicitly
reset.[¶](#section-10.2-1){.pilcrow}

After sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame, an endpoint immediately enters
the closing state; see [Section 10.2.1](#closing){.xref}. After
receiving a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame, endpoints enter the draining state;
see [Section 10.2.2](#draining){.xref}.[¶](#section-10.2-2){.pilcrow}

Violations of the protocol lead to an immediate
close.[¶](#section-10.2-3){.pilcrow}

An immediate close can be used after an application protocol has
arranged to close a connection. This might be after the application
protocol negotiates a graceful shutdown. The application protocol can
exchange messages that are needed for both application endpoints to
agree that the connection can be closed, after which the application
requests that QUIC close the connection. When QUIC consequently closes
the connection, a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame with an application-supplied
error code will be used to signal closure to the
peer.[¶](#section-10.2-4){.pilcrow}

The closing and draining connection states exist to ensure that
connections close cleanly and that delayed or reordered packets are
properly discarded. These states [SHOULD]{.bcp14} persist for at least
three times the current PTO interval as defined in
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-10.2-5){.pilcrow}

Disposing of connection state prior to exiting the closing or draining
state could result in an endpoint generating a Stateless Reset
unnecessarily when it receives a late-arriving packet. Endpoints that
have some alternative means to ensure that late-arriving packets do not
induce a response, such as those that are able to close the UDP socket,
[MAY]{.bcp14} end these states earlier to allow for faster resource
recovery. Servers that retain an open socket for accepting new
connections [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} end the closing or draining state
early.[¶](#section-10.2-6){.pilcrow}

Once its closing or draining state ends, an endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
discard all connection state. The endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} send a
Stateless Reset in response to any further incoming packets belonging to
this connection.[¶](#section-10.2-7){.pilcrow}

::: {#closing}
::: {#section-10.2.1 .section}
#### [10.2.1.](#section-10.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Closing Connection State](#name-closing-connection-state){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-closing-connection-state}

An endpoint enters the closing state after initiating an immediate
close.[¶](#section-10.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

In the closing state, an endpoint retains only enough information to
generate a packet containing a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame and to identify
packets as belonging to the connection. An endpoint in the closing state
sends a packet containing a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in response to any
incoming packet that it attributes to the
connection.[¶](#section-10.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} limit the rate at which it generates
packets in the closing state. For instance, an endpoint could wait for a
progressively increasing number of received packets or amount of time
before responding to received packets.[¶](#section-10.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint\'s selected connection ID and the QUIC version are
sufficient information to identify packets for a closing connection; the
endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} discard all other connection state. An endpoint
that is closing is not required to process any received frame. An
endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} retain packet protection keys for incoming
packets to allow it to read and process a CONNECTION_CLOSE
frame.[¶](#section-10.2.1-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} drop packet protection keys when entering the
closing state and send a packet containing a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in
response to any UDP datagram that is received. However, an endpoint that
discards packet protection keys cannot identify and discard invalid
packets. To avoid being used for an amplification attack, such endpoints
[MUST]{.bcp14} limit the cumulative size of packets it sends to three
times the cumulative size of the packets that are received and
attributed to the connection. To minimize the state that an endpoint
maintains for a closing connection, endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} send the
exact same packet in response to any received
packet.[¶](#section-10.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

Note: Allowing retransmission of a closing packet is an exception to the
requirement that a new packet number be used for each packet; see
[Section 12.3](#packet-numbers){.xref}. Sending new packet numbers is
primarily of advantage to loss recovery and congestion control, which
are not expected to be relevant for a closed connection. Retransmitting
the final packet requires less state.[¶](#section-10.2.1-6.1){.pilcrow}

While in the closing state, an endpoint could receive packets from a new
source address, possibly indicating a connection migration; see [Section
9](#migration){.xref}. An endpoint in the closing state [MUST]{.bcp14}
either discard packets received from an unvalidated address or limit the
cumulative size of packets it sends to an unvalidated address to three
times the size of packets it receives from that
address.[¶](#section-10.2.1-7){.pilcrow}

An endpoint is not expected to handle key updates when it is closing
([Section 6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-6){.relref}
of \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]). A key update might prevent the
endpoint from moving from the closing state to the draining state, as
the endpoint will not be able to process subsequently received packets,
but it otherwise has no impact.[¶](#section-10.2.1-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#draining}
::: {#section-10.2.2 .section}
#### [10.2.2.](#section-10.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Draining Connection State](#name-draining-connection-state){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-draining-connection-state}

The draining state is entered once an endpoint receives a
CONNECTION_CLOSE frame, which indicates that its peer is closing or
draining. While otherwise identical to the closing state, an endpoint in
the draining state [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send any packets. Retaining packet
protection keys is unnecessary once a connection is in the draining
state.[¶](#section-10.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that receives a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame [MAY]{.bcp14} send a
single packet containing a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame before entering the
draining state, using a NO_ERROR code if appropriate. An endpoint [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} send further packets. Doing so could result in a constant
exchange of CONNECTION_CLOSE frames until one of the endpoints exits the
closing state.[¶](#section-10.2.2-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} enter the draining state from the closing
state if it receives a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame, which indicates that the
peer is also closing or draining. In this case, the draining state ends
when the closing state would have ended. In other words, the endpoint
uses the same end time but ceases transmission of any packets on this
connection.[¶](#section-10.2.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#immediate-close-hs}
::: {#section-10.2.3 .section}
#### [10.2.3.](#section-10.2.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Immediate Close during the Handshake](#name-immediate-close-during-the-){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-immediate-close-during-the-}

When sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame, the goal is to ensure that the
peer will process the frame. Generally, this means sending the frame in
a packet with the highest level of packet protection to avoid the packet
being discarded. After the handshake is confirmed (see [Section
4.1.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.1.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]), an endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} send any
CONNECTION_CLOSE frames in a 1-RTT packet. However, prior to confirming
the handshake, it is possible that more advanced packet protection keys
are not available to the peer, so another CONNECTION_CLOSE frame
[MAY]{.bcp14} be sent in a packet that uses a lower packet protection
level. More specifically:[¶](#section-10.2.3-1){.pilcrow}

-   [A client will always know whether the server has Handshake keys
    (see [Section 17.2.2.1](#discard-initial){.xref}), but it is
    possible that a server does not know whether the client has
    Handshake keys. Under these circumstances, a server [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
    send a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in both Handshake and Initial packets
    to ensure that at least one of them is processable by the
    client.[¶](#section-10.2.3-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.2.3-2.1}
-   [A client that sends a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in a 0-RTT packet
    cannot be assured that the server has accepted 0-RTT. Sending a
    CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in an Initial packet makes it more likely
    that the server can receive the close signal, even if the
    application error code might not be
    received.[¶](#section-10.2.3-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.2.3-2.2}
-   [Prior to confirming the handshake, a peer might be unable to
    process 1-RTT packets, so an endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a
    CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in both Handshake and 1-RTT packets. A server
    [SHOULD]{.bcp14} also send a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in an Initial
    packet.[¶](#section-10.2.3-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.2.3-2.3}

Sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE of type 0x1d in an Initial or Handshake
packet could expose application state or be used to alter application
state. A CONNECTION_CLOSE of type 0x1d [MUST]{.bcp14} be replaced by a
CONNECTION_CLOSE of type 0x1c when sending the frame in Initial or
Handshake packets. Otherwise, information about the application state
might be revealed. Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} clear the value of the
Reason Phrase field and [SHOULD]{.bcp14} use the APPLICATION_ERROR code
when converting to a CONNECTION_CLOSE of type
0x1c.[¶](#section-10.2.3-3){.pilcrow}

CONNECTION_CLOSE frames sent in multiple packet types can be coalesced
into a single UDP datagram; see [Section
12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}.[¶](#section-10.2.3-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint can send a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame in an Initial packet. This
might be in response to unauthenticated information received in Initial
or Handshake packets. Such an immediate close might expose legitimate
connections to a denial of service. QUIC does not include defensive
measures for on-path attacks during the handshake; see [Section
21.2](#handshake-dos){.xref}. However, at the cost of reducing feedback
about errors for legitimate peers, some forms of denial of service can
be made more difficult for an attacker if endpoints discard illegal
packets rather than terminating a connection with CONNECTION_CLOSE. For
this reason, endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} discard packets rather than
immediately close if errors are detected in packets that lack
authentication.[¶](#section-10.2.3-5){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that has not established state, such as a server that
detects an error in an Initial packet, does not enter the closing state.
An endpoint that has no state for the connection does not enter a
closing or draining period on sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE
frame.[¶](#section-10.2.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#stateless-reset}
::: {#section-10.3 .section}
### [10.3.](#section-10.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Stateless Reset](#name-stateless-reset){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-stateless-reset}

A stateless reset is provided as an option of last resort for an
endpoint that does not have access to the state of a connection. A crash
or outage might result in peers continuing to send data to an endpoint
that is unable to properly continue the connection. An endpoint
[MAY]{.bcp14} send a Stateless Reset in response to receiving a packet
that it cannot associate with an active
connection.[¶](#section-10.3-1){.pilcrow}

A stateless reset is not appropriate for indicating errors in active
connections. An endpoint that wishes to communicate a fatal connection
error [MUST]{.bcp14} use a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame if it is
able.[¶](#section-10.3-2){.pilcrow}

To support this process, an endpoint issues a stateless reset token,
which is a 16-byte value that is hard to guess. If the peer subsequently
receives a Stateless Reset, which is a UDP datagram that ends in that
stateless reset token, the peer will immediately end the
connection.[¶](#section-10.3-3){.pilcrow}

A stateless reset token is specific to a connection ID. An endpoint
issues a stateless reset token by including the value in the Stateless
Reset Token field of a NEW_CONNECTION_ID frame. Servers can also issue a
stateless_reset_token transport parameter during the handshake that
applies to the connection ID that it selected during the handshake.
These exchanges are protected by encryption, so only client and server
know their value. Note that clients cannot use the stateless_reset_token
transport parameter because their transport parameters do not have
confidentiality protection.[¶](#section-10.3-4){.pilcrow}

Tokens are invalidated when their associated connection ID is retired
via a RETIRE_CONNECTION_ID frame ([Section
19.16](#frame-retire-connection-id){.xref}).[¶](#section-10.3-5){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that receives packets that it cannot process sends a packet
in the following layout (see [Section
1.3](#notation){.xref}):[¶](#section-10.3-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-stateless-reset-2}

::: {#fig-stateless-reset}
::: {#section-10.3-7.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Stateless Reset {
      Fixed Bits (2) = 1,
      Unpredictable Bits (38..),
      Stateless Reset Token (128),
    }
:::

[Figure 10](#figure-10){.selfRef}: [Stateless
Reset](#name-stateless-reset-2){.selfRef}
:::

This design ensures that a Stateless Reset is \-- to the extent possible
\-- indistinguishable from a regular packet with a short
header.[¶](#section-10.3-8){.pilcrow}

A Stateless Reset uses an entire UDP datagram, starting with the first
two bits of the packet header. The remainder of the first byte and an
arbitrary number of bytes following it are set to values that
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be indistinguishable from random. The last 16 bytes of
the datagram contain a stateless reset
token.[¶](#section-10.3-9){.pilcrow}

To entities other than its intended recipient, a Stateless Reset will
appear to be a packet with a short header. For the Stateless Reset to
appear as a valid QUIC packet, the Unpredictable Bits field needs to
include at least 38 bits of data (or 5 bytes, less the two fixed
bits).[¶](#section-10.3-10){.pilcrow}

The resulting minimum size of 21 bytes does not guarantee that a
Stateless Reset is difficult to distinguish from other packets if the
recipient requires the use of a connection ID. To achieve that end, the
endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ensure that all packets it sends are at least
22 bytes longer than the minimum connection ID length that it requests
the peer to include in its packets, adding PADDING frames as necessary.
This ensures that any Stateless Reset sent by the peer is
indistinguishable from a valid packet sent to the endpoint. An endpoint
that sends a Stateless Reset in response to a packet that is 43 bytes or
shorter [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a Stateless Reset that is one byte shorter
than the packet it responds to.[¶](#section-10.3-11){.pilcrow}

These values assume that the stateless reset token is the same length as
the minimum expansion of the packet protection AEAD. Additional
unpredictable bytes are necessary if the endpoint could have negotiated
a packet protection scheme with a larger minimum
expansion.[¶](#section-10.3-12){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a Stateless Reset that is three
times or more larger than the packet it receives to avoid being used for
amplification. [Section 10.3.3](#reset-looping){.xref} describes
additional limits on Stateless Reset
size.[¶](#section-10.3-13){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} discard packets that are too small to be valid
QUIC packets. To give an example, with the set of AEAD functions defined
in \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\], short header packets that are
smaller than 21 bytes are never valid.[¶](#section-10.3-14){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} send Stateless Resets formatted as a packet
with a short header. However, endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} treat any packet
ending in a valid stateless reset token as a Stateless Reset, as other
QUIC versions might allow the use of a long
header.[¶](#section-10.3-15){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} send a Stateless Reset in response to a packet
with a long header. Sending a Stateless Reset is not effective prior to
the stateless reset token being available to a peer. In this QUIC
version, packets with a long header are only used during connection
establishment. Because the stateless reset token is not available until
connection establishment is complete or near completion, ignoring an
unknown packet with a long header might be as effective as sending a
Stateless Reset.[¶](#section-10.3-16){.pilcrow}

An endpoint cannot determine the Source Connection ID from a packet with
a short header; therefore, it cannot set the Destination Connection ID
in the Stateless Reset. The Destination Connection ID will therefore
differ from the value used in previous packets. A random Destination
Connection ID makes the connection ID appear to be the result of moving
to a new connection ID that was provided using a NEW_CONNECTION_ID
frame; see [Section
19.15](#frame-new-connection-id){.xref}.[¶](#section-10.3-17){.pilcrow}

Using a randomized connection ID results in two
problems:[¶](#section-10.3-18){.pilcrow}

-   [The packet might not reach the peer. If the Destination Connection
    ID is critical for routing toward the peer, then this packet could
    be incorrectly routed. This might also trigger another Stateless
    Reset in response; see [Section 10.3.3](#reset-looping){.xref}. A
    Stateless Reset that is not correctly routed is an ineffective error
    detection and recovery mechanism. In this case, endpoints will need
    to rely on other methods \-- such as timers \-- to detect that the
    connection has
    failed.[¶](#section-10.3-19.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.3-19.1}
-   [The randomly generated connection ID can be used by entities other
    than the peer to identify this as a potential Stateless Reset. An
    endpoint that occasionally uses different connection IDs might
    introduce some uncertainty about
    this.[¶](#section-10.3-19.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.3-19.2}

This stateless reset design is specific to QUIC version 1. An endpoint
that supports multiple versions of QUIC needs to generate a Stateless
Reset that will be accepted by peers that support any version that the
endpoint might support (or might have supported prior to losing state).
Designers of new versions of QUIC need to be aware of this and either
(1) reuse this design or (2) use a portion of the packet other than the
last 16 bytes for carrying data.[¶](#section-10.3-20){.pilcrow}

::: {#detecting-a-stateless-reset}
::: {#section-10.3.1 .section}
#### [10.3.1.](#section-10.3.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Detecting a Stateless Reset](#name-detecting-a-stateless-reset){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-detecting-a-stateless-reset}

An endpoint detects a potential Stateless Reset using the trailing 16
bytes of the UDP datagram. An endpoint remembers all stateless reset
tokens associated with the connection IDs and remote addresses for
datagrams it has recently sent. This includes Stateless Reset Token
field values from NEW_CONNECTION_ID frames and the server\'s transport
parameters but excludes stateless reset tokens associated with
connection IDs that are either unused or retired. The endpoint
identifies a received datagram as a Stateless Reset by comparing the
last 16 bytes of the datagram with all stateless reset tokens associated
with the remote address on which the datagram was
received.[¶](#section-10.3.1-1){.pilcrow}

This comparison can be performed for every inbound datagram. Endpoints
[MAY]{.bcp14} skip this check if any packet from a datagram is
successfully processed. However, the comparison [MUST]{.bcp14} be
performed when the first packet in an incoming datagram either cannot be
associated with a connection or cannot be
decrypted.[¶](#section-10.3.1-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} check for any stateless reset tokens
associated with connection IDs it has not used or for connection IDs
that have been retired.[¶](#section-10.3.1-3){.pilcrow}

When comparing a datagram to stateless reset token values, endpoints
[MUST]{.bcp14} perform the comparison without leaking information about
the value of the token. For example, performing this comparison in
constant time protects the value of individual stateless reset tokens
from information leakage through timing side channels. Another approach
would be to store and compare the transformed values of stateless reset
tokens instead of the raw token values, where the transformation is
defined as a cryptographically secure pseudorandom function using a
secret key (e.g., block cipher, Hashed Message Authentication Code
(HMAC) \[[RFC2104](#RFC2104){.xref}\]). An endpoint is not expected to
protect information about whether a packet was successfully decrypted or
the number of valid stateless reset
tokens.[¶](#section-10.3.1-4){.pilcrow}

If the last 16 bytes of the datagram are identical in value to a
stateless reset token, the endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} enter the draining
period and not send any further packets on this
connection.[¶](#section-10.3.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reset-token}
::: {#section-10.3.2 .section}
#### [10.3.2.](#section-10.3.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Calculating a Stateless Reset Token](#name-calculating-a-stateless-res){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-calculating-a-stateless-res}

The stateless reset token [MUST]{.bcp14} be difficult to guess. In order
to create a stateless reset token, an endpoint could randomly generate
\[[RANDOM](#RANDOM){.xref}\] a secret for every connection that it
creates. However, this presents a coordination problem when there are
multiple instances in a cluster or a storage problem for an endpoint
that might lose state. Stateless reset specifically exists to handle the
case where state is lost, so this approach is
suboptimal.[¶](#section-10.3.2-1){.pilcrow}

A single static key can be used across all connections to the same
endpoint by generating the proof using a pseudorandom function that
takes a static key and the connection ID chosen by the endpoint (see
[Section 5.1](#connection-id){.xref}) as input. An endpoint could use
HMAC \[[RFC2104](#RFC2104){.xref}\] (for example, HMAC(static_key,
connection_id)) or the HMAC-based Key Derivation Function (HKDF)
\[[RFC5869](#RFC5869){.xref}\] (for example, using the static key as
input keying material, with the connection ID as salt). The output of
this function is truncated to 16 bytes to produce the stateless reset
token for that connection.[¶](#section-10.3.2-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that loses state can use the same method to generate a valid
stateless reset token. The connection ID comes from the packet that the
endpoint receives.[¶](#section-10.3.2-3){.pilcrow}

This design relies on the peer always sending a connection ID in its
packets so that the endpoint can use the connection ID from a packet to
reset the connection. An endpoint that uses this design [MUST]{.bcp14}
either use the same connection ID length for all connections or encode
the length of the connection ID such that it can be recovered without
state. In addition, it cannot provide a zero-length connection
ID.[¶](#section-10.3.2-4){.pilcrow}

Revealing the stateless reset token allows any entity to terminate the
connection, so a value can only be used once. This method for choosing
the stateless reset token means that the combination of connection ID
and static key [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be used for another connection. A
denial-of-service attack is possible if the same connection ID is used
by instances that share a static key or if an attacker can cause a
packet to be routed to an instance that has no state but the same static
key; see [Section 21.11](#reset-oracle){.xref}. A connection ID from a
connection that is reset by revealing the stateless reset token [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} be reused for new connections at nodes that share a static
key.[¶](#section-10.3.2-5){.pilcrow}

The same stateless reset token [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be used for multiple
connection IDs. Endpoints are not required to compare new values against
all previous values, but a duplicate value [MAY]{.bcp14} be treated as a
connection error of type
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-10.3.2-6){.pilcrow}

Note that Stateless Resets do not have any cryptographic
protection.[¶](#section-10.3.2-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reset-looping}
::: {#section-10.3.3 .section}
#### [10.3.3.](#section-10.3.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Looping](#name-looping){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-looping}

The design of a Stateless Reset is such that without knowing the
stateless reset token it is indistinguishable from a valid packet. For
instance, if a server sends a Stateless Reset to another server, it
might receive another Stateless Reset in response, which could lead to
an infinite exchange.[¶](#section-10.3.3-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} ensure that every Stateless Reset that it
sends is smaller than the packet that triggered it, unless it maintains
state sufficient to prevent looping. In the event of a loop, this
results in packets eventually being too small to trigger a
response.[¶](#section-10.3.3-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint can remember the number of Stateless Resets that it has sent
and stop generating new Stateless Resets once a limit is reached. Using
separate limits for different remote addresses will ensure that
Stateless Resets can be used to close connections when other peers or
connections have exhausted limits.[¶](#section-10.3.3-3){.pilcrow}

A Stateless Reset that is smaller than 41 bytes might be identifiable as
a Stateless Reset by an observer, depending upon the length of the
peer\'s connection IDs. Conversely, not sending a Stateless Reset in
response to a small packet might result in Stateless Resets not being
useful in detecting cases of broken connections where only very small
packets are sent; such failures might only be detected by other means,
such as timers.[¶](#section-10.3.3-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#error-handling}
::: {#section-11 .section}
## [11.](#section-11){.section-number .selfRef} [Error Handling](#name-error-handling){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-error-handling}

An endpoint that detects an error [SHOULD]{.bcp14} signal the existence
of that error to its peer. Both transport-level and application-level
errors can affect an entire connection; see [Section
11.1](#connection-errors){.xref}. Only application-level errors can be
isolated to a single stream; see [Section
11.2](#stream-errors){.xref}.[¶](#section-11-1){.pilcrow}

The most appropriate error code ([Section 20](#error-codes){.xref})
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be included in the frame that signals the error. Where
this specification identifies error conditions, it also identifies the
error code that is used; though these are worded as requirements,
different implementation strategies might lead to different errors being
reported. In particular, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} use any applicable
error code when it detects an error condition; a generic error code
(such as PROTOCOL_VIOLATION or INTERNAL_ERROR) can always be used in
place of specific error codes.[¶](#section-11-2){.pilcrow}

A stateless reset ([Section 10.3](#stateless-reset){.xref}) is not
suitable for any error that can be signaled with a CONNECTION_CLOSE or
RESET_STREAM frame. A stateless reset [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be used by an
endpoint that has the state necessary to send a frame on the
connection.[¶](#section-11-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#connection-errors}
::: {#section-11.1 .section}
### [11.1.](#section-11.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Connection Errors](#name-connection-errors){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-connection-errors}

Errors that result in the connection being unusable, such as an obvious
violation of protocol semantics or corruption of state that affects an
entire connection, [MUST]{.bcp14} be signaled using a CONNECTION_CLOSE
frame ([Section
19.19](#frame-connection-close){.xref}).[¶](#section-11.1-1){.pilcrow}

Application-specific protocol errors are signaled using the
CONNECTION_CLOSE frame with a frame type of 0x1d. Errors that are
specific to the transport, including all those described in this
document, are carried in the CONNECTION_CLOSE frame with a frame type of
0x1c.[¶](#section-11.1-2){.pilcrow}

A CONNECTION_CLOSE frame could be sent in a packet that is lost. An
endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be prepared to retransmit a packet containing
a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame if it receives more packets on a terminated
connection. Limiting the number of retransmissions and the time over
which this final packet is sent limits the effort expended on terminated
connections.[¶](#section-11.1-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that chooses not to retransmit packets containing a
CONNECTION_CLOSE frame risks a peer missing the first such packet. The
only mechanism available to an endpoint that continues to receive data
for a terminated connection is to attempt the stateless reset process
([Section 10.3](#stateless-reset){.xref}).[¶](#section-11.1-4){.pilcrow}

As the AEAD for Initial packets does not provide strong authentication,
an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} discard an invalid Initial packet. Discarding
an Initial packet is permitted even where this specification otherwise
mandates a connection error. An endpoint can only discard a packet if it
does not process the frames in the packet or reverts the effects of any
processing. Discarding invalid Initial packets might be used to reduce
exposure to denial of service; see [Section
21.2](#handshake-dos){.xref}.[¶](#section-11.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#stream-errors}
::: {#section-11.2 .section}
### [11.2.](#section-11.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Stream Errors](#name-stream-errors){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-stream-errors}

If an application-level error affects a single stream but otherwise
leaves the connection in a recoverable state, the endpoint can send a
RESET_STREAM frame ([Section 19.4](#frame-reset-stream){.xref}) with an
appropriate error code to terminate just the affected
stream.[¶](#section-11.2-1){.pilcrow}

Resetting a stream without the involvement of the application protocol
could cause the application protocol to enter an unrecoverable state.
RESET_STREAM [MUST]{.bcp14} only be instigated by the application
protocol that uses QUIC.[¶](#section-11.2-2){.pilcrow}

The semantics of the application error code carried in RESET_STREAM are
defined by the application protocol. Only the application protocol is
able to cause a stream to be terminated. A local instance of the
application protocol uses a direct API call, and a remote instance uses
the STOP_SENDING frame, which triggers an automatic
RESET_STREAM.[¶](#section-11.2-3){.pilcrow}

Application protocols [SHOULD]{.bcp14} define rules for handling streams
that are prematurely canceled by either
endpoint.[¶](#section-11.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#packets-frames}
::: {#section-12 .section}
## [12.](#section-12){.section-number .selfRef} [Packets and Frames](#name-packets-and-frames){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packets-and-frames}

QUIC endpoints communicate by exchanging packets. Packets have
confidentiality and integrity protection; see [Section
12.1](#packet-protected){.xref}. Packets are carried in UDP datagrams;
see [Section 12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}.[¶](#section-12-1){.pilcrow}

This version of QUIC uses the long packet header during connection
establishment; see [Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}. Packets with the
long header are Initial ([Section 17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}),
0-RTT ([Section 17.2.3](#packet-0rtt){.xref}), Handshake ([Section
17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref}), and Retry ([Section
17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}). Version negotiation uses a
version-independent packet with a long header; see [Section
17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}.[¶](#section-12-2){.pilcrow}

Packets with the short header are designed for minimal overhead and are
used after a connection is established and 1-RTT keys are available; see
[Section 17.3](#short-header){.xref}.[¶](#section-12-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#packet-protected}
::: {#section-12.1 .section}
### [12.1.](#section-12.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Protected Packets](#name-protected-packets){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-protected-packets}

QUIC packets have different levels of cryptographic protection based on
the type of packet. Details of packet protection are found in
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]; this section includes an overview of
the protections that are provided.[¶](#section-12.1-1){.pilcrow}

Version Negotiation packets have no cryptographic protection; see
\[[QUIC-INVARIANTS](#QUIC-INVARIANTS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-12.1-2){.pilcrow}

Retry packets use an AEAD function \[[AEAD](#AEAD){.xref}\] to protect
against accidental modification.[¶](#section-12.1-3){.pilcrow}

Initial packets use an AEAD function, the keys for which are derived
using a value that is visible on the wire. Initial packets therefore do
not have effective confidentiality protection. Initial protection exists
to ensure that the sender of the packet is on the network path. Any
entity that receives an Initial packet from a client can recover the
keys that will allow them to both read the contents of the packet and
generate Initial packets that will be successfully authenticated at
either endpoint. The AEAD also protects Initial packets against
accidental modification.[¶](#section-12.1-4){.pilcrow}

All other packets are protected with keys derived from the cryptographic
handshake. The cryptographic handshake ensures that only the
communicating endpoints receive the corresponding keys for Handshake,
0-RTT, and 1-RTT packets. Packets protected with 0-RTT and 1-RTT keys
have strong confidentiality and integrity
protection.[¶](#section-12.1-5){.pilcrow}

The Packet Number field that appears in some packet types has
alternative confidentiality protection that is applied as part of header
protection; see [Section
5.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.4){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\] for details. The underlying packet
number increases with each packet sent in a given packet number space;
see [Section 12.3](#packet-numbers){.xref} for
details.[¶](#section-12.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-coalesce}
::: {#section-12.2 .section}
### [12.2.](#section-12.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Coalescing Packets](#name-coalescing-packets){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-coalescing-packets}

Initial ([Section 17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}), 0-RTT ([Section
17.2.3](#packet-0rtt){.xref}), and Handshake ([Section
17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref}) packets contain a Length field that
determines the end of the packet. The length includes both the Packet
Number and Payload fields, both of which are confidentiality protected
and initially of unknown length. The length of the Payload field is
learned once header protection is removed.[¶](#section-12.2-1){.pilcrow}

Using the Length field, a sender can coalesce multiple QUIC packets into
one UDP datagram. This can reduce the number of UDP datagrams needed to
complete the cryptographic handshake and start sending data. This can
also be used to construct Path Maximum Transmission Unit (PMTU) probes;
see [Section 14.4.1](#pmtu-probes-src-cid){.xref}. Receivers
[MUST]{.bcp14} be able to process coalesced
packets.[¶](#section-12.2-2){.pilcrow}

Coalescing packets in order of increasing encryption levels (Initial,
0-RTT, Handshake, 1-RTT; see [Section
4.1.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.1.4){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]) makes it more likely that the receiver
will be able to process all the packets in a single pass. A packet with
a short header does not include a length, so it can only be the last
packet included in a UDP datagram. An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} include
multiple frames in a single packet if they are to be sent at the same
encryption level, instead of coalescing multiple packets at the same
encryption level.[¶](#section-12.2-3){.pilcrow}

Receivers [MAY]{.bcp14} route based on the information in the first
packet contained in a UDP datagram. Senders [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} coalesce
QUIC packets with different connection IDs into a single UDP datagram.
Receivers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ignore any subsequent packets with a
different Destination Connection ID than the first packet in the
datagram.[¶](#section-12.2-4){.pilcrow}

Every QUIC packet that is coalesced into a single UDP datagram is
separate and complete. The receiver of coalesced QUIC packets
[MUST]{.bcp14} individually process each QUIC packet and separately
acknowledge them, as if they were received as the payload of different
UDP datagrams. For example, if decryption fails (because the keys are
not available or for any other reason), the receiver [MAY]{.bcp14}
either discard or buffer the packet for later processing and
[MUST]{.bcp14} attempt to process the remaining
packets.[¶](#section-12.2-5){.pilcrow}

Retry packets ([Section 17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}), Version
Negotiation packets ([Section 17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}), and
packets with a short header ([Section 17.3](#short-header){.xref}) do
not contain a Length field and so cannot be followed by other packets in
the same UDP datagram. Note also that there is no situation where a
Retry or Version Negotiation packet is coalesced with another
packet.[¶](#section-12.2-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-numbers}
::: {#section-12.3 .section}
### [12.3.](#section-12.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Packet Numbers](#name-packet-numbers){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packet-numbers}

The packet number is an integer in the range 0 to 2^62^-1. This number
is used in determining the cryptographic nonce for packet protection.
Each endpoint maintains a separate packet number for sending and
receiving.[¶](#section-12.3-1){.pilcrow}

Packet numbers are limited to this range because they need to be
representable in whole in the Largest Acknowledged field of an ACK frame
([Section 19.3](#frame-ack){.xref}). When present in a long or short
header, however, packet numbers are reduced and encoded in 1 to 4 bytes;
see [Section
17.1](#packet-encoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.3-2){.pilcrow}

Version Negotiation ([Section 17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}) and Retry
([Section 17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}) packets do not include a packet
number.[¶](#section-12.3-3){.pilcrow}

Packet numbers are divided into three spaces in
QUIC:[¶](#section-12.3-4){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Initial space:

:   All Initial packets ([Section 17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}) are
    in this space.[¶](#section-12.3-5.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Handshake space:

:   All Handshake packets ([Section 17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref})
    are in this space.[¶](#section-12.3-5.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Application data space:

:   All 0-RTT ([Section 17.2.3](#packet-0rtt){.xref}) and 1-RTT
    ([Section 17.3.1](#packet-1rtt){.xref}) packets are in this
    space.[¶](#section-12.3-5.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

As described in \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\], each packet type uses
different protection keys.[¶](#section-12.3-6){.pilcrow}

Conceptually, a packet number space is the context in which a packet can
be processed and acknowledged. Initial packets can only be sent with
Initial packet protection keys and acknowledged in packets that are also
Initial packets. Similarly, Handshake packets are sent at the Handshake
encryption level and can only be acknowledged in Handshake
packets.[¶](#section-12.3-7){.pilcrow}

This enforces cryptographic separation between the data sent in the
different packet number spaces. Packet numbers in each space start at
packet number 0. Subsequent packets sent in the same packet number space
[MUST]{.bcp14} increase the packet number by at least
one.[¶](#section-12.3-8){.pilcrow}

0-RTT and 1-RTT data exist in the same packet number space to make loss
recovery algorithms easier to implement between the two packet
types.[¶](#section-12.3-9){.pilcrow}

A QUIC endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} reuse a packet number within the same
packet number space in one connection. If the packet number for sending
reaches 2^62^-1, the sender [MUST]{.bcp14} close the connection without
sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame or any further packets; an endpoint
[MAY]{.bcp14} send a Stateless Reset ([Section
10.3](#stateless-reset){.xref}) in response to further packets that it
receives.[¶](#section-12.3-10){.pilcrow}

A receiver [MUST]{.bcp14} discard a newly unprotected packet unless it
is certain that it has not processed another packet with the same packet
number from the same packet number space. Duplicate suppression
[MUST]{.bcp14} happen after removing packet protection for the reasons
described in [Section
9.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-9.5){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-12.3-11){.pilcrow}

Endpoints that track all individual packets for the purposes of
detecting duplicates are at risk of accumulating excessive state. The
data required for detecting duplicates can be limited by maintaining a
minimum packet number below which all packets are immediately dropped.
Any minimum needs to account for large variations in round-trip time,
which includes the possibility that a peer might probe network paths
with much larger round-trip times; see [Section
9](#migration){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.3-12){.pilcrow}

Packet number encoding at a sender and decoding at a receiver are
described in [Section
17.1](#packet-encoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.3-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#frames}
::: {#section-12.4 .section}
### [12.4.](#section-12.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Frames and Frame Types](#name-frames-and-frame-types){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-frames-and-frame-types}

The payload of QUIC packets, after removing packet protection, consists
of a sequence of complete frames, as shown in [Figure
11](#packet-frames){.xref}. Version Negotiation, Stateless Reset, and
Retry packets do not contain frames.[¶](#section-12.4-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-quic-payload}

::: {#packet-frames}
::: {#section-12.4-2.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Packet Payload {
      Frame (8..) ...,
    }
:::

[Figure 11](#figure-11){.selfRef}: [QUIC
Payload](#name-quic-payload){.selfRef}
:::

The payload of a packet that contains frames [MUST]{.bcp14} contain at
least one frame, and [MAY]{.bcp14} contain multiple frames and multiple
frame types. An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat receipt of a packet
containing no frames as a connection error of type PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.
Frames always fit within a single QUIC packet and cannot span multiple
packets.[¶](#section-12.4-3){.pilcrow}

Each frame begins with a Frame Type, indicating its type, followed by
additional type-dependent fields:[¶](#section-12.4-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-generic-frame-layout}

::: {#frame-layout}
::: {#section-12.4-5.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Frame {
      Frame Type (i),
      Type-Dependent Fields (..),
    }
:::

[Figure 12](#figure-12){.selfRef}: [Generic Frame
Layout](#name-generic-frame-layout){.selfRef}
:::

[Table 3](#frame-types){.xref} lists and summarizes information about
each frame type that is defined in this specification. A description of
this summary is included after the table.[¶](#section-12.4-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-frame-types}

::: {#frame-types}
  Type Value   Frame Type Name        Definition                                            Pkts      Spec
  ------------ ---------------------- ----------------------------------------------------- --------- ------
  0x00         PADDING                [Section 19.1](#frame-padding){.xref}                 IH01      NP
  0x01         PING                   [Section 19.2](#frame-ping){.xref}                    IH01      
  0x02-0x03    ACK                    [Section 19.3](#frame-ack){.xref}                     IH_1      NC
  0x04         RESET_STREAM           [Section 19.4](#frame-reset-stream){.xref}            \_\_01    
  0x05         STOP_SENDING           [Section 19.5](#frame-stop-sending){.xref}            \_\_01    
  0x06         CRYPTO                 [Section 19.6](#frame-crypto){.xref}                  IH_1      
  0x07         NEW_TOKEN              [Section 19.7](#frame-new-token){.xref}               \_\_\_1   
  0x08-0x0f    STREAM                 [Section 19.8](#frame-stream){.xref}                  \_\_01    F
  0x10         MAX_DATA               [Section 19.9](#frame-max-data){.xref}                \_\_01    
  0x11         MAX_STREAM_DATA        [Section 19.10](#frame-max-stream-data){.xref}        \_\_01    
  0x12-0x13    MAX_STREAMS            [Section 19.11](#frame-max-streams){.xref}            \_\_01    
  0x14         DATA_BLOCKED           [Section 19.12](#frame-data-blocked){.xref}           \_\_01    
  0x15         STREAM_DATA_BLOCKED    [Section 19.13](#frame-stream-data-blocked){.xref}    \_\_01    
  0x16-0x17    STREAMS_BLOCKED        [Section 19.14](#frame-streams-blocked){.xref}        \_\_01    
  0x18         NEW_CONNECTION_ID      [Section 19.15](#frame-new-connection-id){.xref}      \_\_01    P
  0x19         RETIRE_CONNECTION_ID   [Section 19.16](#frame-retire-connection-id){.xref}   \_\_01    
  0x1a         PATH_CHALLENGE         [Section 19.17](#frame-path-challenge){.xref}         \_\_01    P
  0x1b         PATH_RESPONSE          [Section 19.18](#frame-path-response){.xref}          \_\_\_1   P
  0x1c-0x1d    CONNECTION_CLOSE       [Section 19.19](#frame-connection-close){.xref}       ih01      N
  0x1e         HANDSHAKE_DONE         [Section 19.20](#frame-handshake-done){.xref}         \_\_\_1   

  : [Table 3](#table-3){.selfRef}: [Frame
  Types](#name-frame-types){.selfRef}
:::

The format and semantics of each frame type are explained in more detail
in [Section 19](#frame-formats){.xref}. The remainder of this section
provides a summary of important and general
information.[¶](#section-12.4-8){.pilcrow}

The Frame Type in ACK, STREAM, MAX_STREAMS, STREAMS_BLOCKED, and
CONNECTION_CLOSE frames is used to carry other frame-specific flags. For
all other frames, the Frame Type field simply identifies the
frame.[¶](#section-12.4-9){.pilcrow}

The \"Pkts\" column in [Table 3](#frame-types){.xref} lists the types of
packets that each frame type could appear in, indicated by the following
characters:[¶](#section-12.4-10){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

I:

:   Initial ([Section
    17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref})[¶](#section-12.4-11.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

H:

:   Handshake ([Section
    17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref})[¶](#section-12.4-11.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

0:

:   0-RTT ([Section
    17.2.3](#packet-0rtt){.xref})[¶](#section-12.4-11.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

1:

:   1-RTT ([Section
    17.3.1](#packet-1rtt){.xref})[¶](#section-12.4-11.8.1){.pilcrow}

:   

ih:

:   Only a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame of type 0x1c can appear in Initial or
    Handshake packets.[¶](#section-12.4-11.10.1){.pilcrow}

:   

For more details about these restrictions, see [Section
12.5](#frames-and-spaces){.xref}. Note that all frames can appear in
1-RTT packets. An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat receipt of a frame in a
packet type that is not permitted as a connection error of type
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-12.4-12){.pilcrow}

The \"Spec\" column in [Table 3](#frame-types){.xref} summarizes any
special rules governing the processing or generation of the frame type,
as indicated by the following characters:[¶](#section-12.4-13){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

N:

:   Packets containing only frames with this marking are not
    ack-eliciting; see [Section
    13.2](#generating-acks){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.4-14.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

C:

:   Packets containing only frames with this marking do not count toward
    bytes in flight for congestion control purposes; see
    \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-12.4-14.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

P:

:   Packets containing only frames with this marking can be used to
    probe new network paths during connection migration; see [Section
    9.1](#probing){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.4-14.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

F:

:   The contents of frames with this marking are flow controlled; see
    [Section
    4](#flow-control){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.4-14.8.1){.pilcrow}

:   

The \"Pkts\" and \"Spec\" columns in [Table 3](#frame-types){.xref} do
not form part of the IANA registry; see [Section
22.4](#iana-frames){.xref}.[¶](#section-12.4-15){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} treat the receipt of a frame of unknown type
as a connection error of type
FRAME_ENCODING_ERROR.[¶](#section-12.4-16){.pilcrow}

All frames are idempotent in this version of QUIC. That is, a valid
frame does not cause undesirable side effects or errors when received
more than once.[¶](#section-12.4-17){.pilcrow}

The Frame Type field uses a variable-length integer encoding (see
[Section 16](#integer-encoding){.xref}), with one exception. To ensure
simple and efficient implementations of frame parsing, a frame type
[MUST]{.bcp14} use the shortest possible encoding. For frame types
defined in this document, this means a single-byte encoding, even though
it is possible to encode these values as a two-, four-, or eight-byte
variable-length integer. For instance, though 0x4001 is a legitimate
two-byte encoding for a variable-length integer with a value of 1, PING
frames are always encoded as a single byte with the value 0x01. This
rule applies to all current and future QUIC frame types. An endpoint
[MAY]{.bcp14} treat the receipt of a frame type that uses a longer
encoding than necessary as a connection error of type
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-12.4-18){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#frames-and-spaces}
::: {#section-12.5 .section}
### [12.5.](#section-12.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Frames and Number Spaces](#name-frames-and-number-spaces){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-frames-and-number-spaces}

Some frames are prohibited in different packet number spaces. The rules
here generalize those of TLS, in that frames associated with
establishing the connection can usually appear in packets in any packet
number space, whereas those associated with transferring data can only
appear in the application data packet number
space:[¶](#section-12.5-1){.pilcrow}

-   [PADDING, PING, and CRYPTO frames [MAY]{.bcp14} appear in any packet
    number space.[¶](#section-12.5-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.5-2.1}
-   [CONNECTION_CLOSE frames signaling errors at the QUIC layer (type
    0x1c) [MAY]{.bcp14} appear in any packet number space.
    CONNECTION_CLOSE frames signaling application errors (type 0x1d)
    [MUST]{.bcp14} only appear in the application data packet number
    space.[¶](#section-12.5-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.5-2.2}
-   [ACK frames [MAY]{.bcp14} appear in any packet number space but can
    only acknowledge packets that appeared in that packet number space.
    However, as noted below, 0-RTT packets cannot contain ACK
    frames.[¶](#section-12.5-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.5-2.3}
-   [All other frame types [MUST]{.bcp14} only be sent in the
    application data packet number
    space.[¶](#section-12.5-2.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.5-2.4}

Note that it is not possible to send the following frames in 0-RTT
packets for various reasons: ACK, CRYPTO, HANDSHAKE_DONE, NEW_TOKEN,
PATH_RESPONSE, and RETIRE_CONNECTION_ID. A server [MAY]{.bcp14} treat
receipt of these frames in 0-RTT packets as a connection error of type
PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-12.5-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#packetization}
::: {#section-13 .section}
## [13.](#section-13){.section-number .selfRef} [Packetization and Reliability](#name-packetization-and-reliabili){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packetization-and-reliabili}

A sender sends one or more frames in a QUIC packet; see [Section
12.4](#frames){.xref}.[¶](#section-13-1){.pilcrow}

A sender can minimize per-packet bandwidth and computational costs by
including as many frames as possible in each QUIC packet. A sender
[MAY]{.bcp14} wait for a short period of time to collect multiple frames
before sending a packet that is not maximally packed, to avoid sending
out large numbers of small packets. An implementation [MAY]{.bcp14} use
knowledge about application sending behavior or heuristics to determine
whether and for how long to wait. This waiting period is an
implementation decision, and an implementation should be careful to
delay conservatively, since any delay is likely to increase
application-visible latency.[¶](#section-13-2){.pilcrow}

Stream multiplexing is achieved by interleaving STREAM frames from
multiple streams into one or more QUIC packets. A single QUIC packet can
include multiple STREAM frames from one or more
streams.[¶](#section-13-3){.pilcrow}

One of the benefits of QUIC is avoidance of head-of-line blocking across
multiple streams. When a packet loss occurs, only streams with data in
that packet are blocked waiting for a retransmission to be received,
while other streams can continue making progress. Note that when data
from multiple streams is included in a single QUIC packet, loss of that
packet blocks all those streams from making progress. Implementations
are advised to include as few streams as necessary in outgoing packets
without losing transmission efficiency to underfilled
packets.[¶](#section-13-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#processing}
::: {#section-13.1 .section}
### [13.1.](#section-13.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Packet Processing](#name-packet-processing){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packet-processing}

A packet [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be acknowledged until packet protection has
been successfully removed and all frames contained in the packet have
been processed. For STREAM frames, this means the data has been enqueued
in preparation to be received by the application protocol, but it does
not require that data be delivered and
consumed.[¶](#section-13.1-1){.pilcrow}

Once the packet has been fully processed, a receiver acknowledges
receipt by sending one or more ACK frames containing the packet number
of the received packet.[¶](#section-13.1-2){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} treat receipt of an acknowledgment for a
packet it did not send as a connection error of type PROTOCOL_VIOLATION,
if it is able to detect the condition. For further discussion of how
this might be achieved, see [Section
21.4](#optimistic-ack-attack){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#generating-acks}
::: {#section-13.2 .section}
### [13.2.](#section-13.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Generating Acknowledgments](#name-generating-acknowledgments){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-generating-acknowledgments}

Endpoints acknowledge all packets they receive and process. However,
only ack-eliciting packets cause an ACK frame to be sent within the
maximum ack delay. Packets that are not ack-eliciting are only
acknowledged when an ACK frame is sent for other
reasons.[¶](#section-13.2-1){.pilcrow}

When sending a packet for any reason, an endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
attempt to include an ACK frame if one has not been sent recently. Doing
so helps with timely loss detection at the
peer.[¶](#section-13.2-2){.pilcrow}

In general, frequent feedback from a receiver improves loss and
congestion response, but this has to be balanced against excessive load
generated by a receiver that sends an ACK frame in response to every
ack-eliciting packet. The guidance offered below seeks to strike this
balance.[¶](#section-13.2-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#sending-acknowledgments}
::: {#section-13.2.1 .section}
#### [13.2.1.](#section-13.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Sending ACK Frames](#name-sending-ack-frames){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sending-ack-frames}

Every packet [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be acknowledged at least once, and
ack-eliciting packets [MUST]{.bcp14} be acknowledged at least once
within the maximum delay an endpoint communicated using the
max_ack_delay transport parameter; see [Section
18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}. max_ack_delay declares
an explicit contract: an endpoint promises to never intentionally delay
acknowledgments of an ack-eliciting packet by more than the indicated
value. If it does, any excess accrues to the RTT estimate and could
result in spurious or delayed retransmissions from the peer. A sender
uses the receiver\'s max_ack_delay value in determining timeouts for
timer-based retransmission, as detailed in [Section
6.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-13.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} acknowledge all ack-eliciting Initial and
Handshake packets immediately and all ack-eliciting 0-RTT and 1-RTT
packets within its advertised max_ack_delay, with the following
exception. Prior to handshake confirmation, an endpoint might not have
packet protection keys for decrypting Handshake, 0-RTT, or 1-RTT packets
when they are received. It might therefore buffer them and acknowledge
them when the requisite keys become
available.[¶](#section-13.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

Since packets containing only ACK frames are not congestion controlled,
an endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send more than one such packet in
response to receiving an ack-eliciting
packet.[¶](#section-13.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a non-ack-eliciting packet in
response to a non-ack-eliciting packet, even if there are packet gaps
that precede the received packet. This avoids an infinite feedback loop
of acknowledgments, which could prevent the connection from ever
becoming idle. Non-ack-eliciting packets are eventually acknowledged
when the endpoint sends an ACK frame in response to other
events.[¶](#section-13.2.1-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that is only sending ACK frames will not receive
acknowledgments from its peer unless those acknowledgments are included
in packets with ack-eliciting frames. An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send
an ACK frame with other frames when there are new ack-eliciting packets
to acknowledge. When only non-ack-eliciting packets need to be
acknowledged, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} choose not to send an ACK frame
with outgoing frames until an ack-eliciting packet has been
received.[¶](#section-13.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

An endpoint that is only sending non-ack-eliciting packets might choose
to occasionally add an ack-eliciting frame to those packets to ensure
that it receives an acknowledgment; see [Section
13.2.4](#ack-tracking){.xref}. In that case, an endpoint [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} send an ack-eliciting frame in all packets that would
otherwise be non-ack-eliciting, to avoid an infinite feedback loop of
acknowledgments.[¶](#section-13.2.1-6){.pilcrow}

In order to assist loss detection at the sender, an endpoint
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} generate and send an ACK frame without delay when it
receives an ack-eliciting packet either:[¶](#section-13.2.1-7){.pilcrow}

-   [when the received packet has a packet number less than another
    ack-eliciting packet that has been received,
    or[¶](#section-13.2.1-8.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.2.1-8.1}
-   [when the packet has a packet number larger than the
    highest-numbered ack-eliciting packet that has been received and
    there are missing packets between that packet and this
    packet.[¶](#section-13.2.1-8.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.2.1-8.2}

Similarly, packets marked with the ECN Congestion Experienced (CE)
codepoint in the IP header [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be acknowledged immediately,
to reduce the peer\'s response time to congestion
events.[¶](#section-13.2.1-9){.pilcrow}

The algorithms in \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\] are
expected to be resilient to receivers that do not follow the guidance
offered above. However, an implementation should only deviate from these
requirements after careful consideration of the performance implications
of a change, for connections made by the endpoint and for other users of
the network.[¶](#section-13.2.1-10){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#acknowledgment-frequency}
::: {#section-13.2.2 .section}
#### [13.2.2.](#section-13.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Acknowledgment Frequency](#name-acknowledgment-frequency){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-acknowledgment-frequency}

A receiver determines how frequently to send acknowledgments in response
to ack-eliciting packets. This determination involves a
trade-off.[¶](#section-13.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

Endpoints rely on timely acknowledgment to detect loss; see [Section
6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]. Window-based congestion
