controllers, such as the one described in [Section
7](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-7){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\], rely on acknowledgments to
manage their congestion window. In both cases, delaying acknowledgments
can adversely affect performance.[¶](#section-13.2.2-2){.pilcrow}

On the other hand, reducing the frequency of packets that carry only
acknowledgments reduces packet transmission and processing cost at both
endpoints. It can improve connection throughput on severely asymmetric
links and reduce the volume of acknowledgment traffic using return path
capacity; see [Section
3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3449#section-3){.relref} of
\[[RFC3449](#RFC3449){.xref}\].[¶](#section-13.2.2-3){.pilcrow}

A receiver [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send an ACK frame after receiving at least
two ack-eliciting packets. This recommendation is general in nature and
consistent with recommendations for TCP endpoint behavior
\[[RFC5681](#RFC5681){.xref}\]. Knowledge of network conditions,
knowledge of the peer\'s congestion controller, or further research and
experimentation might suggest alternative acknowledgment strategies with
better performance characteristics.[¶](#section-13.2.2-4){.pilcrow}

A receiver [MAY]{.bcp14} process multiple available packets before
determining whether to send an ACK frame in
response.[¶](#section-13.2.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#managing-ack-ranges}
::: {#section-13.2.3 .section}
#### [13.2.3.](#section-13.2.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Managing ACK Ranges](#name-managing-ack-ranges){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-managing-ack-ranges}

When an ACK frame is sent, one or more ranges of acknowledged packets
are included. Including acknowledgments for older packets reduces the
chance of spurious retransmissions caused by losing previously sent ACK
frames, at the cost of larger ACK
frames.[¶](#section-13.2.3-1){.pilcrow}

ACK frames [SHOULD]{.bcp14} always acknowledge the most recently
received packets, and the more out of order the packets are, the more
important it is to send an updated ACK frame quickly, to prevent the
peer from declaring a packet as lost and spuriously retransmitting the
frames it contains. An ACK frame is expected to fit within a single QUIC
packet. If it does not, then older ranges (those with the smallest
packet numbers) are omitted.[¶](#section-13.2.3-2){.pilcrow}

A receiver limits the number of ACK Ranges ([Section
19.3.1](#ack-ranges){.xref}) it remembers and sends in ACK frames, both
to limit the size of ACK frames and to avoid resource exhaustion. After
receiving acknowledgments for an ACK frame, the receiver
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} stop tracking those acknowledged ACK Ranges. Senders
can expect acknowledgments for most packets, but QUIC does not guarantee
receipt of an acknowledgment for every packet that the receiver
processes.[¶](#section-13.2.3-3){.pilcrow}

It is possible that retaining many ACK Ranges could cause an ACK frame
to become too large. A receiver can discard unacknowledged ACK Ranges to
limit ACK frame size, at the cost of increased retransmissions from the
sender. This is necessary if an ACK frame would be too large to fit in a
packet. Receivers [MAY]{.bcp14} also limit ACK frame size further to
preserve space for other frames or to limit the capacity that
acknowledgments consume.[¶](#section-13.2.3-4){.pilcrow}

A receiver [MUST]{.bcp14} retain an ACK Range unless it can ensure that
it will not subsequently accept packets with numbers in that range.
Maintaining a minimum packet number that increases as ranges are
discarded is one way to achieve this with minimal
state.[¶](#section-13.2.3-5){.pilcrow}

Receivers can discard all ACK Ranges, but they [MUST]{.bcp14} retain the
largest packet number that has been successfully processed, as that is
used to recover packet numbers from subsequent packets; see [Section
17.1](#packet-encoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.2.3-6){.pilcrow}

A receiver [SHOULD]{.bcp14} include an ACK Range containing the largest
received packet number in every ACK frame. The Largest Acknowledged
field is used in ECN validation at a sender, and including a lower value
than what was included in a previous ACK frame could cause ECN to be
unnecessarily disabled; see [Section
13.4.2](#ecn-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.2.3-7){.pilcrow}

[Section 13.2.4](#ack-tracking){.xref} describes an exemplary approach
for determining what packets to acknowledge in each ACK frame. Though
the goal of this algorithm is to generate an acknowledgment for every
packet that is processed, it is still possible for acknowledgments to be
lost.[¶](#section-13.2.3-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ack-tracking}
::: {#section-13.2.4 .section}
#### [13.2.4.](#section-13.2.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Limiting Ranges by Tracking ACK Frames](#name-limiting-ranges-by-tracking){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-limiting-ranges-by-tracking}

When a packet containing an ACK frame is sent, the Largest Acknowledged
field in that frame can be saved. When a packet containing an ACK frame
is acknowledged, the receiver can stop acknowledging packets less than
or equal to the Largest Acknowledged field in the sent ACK
frame.[¶](#section-13.2.4-1){.pilcrow}

A receiver that sends only non-ack-eliciting packets, such as ACK
frames, might not receive an acknowledgment for a long period of time.
This could cause the receiver to maintain state for a large number of
ACK frames for a long period of time, and ACK frames it sends could be
unnecessarily large. In such a case, a receiver could send a PING or
other small ack-eliciting frame occasionally, such as once per round
trip, to elicit an ACK from the peer.[¶](#section-13.2.4-2){.pilcrow}

In cases without ACK frame loss, this algorithm allows for a minimum of
1 RTT of reordering. In cases with ACK frame loss and reordering, this
approach does not guarantee that every acknowledgment is seen by the
sender before it is no longer included in the ACK frame. Packets could
be received out of order, and all subsequent ACK frames containing them
could be lost. In this case, the loss recovery algorithm could cause
spurious retransmissions, but the sender will continue making forward
progress.[¶](#section-13.2.4-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#host-delay}
::: {#section-13.2.5 .section}
#### [13.2.5.](#section-13.2.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Measuring and Reporting Host Delay](#name-measuring-and-reporting-hos){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-measuring-and-reporting-hos}

An endpoint measures the delays intentionally introduced between the
time the packet with the largest packet number is received and the time
an acknowledgment is sent. The endpoint encodes this acknowledgment
delay in the ACK Delay field of an ACK frame; see [Section
19.3](#frame-ack){.xref}. This allows the receiver of the ACK frame to
adjust for any intentional delays, which is important for getting a
better estimate of the path RTT when acknowledgments are
delayed.[¶](#section-13.2.5-1){.pilcrow}

A packet might be held in the OS kernel or elsewhere on the host before
being processed. An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} include delays that it
does not control when populating the ACK Delay field in an ACK frame.
However, endpoints [SHOULD]{.bcp14} include buffering delays caused by
unavailability of decryption keys, since these delays can be large and
are likely to be non-repeating.[¶](#section-13.2.5-2){.pilcrow}

When the measured acknowledgment delay is larger than its max_ack_delay,
an endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} report the measured delay. This information
is especially useful during the handshake when delays might be large;
see [Section
13.2.1](#sending-acknowledgments){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.2.5-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ack-frames-and-packet-protection}
::: {#section-13.2.6 .section}
#### [13.2.6.](#section-13.2.6){.section-number .selfRef} [ACK Frames and Packet Protection](#name-ack-frames-and-packet-prote){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-ack-frames-and-packet-prote}

ACK frames [MUST]{.bcp14} only be carried in a packet that has the same
packet number space as the packet being acknowledged; see [Section
12.1](#packet-protected){.xref}. For instance, packets that are
protected with 1-RTT keys [MUST]{.bcp14} be acknowledged in packets that
are also protected with 1-RTT keys.[¶](#section-13.2.6-1){.pilcrow}

Packets that a client sends with 0-RTT packet protection [MUST]{.bcp14}
be acknowledged by the server in packets protected by 1-RTT keys. This
can mean that the client is unable to use these acknowledgments if the
server cryptographic handshake messages are delayed or lost. Note that
the same limitation applies to other data sent by the server protected
by the 1-RTT keys.[¶](#section-13.2.6-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#padding-frames-consume-congestion-window}
::: {#section-13.2.7 .section}
#### [13.2.7.](#section-13.2.7){.section-number .selfRef} [PADDING Frames Consume Congestion Window](#name-padding-frames-consume-cong){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-padding-frames-consume-cong}

Packets containing PADDING frames are considered to be in flight for
congestion control purposes \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].
Packets containing only PADDING frames therefore consume congestion
window but do not generate acknowledgments that will open the congestion
window. To avoid a deadlock, a sender [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ensure that other
frames are sent periodically in addition to PADDING frames to elicit
acknowledgments from the receiver.[¶](#section-13.2.7-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#retransmission-of-information}
::: {#section-13.3 .section}
### [13.3.](#section-13.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Retransmission of Information](#name-retransmission-of-informati){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-retransmission-of-informati}

QUIC packets that are determined to be lost are not retransmitted whole.
The same applies to the frames that are contained within lost packets.
Instead, the information that might be carried in frames is sent again
in new frames as needed.[¶](#section-13.3-1){.pilcrow}

New frames and packets are used to carry information that is determined
to have been lost. In general, information is sent again when a packet
containing that information is determined to be lost, and sending ceases
when a packet containing that information is
acknowledged.[¶](#section-13.3-2){.pilcrow}

-   [Data sent in CRYPTO frames is retransmitted according to the rules
    in \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\], until all data has
    been acknowledged. Data in CRYPTO frames for Initial and Handshake
    packets is discarded when keys for the corresponding packet number
    space are
    discarded.[¶](#section-13.3-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.1}
-   [Application data sent in STREAM frames is retransmitted in new
    STREAM frames unless the endpoint has sent a RESET_STREAM for that
    stream. Once an endpoint sends a RESET_STREAM frame, no further
    STREAM frames are
    needed.[¶](#section-13.3-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.2}
-   [ACK frames carry the most recent set of acknowledgments and the
    acknowledgment delay from the largest acknowledged packet, as
    described in [Section 13.2.1](#sending-acknowledgments){.xref}.
    Delaying the transmission of packets containing ACK frames or
    resending old ACK frames can cause the peer to generate an inflated
    RTT sample or unnecessarily disable
    ECN.[¶](#section-13.3-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.3}
-   [Cancellation of stream transmission, as carried in a RESET_STREAM
    frame, is sent until acknowledged or until all stream data is
    acknowledged by the peer (that is, either the \"Reset Recvd\" or
    \"Data Recvd\" state is reached on the sending part of the stream).
    The content of a RESET_STREAM frame [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} change when
    it is sent
    again.[¶](#section-13.3-3.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.4}
-   [Similarly, a request to cancel stream transmission, as encoded in a
    STOP_SENDING frame, is sent until the receiving part of the stream
    enters either a \"Data Recvd\" or \"Reset Recvd\" state; see
    [Section
    3.5](#solicited-state-transitions){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.3-3.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.5}
-   [Connection close signals, including packets that contain
    CONNECTION_CLOSE frames, are not sent again when packet loss is
    detected. Resending these signals is described in [Section
    10](#termination){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.3-3.6){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.6}
-   [The current connection maximum data is sent in MAX_DATA frames. An
    updated value is sent in a MAX_DATA frame if the packet containing
    the most recently sent MAX_DATA frame is declared lost or when the
    endpoint decides to update the limit. Care is necessary to avoid
    sending this frame too often, as the limit can increase frequently
    and cause an unnecessarily large number of MAX_DATA frames to be
    sent; see [Section
    4.2](#fc-credit){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.3-3.7){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.7}
-   [The current maximum stream data offset is sent in MAX_STREAM_DATA
    frames. Like MAX_DATA, an updated value is sent when the packet
    containing the most recent MAX_STREAM_DATA frame for a stream is
    lost or when the limit is updated, with care taken to prevent the
    frame from being sent too often. An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} stop
    sending MAX_STREAM_DATA frames when the receiving part of the stream
    enters a \"Size Known\" or \"Reset Recvd\"
    state.[¶](#section-13.3-3.8){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.8}
-   [The limit on streams of a given type is sent in MAX_STREAMS frames.
    Like MAX_DATA, an updated value is sent when a packet containing the
    most recent MAX_STREAMS for a stream type frame is declared lost or
    when the limit is updated, with care taken to prevent the frame from
    being sent too
    often.[¶](#section-13.3-3.9){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.9}
-   [Blocked signals are carried in DATA_BLOCKED, STREAM_DATA_BLOCKED,
    and STREAMS_BLOCKED frames. DATA_BLOCKED frames have connection
    scope, STREAM_DATA_BLOCKED frames have stream scope, and
    STREAMS_BLOCKED frames are scoped to a specific stream type. A new
    frame is sent if a packet containing the most recent frame for a
    scope is lost, but only while the endpoint is blocked on the
    corresponding limit. These frames always include the limit that is
    causing blocking at the time that they are
    transmitted.[¶](#section-13.3-3.10){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.10}
-   [A liveness or path validation check using PATH_CHALLENGE frames is
    sent periodically until a matching PATH_RESPONSE frame is received
    or until there is no remaining need for liveness or path validation
    checking. PATH_CHALLENGE frames include a different payload each
    time they are
    sent.[¶](#section-13.3-3.11){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.11}
-   [Responses to path validation using PATH_RESPONSE frames are sent
    just once. The peer is expected to send more PATH_CHALLENGE frames
    as necessary to evoke additional PATH_RESPONSE
    frames.[¶](#section-13.3-3.12){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.12}
-   [New connection IDs are sent in NEW_CONNECTION_ID frames and
    retransmitted if the packet containing them is lost. Retransmissions
    of this frame carry the same sequence number value. Likewise,
    retired connection IDs are sent in RETIRE_CONNECTION_ID frames and
    retransmitted if the packet containing them is
    lost.[¶](#section-13.3-3.13){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.13}
-   [NEW_TOKEN frames are retransmitted if the packet containing them is
    lost. No special support is made for detecting reordered and
    duplicated NEW_TOKEN frames other than a direct comparison of the
    frame
    contents.[¶](#section-13.3-3.14){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.14}
-   [PING and PADDING frames contain no information, so lost PING or
    PADDING frames do not require
    repair.[¶](#section-13.3-3.15){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.15}
-   [The HANDSHAKE_DONE frame [MUST]{.bcp14} be retransmitted until it
    is
    acknowledged.[¶](#section-13.3-3.16){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.3-3.16}

Endpoints [SHOULD]{.bcp14} prioritize retransmission of data over
sending new data, unless priorities specified by the application
indicate otherwise; see [Section
2.3](#stream-prioritization){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.3-4){.pilcrow}

Even though a sender is encouraged to assemble frames containing
up-to-date information every time it sends a packet, it is not forbidden
to retransmit copies of frames from lost packets. A sender that
retransmits copies of frames needs to handle decreases in available
payload size due to changes in packet number length, connection ID
length, and path MTU. A receiver [MUST]{.bcp14} accept packets
containing an outdated frame, such as a MAX_DATA frame carrying a
smaller maximum data value than one found in an older
packet.[¶](#section-13.3-5){.pilcrow}

A sender [SHOULD]{.bcp14} avoid retransmitting information from packets
once they are acknowledged. This includes packets that are acknowledged
after being declared lost, which can happen in the presence of network
reordering. Doing so requires senders to retain information about
packets after they are declared lost. A sender can discard this
information after a period of time elapses that adequately allows for
reordering, such as a PTO ([Section
6.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]), or based on other events,
such as reaching a memory limit.[¶](#section-13.3-6){.pilcrow}

Upon detecting losses, a sender [MUST]{.bcp14} take appropriate
congestion control action. The details of loss detection and congestion
control are described in
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-13.3-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ecn}
::: {#section-13.4 .section}
### [13.4.](#section-13.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Explicit Congestion Notification](#name-explicit-congestion-notific){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-explicit-congestion-notific}

QUIC endpoints can use ECN \[[RFC3168](#RFC3168){.xref}\] to detect and
respond to network congestion. ECN allows an endpoint to set an
ECN-Capable Transport (ECT) codepoint in the ECN field of an IP packet.
A network node can then indicate congestion by setting the ECN-CE
codepoint in the ECN field instead of dropping the packet
\[[RFC8087](#RFC8087){.xref}\]. Endpoints react to reported congestion
by reducing their sending rate in response, as described in
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-13.4-1){.pilcrow}

To enable ECN, a sending QUIC endpoint first determines whether a path
supports ECN marking and whether the peer reports the ECN values in
received IP headers; see [Section
13.4.2](#ecn-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.4-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#reporting-ecn-counts}
::: {#section-13.4.1 .section}
#### [13.4.1.](#section-13.4.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Reporting ECN Counts](#name-reporting-ecn-counts){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-reporting-ecn-counts}

The use of ECN requires the receiving endpoint to read the ECN field
from an IP packet, which is not possible on all platforms. If an
endpoint does not implement ECN support or does not have access to
received ECN fields, it does not report ECN counts for packets it
receives.[¶](#section-13.4.1-1){.pilcrow}

Even if an endpoint does not set an ECT field in packets it sends, the
endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} provide feedback about ECN markings it receives,
if these are accessible. Failing to report the ECN counts will cause the
sender to disable the use of ECN for this
connection.[¶](#section-13.4.1-2){.pilcrow}

On receiving an IP packet with an ECT(0), ECT(1), or ECN-CE codepoint,
an ECN-enabled endpoint accesses the ECN field and increases the
corresponding ECT(0), ECT(1), or ECN-CE count. These ECN counts are
included in subsequent ACK frames; see Sections
[13.2](#generating-acks){.xref} and
[19.3](#frame-ack){.xref}.[¶](#section-13.4.1-3){.pilcrow}

Each packet number space maintains separate acknowledgment state and
separate ECN counts. Coalesced QUIC packets (see [Section
12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}) share the same IP header so the ECN
counts are incremented once for each coalesced QUIC
packet.[¶](#section-13.4.1-4){.pilcrow}

For example, if one each of an Initial, Handshake, and 1-RTT QUIC packet
are coalesced into a single UDP datagram, the ECN counts for all three
packet number spaces will be incremented by one each, based on the ECN
field of the single IP header.[¶](#section-13.4.1-5){.pilcrow}

ECN counts are only incremented when QUIC packets from the received IP
packet are processed. As such, duplicate QUIC packets are not processed
and do not increase ECN counts; see [Section
21.10](#security-ecn){.xref} for relevant security
concerns.[¶](#section-13.4.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ecn-validation}
::: {#section-13.4.2 .section}
#### [13.4.2.](#section-13.4.2){.section-number .selfRef} [ECN Validation](#name-ecn-validation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-ecn-validation}

It is possible for faulty network devices to corrupt or erroneously drop
packets that carry a non-zero ECN codepoint. To ensure connectivity in
the presence of such devices, an endpoint validates the ECN counts for
each network path and disables the use of ECN on that path if errors are
detected.[¶](#section-13.4.2-1){.pilcrow}

To perform ECN validation for a new
path:[¶](#section-13.4.2-2){.pilcrow}

-   [The endpoint sets an ECT(0) codepoint in the IP header of early
    outgoing packets sent on a new path to the peer
    \[[RFC8311](#RFC8311){.xref}\].[¶](#section-13.4.2-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.4.2-3.1}
-   [The endpoint monitors whether all packets sent with an ECT
    codepoint are eventually deemed lost ([Section
    6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6){.relref} of
    \[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]), indicating that ECN
    validation has
    failed.[¶](#section-13.4.2-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-13.4.2-3.2}

If an endpoint has cause to expect that IP packets with an ECT codepoint
might be dropped by a faulty network element, the endpoint could set an
ECT codepoint for only the first ten outgoing packets on a path, or for
a period of three PTOs (see [Section
6.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\]). If all packets marked with
non-zero ECN codepoints are subsequently lost, it can disable marking on
the assumption that the marking caused the
loss.[¶](#section-13.4.2-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint thus attempts to use ECN and validates this for each new
connection, when switching to a server\'s preferred address, and on
active connection migration to a new path. [Appendix
A.4](#ecn-alg){.xref} describes one possible
algorithm.[¶](#section-13.4.2-5){.pilcrow}

Other methods of probing paths for ECN support are possible, as are
different marking strategies. Implementations [MAY]{.bcp14} use other
methods defined in RFCs; see \[[RFC8311](#RFC8311){.xref}\].
Implementations that use the ECT(1) codepoint need to perform ECN
validation using the reported ECT(1)
counts.[¶](#section-13.4.2-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#ecn-ack}
::: {#section-13.4.2.1 .section}
##### [13.4.2.1.](#section-13.4.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Receiving ACK Frames with ECN Counts](#name-receiving-ack-frames-with-e){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-receiving-ack-frames-with-e}

Erroneous application of ECN-CE markings by the network can result in
degraded connection performance. An endpoint that receives an ACK frame
with ECN counts therefore validates the counts before using them. It
performs this validation by comparing newly received counts against
those from the last successfully processed ACK frame. Any increase in
the ECN counts is validated based on the ECN markings that were applied
to packets that are newly acknowledged in the ACK
frame.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

If an ACK frame newly acknowledges a packet that the endpoint sent with
either the ECT(0) or ECT(1) codepoint set, ECN validation fails if the
corresponding ECN counts are not present in the ACK frame. This check
detects a network element that zeroes the ECN field or a peer that does
not report ECN markings.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

ECN validation also fails if the sum of the increase in ECT(0) and
ECN-CE counts is less than the number of newly acknowledged packets that
were originally sent with an ECT(0) marking. Similarly, ECN validation
fails if the sum of the increases to ECT(1) and ECN-CE counts is less
than the number of newly acknowledged packets sent with an ECT(1)
marking. These checks can detect remarking of ECN-CE markings by the
network.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

An endpoint could miss acknowledgments for a packet when ACK frames are
lost. It is therefore possible for the total increase in ECT(0), ECT(1),
and ECN-CE counts to be greater than the number of packets that are
newly acknowledged by an ACK frame. This is why ECN counts are permitted
to be larger than the total number of packets that are
acknowledged.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-4){.pilcrow}

Validating ECN counts from reordered ACK frames can result in failure.
An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} fail ECN validation as a result of
processing an ACK frame that does not increase the largest acknowledged
packet number.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

ECN validation can fail if the received total count for either ECT(0) or
ECT(1) exceeds the total number of packets sent with each corresponding
ECT codepoint. In particular, validation will fail when an endpoint
receives a non-zero ECN count corresponding to an ECT codepoint that it
never applied. This check detects when packets are remarked to ECT(0) or
ECT(1) in the network.[¶](#section-13.4.2.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ecn-validation-outcomes}
::: {#section-13.4.2.2 .section}
##### [13.4.2.2.](#section-13.4.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [ECN Validation Outcomes](#name-ecn-validation-outcomes){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-ecn-validation-outcomes}

If validation fails, then the endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} disable ECN. It
stops setting the ECT codepoint in IP packets that it sends, assuming
that either the network path or the peer does not support
ECN.[¶](#section-13.4.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

Even if validation fails, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} revalidate ECN for
the same path at any later time in the connection. An endpoint could
continue to periodically attempt
validation.[¶](#section-13.4.2.2-2){.pilcrow}

Upon successful validation, an endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} continue to set an
ECT codepoint in subsequent packets it sends, with the expectation that
the path is ECN capable. Network routing and path elements can change
mid-connection; an endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} disable ECN if validation
later fails.[¶](#section-13.4.2.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#datagram-size}
::: {#section-14 .section}
## [14.](#section-14){.section-number .selfRef} [Datagram Size](#name-datagram-size){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-datagram-size}

A UDP datagram can include one or more QUIC packets. The datagram size
refers to the total UDP payload size of a single UDP datagram carrying
QUIC packets. The datagram size includes one or more QUIC packet headers
and protected payloads, but not the UDP or IP
headers.[¶](#section-14-1){.pilcrow}

The maximum datagram size is defined as the largest size of UDP payload
that can be sent across a network path using a single UDP datagram. QUIC
[MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be used if the network path cannot support a maximum
datagram size of at least 1200 bytes.[¶](#section-14-2){.pilcrow}

QUIC assumes a minimum IP packet size of at least 1280 bytes. This is
the IPv6 minimum size \[[IPv6](#IPv6){.xref}\] and is also supported by
most modern IPv4 networks. Assuming the minimum IP header size of 40
bytes for IPv6 and 20 bytes for IPv4 and a UDP header size of 8 bytes,
this results in a maximum datagram size of 1232 bytes for IPv6 and 1252
bytes for IPv4. Thus, modern IPv4 and all IPv6 network paths are
expected to be able to support QUIC.[¶](#section-14-3){.pilcrow}

Note: This requirement to support a UDP payload of 1200 bytes limits the
space available for IPv6 extension headers to 32 bytes or IPv4 options
to 52 bytes if the path only supports the IPv6 minimum MTU of 1280
bytes. This affects Initial packets and path
validation.[¶](#section-14-4.1){.pilcrow}

Any maximum datagram size larger than 1200 bytes can be discovered using
Path Maximum Transmission Unit Discovery (PMTUD) (see [Section
14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref}) or Datagram Packetization Layer PMTU Discovery
(DPLPMTUD) (see [Section
14.3](#dplpmtud){.xref}).[¶](#section-14-5){.pilcrow}

Enforcement of the max_udp_payload_size transport parameter ([Section
18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}) might act as an
additional limit on the maximum datagram size. A sender can avoid
exceeding this limit, once the value is known. However, prior to
learning the value of the transport parameter, endpoints risk datagrams
being lost if they send datagrams larger than the smallest allowed
maximum datagram size of 1200 bytes.[¶](#section-14-6){.pilcrow}

UDP datagrams [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be fragmented at the IP layer. In IPv4
\[[IPv4](#IPv4){.xref}\], the Don\'t Fragment (DF) bit [MUST]{.bcp14} be
set if possible, to prevent fragmentation on the
path.[¶](#section-14-7){.pilcrow}

QUIC sometimes requires datagrams to be no smaller than a certain size;
see [Section 8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref} as an example. However,
the size of a datagram is not authenticated. That is, if an endpoint
receives a datagram of a certain size, it cannot know that the sender
sent the datagram at the same size. Therefore, an endpoint [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} close a connection when it receives a datagram that does
not meet size constraints; the endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} discard such
datagrams.[¶](#section-14-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#initial-size}
::: {#section-14.1 .section}
### [14.1.](#section-14.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Initial Datagram Size](#name-initial-datagram-size){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-initial-datagram-size}

A client [MUST]{.bcp14} expand the payload of all UDP datagrams carrying
Initial packets to at least the smallest allowed maximum datagram size
of 1200 bytes by adding PADDING frames to the Initial packet or by
coalescing the Initial packet; see [Section
12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}. Initial packets can even be coalesced
with invalid packets, which a receiver will discard. Similarly, a server
[MUST]{.bcp14} expand the payload of all UDP datagrams carrying
ack-eliciting Initial packets to at least the smallest allowed maximum
datagram size of 1200 bytes.[¶](#section-14.1-1){.pilcrow}

Sending UDP datagrams of this size ensures that the network path
supports a reasonable Path Maximum Transmission Unit (PMTU), in both
directions. Additionally, a client that expands Initial packets helps
reduce the amplitude of amplification attacks caused by server responses
toward an unverified client address; see [Section
8](#address-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-14.1-2){.pilcrow}

Datagrams containing Initial packets [MAY]{.bcp14} exceed 1200 bytes if
the sender believes that the network path and peer both support the size
that it chooses.[¶](#section-14.1-3){.pilcrow}

A server [MUST]{.bcp14} discard an Initial packet that is carried in a
UDP datagram with a payload that is smaller than the smallest allowed
maximum datagram size of 1200 bytes. A server [MAY]{.bcp14} also
immediately close the connection by sending a CONNECTION_CLOSE frame
with an error code of PROTOCOL_VIOLATION; see [Section
10.2.3](#immediate-close-hs){.xref}.[¶](#section-14.1-4){.pilcrow}

The server [MUST]{.bcp14} also limit the number of bytes it sends before
validating the address of the client; see [Section
8](#address-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-14.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#path-maximum-transmission-unit}
::: {#section-14.2 .section}
### [14.2.](#section-14.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Path Maximum Transmission Unit](#name-path-maximum-transmission-u){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-path-maximum-transmission-u}

The PMTU is the maximum size of the entire IP packet, including the IP
header, UDP header, and UDP payload. The UDP payload includes one or
more QUIC packet headers and protected payloads. The PMTU can depend on
path characteristics and can therefore change over time. The largest UDP
payload an endpoint sends at any given time is referred to as the
endpoint\'s maximum datagram size.[¶](#section-14.2-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} use DPLPMTUD ([Section
14.3](#dplpmtud){.xref}) or PMTUD ([Section 14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref}) to
determine whether the path to a destination will support a desired
maximum datagram size without fragmentation. In the absence of these
mechanisms, QUIC endpoints [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} send datagrams larger
than the smallest allowed maximum datagram
size.[¶](#section-14.2-2){.pilcrow}

Both DPLPMTUD and PMTUD send datagrams that are larger than the current
maximum datagram size, referred to as PMTU probes. All QUIC packets that
are not sent in a PMTU probe [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be sized to fit within the
maximum datagram size to avoid the datagram being fragmented or dropped
\[[RFC8085](#RFC8085){.xref}\].[¶](#section-14.2-3){.pilcrow}

If a QUIC endpoint determines that the PMTU between any pair of local
and remote IP addresses cannot support the smallest allowed maximum
datagram size of 1200 bytes, it [MUST]{.bcp14} immediately cease sending
QUIC packets, except for those in PMTU probes or those containing
CONNECTION_CLOSE frames, on the affected path. An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14}
terminate the connection if an alternative path cannot be
found.[¶](#section-14.2-4){.pilcrow}

Each pair of local and remote addresses could have a different PMTU.
QUIC implementations that implement any kind of PMTU discovery therefore
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} maintain a maximum datagram size for each combination
of local and remote IP addresses.[¶](#section-14.2-5){.pilcrow}

A QUIC implementation [MAY]{.bcp14} be more conservative in computing
the maximum datagram size to allow for unknown tunnel overheads or IP
header options/extensions.[¶](#section-14.2-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#pmtud}
::: {#section-14.2.1 .section}
#### [14.2.1.](#section-14.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Handling of ICMP Messages by PMTUD](#name-handling-of-icmp-messages-b){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handling-of-icmp-messages-b}

PMTUD \[[RFC1191](#RFC1191){.xref}\] \[[RFC8201](#RFC8201){.xref}\]
relies on reception of ICMP messages (that is, IPv6 Packet Too Big (PTB)
messages) that indicate when an IP packet is dropped because it is
larger than the local router MTU. DPLPMTUD can also optionally use these
messages. This use of ICMP messages is potentially vulnerable to attacks
by entities that cannot observe packets but might successfully guess the
addresses used on the path. These attacks could reduce the PMTU to a
bandwidth-inefficient value.[¶](#section-14.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14} ignore an ICMP message that claims the PMTU
has decreased below QUIC\'s smallest allowed maximum datagram
size.[¶](#section-14.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

The requirements for generating ICMP \[[RFC1812](#RFC1812){.xref}\]
\[[RFC4443](#RFC4443){.xref}\] state that the quoted packet should
contain as much of the original packet as possible without exceeding the
minimum MTU for the IP version. The size of the quoted packet can
actually be smaller, or the information unintelligible, as described in
[Section
1.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-1.1){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\].[¶](#section-14.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

QUIC endpoints using PMTUD [SHOULD]{.bcp14} validate ICMP messages to
protect from packet injection as specified in
\[[RFC8201](#RFC8201){.xref}\] and [Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8085#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[RFC8085](#RFC8085){.xref}\]. This validation [SHOULD]{.bcp14} use the
quoted packet supplied in the payload of an ICMP message to associate
the message with a corresponding transport connection (see [Section
4.6.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-4.6.1){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]). ICMP message validation
[MUST]{.bcp14} include matching IP addresses and UDP ports
\[[RFC8085](#RFC8085){.xref}\] and, when possible, connection IDs to an
active QUIC session. The endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ignore all ICMP
messages that fail validation.[¶](#section-14.2.1-4){.pilcrow}

An endpoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} increase the PMTU based on ICMP messages;
see Item 6 in [Section
3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-3){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]. Any reduction in QUIC\'s maximum
datagram size in response to ICMP messages [MAY]{.bcp14} be provisional
until QUIC\'s loss detection algorithm determines that the quoted packet
has actually been lost.[¶](#section-14.2.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#dplpmtud}
::: {#section-14.3 .section}
### [14.3.](#section-14.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Datagram Packetization Layer PMTU Discovery](#name-datagram-packetization-laye){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-datagram-packetization-laye}

DPLPMTUD \[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\] relies on tracking loss or
acknowledgment of QUIC packets that are carried in PMTU probes. PMTU
probes for DPLPMTUD that use the PADDING frame implement \"Probing using
padding data\", as defined in [Section
4.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-4.1){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\].[¶](#section-14.3-1){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [SHOULD]{.bcp14} set the initial value of BASE_PLPMTU
([Section
5.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-5.1){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]) to be consistent with QUIC\'s smallest
allowed maximum datagram size. The MIN_PLPMTU is the same as the
BASE_PLPMTU.[¶](#section-14.3-2){.pilcrow}

QUIC endpoints implementing DPLPMTUD maintain a DPLPMTUD Maximum Packet
Size (MPS) ([Section
4.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-4.4){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]) for each combination of local and
remote IP addresses. This corresponds to the maximum datagram
size.[¶](#section-14.3-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#dplpmtud-and-initial-connectivity}
::: {#section-14.3.1 .section}
#### [14.3.1.](#section-14.3.1){.section-number .selfRef} [DPLPMTUD and Initial Connectivity](#name-dplpmtud-and-initial-connec){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-dplpmtud-and-initial-connec}

From the perspective of DPLPMTUD, QUIC is an acknowledged Packetization
Layer (PL). A QUIC sender can therefore enter the DPLPMTUD BASE state
([Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]) when the QUIC connection handshake has
been completed.[¶](#section-14.3.1-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#validating-the-network-path-with-dplpmtud}
::: {#section-14.3.2 .section}
#### [14.3.2.](#section-14.3.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Validating the Network Path with DPLPMTUD](#name-validating-the-network-path){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-validating-the-network-path}

QUIC is an acknowledged PL; therefore, a QUIC sender does not implement
a DPLPMTUD CONFIRMATION_TIMER while in the SEARCH_COMPLETE state; see
[Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\].[¶](#section-14.3.2-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#handling-of-icmp-messages-by-dplpmtud}
::: {#section-14.3.3 .section}
#### [14.3.3.](#section-14.3.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Handling of ICMP Messages by DPLPMTUD](#name-handling-of-icmp-messages-by){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handling-of-icmp-messages-by}

An endpoint using DPLPMTUD requires the validation of any received ICMP
PTB message before using the PTB information, as defined in [Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]. In addition to UDP port validation,
QUIC validates an ICMP message by using other PL information (e.g.,
validation of connection IDs in the quoted packet of any received ICMP
message).[¶](#section-14.3.3-1){.pilcrow}

The considerations for processing ICMP messages described in [Section
14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref} also apply if these messages are used by
DPLPMTUD.[¶](#section-14.3.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#sending-quic-pmtu-probes}
::: {#section-14.4 .section}
### [14.4.](#section-14.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Sending QUIC PMTU Probes](#name-sending-quic-pmtu-probes){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sending-quic-pmtu-probes}

PMTU probes are ack-eliciting packets.[¶](#section-14.4-1){.pilcrow}

Endpoints could limit the content of PMTU probes to PING and PADDING
frames, since packets that are larger than the current maximum datagram
size are more likely to be dropped by the network. Loss of a QUIC packet
that is carried in a PMTU probe is therefore not a reliable indication
of congestion and [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} trigger a congestion control
reaction; see Item 7 in [Section
3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8899#section-3){.relref} of
\[[DPLPMTUD](#DPLPMTUD){.xref}\]. However, PMTU probes consume
congestion window, which could delay subsequent transmission by an
application.[¶](#section-14.4-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#pmtu-probes-src-cid}
::: {#section-14.4.1 .section}
#### [14.4.1.](#section-14.4.1){.section-number .selfRef} [PMTU Probes Containing Source Connection ID](#name-pmtu-probes-containing-sour){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-pmtu-probes-containing-sour}

Endpoints that rely on the Destination Connection ID field for routing
incoming QUIC packets are likely to require that the connection ID be
included in PMTU probes to route any resulting ICMP messages ([Section
14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref}) back to the correct endpoint. However, only long
header packets ([Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}) contain the Source
Connection ID field, and long header packets are not decrypted or
acknowledged by the peer once the handshake is
complete.[¶](#section-14.4.1-1){.pilcrow}

One way to construct a PMTU probe is to coalesce (see [Section
12.2](#packet-coalesce){.xref}) a packet with a long header, such as a
Handshake or 0-RTT packet ([Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}), with a
short header packet in a single UDP datagram. If the resulting PMTU
probe reaches the endpoint, the packet with the long header will be
ignored, but the short header packet will be acknowledged. If the PMTU
probe causes an ICMP message to be sent, the first part of the probe
will be quoted in that message. If the Source Connection ID field is
within the quoted portion of the probe, that could be used for routing
or validation of the ICMP message.[¶](#section-14.4.1-2){.pilcrow}

Note: The purpose of using a packet with a long header is only to ensure
that the quoted packet contained in the ICMP message contains a Source
Connection ID field. This packet does not need to be a valid packet, and
it can be sent even if there is no current use for packets of that
type.[¶](#section-14.4.1-3.1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#versions}
::: {#section-15 .section}
## [15.](#section-15){.section-number .selfRef} [Versions](#name-versions){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-versions}

QUIC versions are identified using a 32-bit unsigned
number.[¶](#section-15-1){.pilcrow}

The version 0x00000000 is reserved to represent version negotiation.
This version of the specification is identified by the number
0x00000001.[¶](#section-15-2){.pilcrow}

Other versions of QUIC might have different properties from this
version. The properties of QUIC that are guaranteed to be consistent
across all versions of the protocol are described in
\[[QUIC-INVARIANTS](#QUIC-INVARIANTS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-15-3){.pilcrow}

Version 0x00000001 of QUIC uses TLS as a cryptographic handshake
protocol, as described in
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-15-4){.pilcrow}

Versions with the most significant 16 bits of the version number cleared
are reserved for use in future IETF consensus
documents.[¶](#section-15-5){.pilcrow}

Versions that follow the pattern 0x?a?a?a?a are reserved for use in
forcing version negotiation to be exercised \-- that is, any version
number where the low four bits of all bytes is 1010 (in binary). A
client or server [MAY]{.bcp14} advertise support for any of these
reserved versions.[¶](#section-15-6){.pilcrow}

Reserved version numbers will never represent a real protocol; a client
[MAY]{.bcp14} use one of these version numbers with the expectation that
the server will initiate version negotiation; a server [MAY]{.bcp14}
advertise support for one of these versions and can expect that clients
ignore the value.[¶](#section-15-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#integer-encoding}
::: {#section-16 .section}
## [16.](#section-16){.section-number .selfRef} [Variable-Length Integer Encoding](#name-variable-length-integer-enc){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-variable-length-integer-enc}

QUIC packets and frames commonly use a variable-length encoding for
non-negative integer values. This encoding ensures that smaller integer
values need fewer bytes to encode.[¶](#section-16-1){.pilcrow}

The QUIC variable-length integer encoding reserves the two most
significant bits of the first byte to encode the base-2 logarithm of the
integer encoding length in bytes. The integer value is encoded on the
remaining bits, in network byte order.[¶](#section-16-2){.pilcrow}

This means that integers are encoded on 1, 2, 4, or 8 bytes and can
encode 6-, 14-, 30-, or 62-bit values, respectively. [Table
4](#integer-summary){.xref} summarizes the encoding
properties.[¶](#section-16-3){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-summary-of-integer-encoding}

::: {#integer-summary}
  2MSB   Length   Usable Bits   Range
  ------ -------- ------------- -----------------------
  00     1        6             0-63
  01     2        14            0-16383
  10     4        30            0-1073741823
  11     8        62            0-4611686018427387903

  : [Table 4](#table-4){.selfRef}: [Summary of Integer
  Encodings](#name-summary-of-integer-encoding){.selfRef}
:::

An example of a decoding algorithm and sample encodings are shown in
[Appendix A.1](#sample-varint){.xref}.[¶](#section-16-5){.pilcrow}

Values do not need to be encoded on the minimum number of bytes
necessary, with the sole exception of the Frame Type field; see [Section
12.4](#frames){.xref}.[¶](#section-16-6){.pilcrow}

Versions ([Section 15](#versions){.xref}), packet numbers sent in the
header ([Section 17.1](#packet-encoding){.xref}), and the length of
connection IDs in long header packets ([Section
17.2](#long-header){.xref}) are described using integers but do not use
this encoding.[¶](#section-16-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-formats}
::: {#section-17 .section}
## [17.](#section-17){.section-number .selfRef} [Packet Formats](#name-packet-formats){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packet-formats}

All numeric values are encoded in network byte order (that is, big
endian), and all field sizes are in bits. Hexadecimal notation is used
for describing the value of fields.[¶](#section-17-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#packet-encoding}
::: {#section-17.1 .section}
### [17.1.](#section-17.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Packet Number Encoding and Decoding](#name-packet-number-encoding-and-){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-packet-number-encoding-and-}

Packet numbers are integers in the range 0 to 2^62^-1 ([Section
12.3](#packet-numbers){.xref}). When present in long or short packet
headers, they are encoded in 1 to 4 bytes. The number of bits required
to represent the packet number is reduced by including only the least
significant bits of the packet number.[¶](#section-17.1-1){.pilcrow}

The encoded packet number is protected as described in [Section
5.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.4){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.1-2){.pilcrow}

Prior to receiving an acknowledgment for a packet number space, the full
packet number [MUST]{.bcp14} be included; it is not to be truncated, as
described below.[¶](#section-17.1-3){.pilcrow}

After an acknowledgment is received for a packet number space, the
sender [MUST]{.bcp14} use a packet number size able to represent more
than twice as large a range as the difference between the largest
acknowledged packet number and the packet number being sent. A peer
receiving the packet will then correctly decode the packet number,
unless the packet is delayed in transit such that it arrives after many
higher-numbered packets have been received. An endpoint [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
use a large enough packet number encoding to allow the packet number to
be recovered even if the packet arrives after packets that are sent
afterwards.[¶](#section-17.1-4){.pilcrow}

As a result, the size of the packet number encoding is at least one bit
more than the base-2 logarithm of the number of contiguous
unacknowledged packet numbers, including the new packet. Pseudocode and
an example for packet number encoding can be found in [Appendix
A.2](#sample-packet-number-encoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.1-5){.pilcrow}

At a receiver, protection of the packet number is removed prior to
recovering the full packet number. The full packet number is then
reconstructed based on the number of significant bits present, the value
of those bits, and the largest packet number received in a successfully
authenticated packet. Recovering the full packet number is necessary to
successfully complete the removal of packet
protection.[¶](#section-17.1-6){.pilcrow}

Once header protection is removed, the packet number is decoded by
finding the packet number value that is closest to the next expected
packet. The next expected packet is the highest received packet number
plus one. Pseudocode and an example for packet number decoding can be
found in [Appendix
A.3](#sample-packet-number-decoding){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.1-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#long-header}
::: {#section-17.2 .section}
### [17.2.](#section-17.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Long Header Packets](#name-long-header-packets){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-long-header-packets}

[]{#name-long-header-packet-format}

::: {#fig-long-header}
::: {#section-17.2-1.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Long Header Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Fixed Bit (1) = 1,
      Long Packet Type (2),
      Type-Specific Bits (4),
      Version (32),
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..160),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..160),
      Type-Specific Payload (..),
    }
:::

[Figure 13](#figure-13){.selfRef}: [Long Header Packet
Format](#name-long-header-packet-format){.selfRef}
:::

Long headers are used for packets that are sent prior to the
establishment of 1-RTT keys. Once 1-RTT keys are available, a sender
switches to sending packets using the short header ([Section
17.3](#short-header){.xref}). The long form allows for special packets
\-- such as the Version Negotiation packet \-- to be represented in this
uniform fixed-length packet format. Packets that use the long header
contain the following fields:[¶](#section-17.2-2){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Header Form:

:   The most significant bit (0x80) of byte 0 (the first byte) is set to
    1 for long headers.[¶](#section-17.2-3.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Fixed Bit:

:   The next bit (0x40) of byte 0 is set to 1, unless the packet is a
    Version Negotiation packet. Packets containing a zero value for this
    bit are not valid packets in this version and [MUST]{.bcp14} be
    discarded. A value of 1 for this bit allows QUIC to coexist with
    other protocols; see
    \[[RFC7983](#RFC7983){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2-3.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Long Packet Type:

:   The next two bits (those with a mask of 0x30) of byte 0 contain a
    packet type. Packet types are listed in [Table
    5](#long-packet-types){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2-3.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Type-Specific Bits:

:   The semantics of the lower four bits (those with a mask of 0x0f) of
    byte 0 are determined by the packet
    type.[¶](#section-17.2-3.8.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Version:

:   The QUIC Version is a 32-bit field that follows the first byte. This
    field indicates the version of QUIC that is in use and determines
    how the rest of the protocol fields are
    interpreted.[¶](#section-17.2-3.10.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Destination Connection ID Length:

:   The byte following the version contains the length in bytes of the
    Destination Connection ID field that follows it. This length is
    encoded as an 8-bit unsigned integer. In QUIC version 1, this value
    [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} exceed 20 bytes. Endpoints that receive a version
    1 long header with a value larger than 20 [MUST]{.bcp14} drop the
    packet. In order to properly form a Version Negotiation packet,
    servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be able to read longer connection IDs from
    other QUIC versions.[¶](#section-17.2-3.12.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Destination Connection ID:

:   The Destination Connection ID field follows the Destination
    Connection ID Length field, which indicates the length of this
    field. [Section 7.2](#negotiating-connection-ids){.xref} describes
    the use of this field in more
    detail.[¶](#section-17.2-3.14.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Source Connection ID Length:

:   The byte following the Destination Connection ID contains the length
    in bytes of the Source Connection ID field that follows it. This
    length is encoded as an 8-bit unsigned integer. In QUIC version 1,
    this value [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} exceed 20 bytes. Endpoints that
    receive a version 1 long header with a value larger than 20
    [MUST]{.bcp14} drop the packet. In order to properly form a Version
    Negotiation packet, servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be able to read longer
    connection IDs from other QUIC
    versions.[¶](#section-17.2-3.16.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Source Connection ID:

:   The Source Connection ID field follows the Source Connection ID
    Length field, which indicates the length of this field. [Section
    7.2](#negotiating-connection-ids){.xref} describes the use of this
    field in more detail.[¶](#section-17.2-3.18.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Type-Specific Payload:

:   The remainder of the packet, if any, is type
    specific.[¶](#section-17.2-3.20.1){.pilcrow}

:   

In this version of QUIC, the following packet types with the long header
are defined:[¶](#section-17.2-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-long-header-packet-types}

::: {#long-packet-types}
  Type   Name        Section
  ------ ----------- --------------------------------------------
  0x00   Initial     [Section 17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}
  0x01   0-RTT       [Section 17.2.3](#packet-0rtt){.xref}
  0x02   Handshake   [Section 17.2.4](#packet-handshake){.xref}
  0x03   Retry       [Section 17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}

  : [Table 5](#table-5){.selfRef}: [Long Header Packet
  Types](#name-long-header-packet-types){.selfRef}
:::

The header form bit, Destination and Source Connection ID lengths,
Destination and Source Connection ID fields, and Version fields of a
long header packet are version independent. The other fields in the
first byte are version specific. See
\[[QUIC-INVARIANTS](#QUIC-INVARIANTS){.xref}\] for details on how
packets from different versions of QUIC are
interpreted.[¶](#section-17.2-6){.pilcrow}

The interpretation of the fields and the payload are specific to a
version and packet type. While type-specific semantics for this version
are described in the following sections, several long header packets in
this version of QUIC contain these additional
fields:[¶](#section-17.2-7){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Reserved Bits:

:   Two bits (those with a mask of 0x0c) of byte 0 are reserved across
    multiple packet types. These bits are protected using header
    protection; see [Section
    5.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.4){.relref} of
    \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]. The value included prior to
    protection [MUST]{.bcp14} be set to 0. An endpoint [MUST]{.bcp14}
    treat receipt of a packet that has a non-zero value for these bits
    after removing both packet and header protection as a connection
    error of type PROTOCOL_VIOLATION. Discarding such a packet after
    only removing header protection can expose the endpoint to attacks;
    see [Section
    9.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-9.5){.relref} of
    \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2-8.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Packet Number Length:

:   In packet types that contain a Packet Number field, the least
    significant two bits (those with a mask of 0x03) of byte 0 contain
    the length of the Packet Number field, encoded as an unsigned
    two-bit integer that is one less than the length of the Packet
    Number field in bytes. That is, the length of the Packet Number
    field is the value of this field plus one. These bits are protected
    using header protection; see [Section
    5.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.4){.relref} of
    \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2-8.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Length:

:   This is the length of the remainder of the packet (that is, the
    Packet Number and Payload fields) in bytes, encoded as a
    variable-length integer ([Section
    16](#integer-encoding){.xref}).[¶](#section-17.2-8.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Packet Number:

:   This field is 1 to 4 bytes long. The packet number is protected
    using header protection; see [Section
    5.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.4){.relref} of
    \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]. The length of the Packet Number
    field is encoded in the Packet Number Length bits of byte 0; see
    above.[¶](#section-17.2-8.8.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Packet Payload:

:   This is the payload of the packet \-- containing a sequence of
    frames \-- that is protected using packet
    protection.[¶](#section-17.2-8.10.1){.pilcrow}

:   

::: {#packet-version}
::: {#section-17.2.1 .section}
#### [17.2.1.](#section-17.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Version Negotiation Packet](#name-version-negotiation-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-version-negotiation-packet}

A Version Negotiation packet is inherently not version specific. Upon
receipt by a client, it will be identified as a Version Negotiation
packet based on the Version field having a value of
0.[¶](#section-17.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

The Version Negotiation packet is a response to a client packet that
contains a version that is not supported by the server. It is only sent
by servers.[¶](#section-17.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

The layout of a Version Negotiation packet
is:[¶](#section-17.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-version-negotiation-packet-2}

::: {#version-negotiation-format}
::: {#section-17.2.1-4.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Version Negotiation Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Unused (7),
      Version (32) = 0,
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..2040),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..2040),
      Supported Version (32) ...,
    }
:::

[Figure 14](#figure-14){.selfRef}: [Version Negotiation
Packet](#name-version-negotiation-packet-2){.selfRef}
:::

The value in the Unused field is set to an arbitrary value by the
server. Clients [MUST]{.bcp14} ignore the value of this field. Where
QUIC might be multiplexed with other protocols (see
\[[RFC7983](#RFC7983){.xref}\]), servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} set the most
significant bit of this field (0x40) to 1 so that Version Negotiation
packets appear to have the Fixed Bit field. Note that other versions of
QUIC might not make a similar
recommendation.[¶](#section-17.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

The Version field of a Version Negotiation packet [MUST]{.bcp14} be set
to 0x00000000.[¶](#section-17.2.1-6){.pilcrow}

The server [MUST]{.bcp14} include the value from the Source Connection
ID field of the packet it receives in the Destination Connection ID
field. The value for Source Connection ID [MUST]{.bcp14} be copied from
the Destination Connection ID of the received packet, which is initially
randomly selected by a client. Echoing both connection IDs gives clients
some assurance that the server received the packet and that the Version
Negotiation packet was not generated by an entity that did not observe
the Initial packet.[¶](#section-17.2.1-7){.pilcrow}

Future versions of QUIC could have different requirements for the
lengths of connection IDs. In particular, connection IDs might have a
smaller minimum length or a greater maximum length. Version-specific
rules for the connection ID therefore [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} influence a
decision about whether to send a Version Negotiation
packet.[¶](#section-17.2.1-8){.pilcrow}

The remainder of the Version Negotiation packet is a list of 32-bit
versions that the server supports.[¶](#section-17.2.1-9){.pilcrow}

A Version Negotiation packet is not acknowledged. It is only sent in
response to a packet that indicates an unsupported version; see [Section
5.2.2](#server-pkt-handling){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.1-10){.pilcrow}

The Version Negotiation packet does not include the Packet Number and
Length fields present in other packets that use the long header form.
Consequently, a Version Negotiation packet consumes an entire UDP
datagram.[¶](#section-17.2.1-11){.pilcrow}

A server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send more than one Version Negotiation
packet in response to a single UDP
datagram.[¶](#section-17.2.1-12){.pilcrow}

See [Section 6](#version-negotiation){.xref} for a description of the
version negotiation process.[¶](#section-17.2.1-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-initial}
::: {#section-17.2.2 .section}
#### [17.2.2.](#section-17.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Initial Packet](#name-initial-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-initial-packet}

An Initial packet uses long headers with a type value of 0x00. It
carries the first CRYPTO frames sent by the client and server to perform
key exchange, and it carries ACK frames in either
direction.[¶](#section-17.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-initial-packet-2}

::: {#initial-format}
::: {#section-17.2.2-2.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Initial Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Fixed Bit (1) = 1,
      Long Packet Type (2) = 0,
      Reserved Bits (2),
      Packet Number Length (2),
      Version (32),
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..160),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..160),
      Token Length (i),
      Token (..),
      Length (i),
      Packet Number (8..32),
      Packet Payload (8..),
    }
:::

[Figure 15](#figure-15){.selfRef}: [Initial
Packet](#name-initial-packet-2){.selfRef}
:::

The Initial packet contains a long header as well as the Length and
Packet Number fields; see [Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}. The first
byte contains the Reserved and Packet Number Length bits; see also
[Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}. Between the Source Connection ID
and Length fields, there are two additional fields specific to the
Initial packet.[¶](#section-17.2.2-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Token Length:

:   A variable-length integer specifying the length of the Token field,
    in bytes. This value is 0 if no token is present. Initial packets
    sent by the server [MUST]{.bcp14} set the Token Length field to 0;
    clients that receive an Initial packet with a non-zero Token Length
    field [MUST]{.bcp14} either discard the packet or generate a
    connection error of type
    PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-17.2.2-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Token:

:   The value of the token that was previously provided in a Retry
    packet or NEW_TOKEN frame; see [Section
    8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.2-4.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

In order to prevent tampering by version-unaware middleboxes, Initial
packets are protected with connection- and version-specific keys
(Initial keys) as described in \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]. This
protection does not provide confidentiality or integrity against
attackers that can observe packets, but it does prevent attackers that
cannot observe packets from spoofing Initial
packets.[¶](#section-17.2.2-5){.pilcrow}

The client and server use the Initial packet type for any packet that
contains an initial cryptographic handshake message. This includes all
cases where a new packet containing the initial cryptographic message
needs to be created, such as the packets sent after receiving a Retry
packet; see [Section
17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.2-6){.pilcrow}

A server sends its first Initial packet in response to a client Initial.
A server [MAY]{.bcp14} send multiple Initial packets. The cryptographic
key exchange could require multiple round trips or retransmissions of
this data.[¶](#section-17.2.2-7){.pilcrow}

The payload of an Initial packet includes a CRYPTO frame (or frames)
containing a cryptographic handshake message, ACK frames, or both. PING,
PADDING, and CONNECTION_CLOSE frames of type 0x1c are also permitted. An
endpoint that receives an Initial packet containing other frames can
either discard the packet as spurious or treat it as a connection
error.[¶](#section-17.2.2-8){.pilcrow}

The first packet sent by a client always includes a CRYPTO frame that
contains the start or all of the first cryptographic handshake message.
The first CRYPTO frame sent always begins at an offset of 0; see
[Section 7](#handshake){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.2-9){.pilcrow}

Note that if the server sends a TLS HelloRetryRequest (see [Section
4.7](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.7){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]), the client will send another series
of Initial packets. These Initial packets will continue the
cryptographic handshake and will contain CRYPTO frames starting at an
offset matching the size of the CRYPTO frames sent in the first flight
of Initial packets.[¶](#section-17.2.2-10){.pilcrow}

::: {#discard-initial}
::: {#section-17.2.2.1 .section}
##### [17.2.2.1.](#section-17.2.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Abandoning Initial Packets](#name-abandoning-initial-packets){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-abandoning-initial-packets}

A client stops both sending and processing Initial packets when it sends
its first Handshake packet. A server stops sending and processing
Initial packets when it receives its first Handshake packet. Though
packets might still be in flight or awaiting acknowledgment, no further
Initial packets need to be exchanged beyond this point. Initial packet
protection keys are discarded (see [Section
4.9.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.9.1){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]) along with any loss recovery and
congestion control state; see [Section
6.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9002#section-6.4){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-RECOVERY](#QUIC-RECOVERY){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

Any data in CRYPTO frames is discarded \-- and no longer retransmitted
\-- when Initial keys are discarded.[¶](#section-17.2.2.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#packet-0rtt}
::: {#section-17.2.3 .section}
#### [17.2.3.](#section-17.2.3){.section-number .selfRef} [0-RTT](#name-0-rtt){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-0-rtt}

A 0-RTT packet uses long headers with a type value of 0x01, followed by
the Length and Packet Number fields; see [Section
17.2](#long-header){.xref}. The first byte contains the Reserved and
Packet Number Length bits; see [Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}. A
0-RTT packet is used to carry \"early\" data from the client to the
server as part of the first flight, prior to handshake completion. As
part of the TLS handshake, the server can accept or reject this early
data.[¶](#section-17.2.3-1){.pilcrow}

See [Section
2.3](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8446#section-2.3){.relref} of
\[[TLS13](#TLS13){.xref}\] for a discussion of 0-RTT data and its
limitations.[¶](#section-17.2.3-2){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-0-rtt-packet}

::: {#section-17.2.3-3.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    0-RTT Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Fixed Bit (1) = 1,
      Long Packet Type (2) = 1,
      Reserved Bits (2),
      Packet Number Length (2),
      Version (32),
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..160),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..160),
      Length (i),
      Packet Number (8..32),
      Packet Payload (8..),
    }
:::

[Figure 16](#figure-16){.selfRef}: [0-RTT
Packet](#name-0-rtt-packet){.selfRef}

Packet numbers for 0-RTT protected packets use the same space as 1-RTT
protected packets.[¶](#section-17.2.3-4){.pilcrow}

After a client receives a Retry packet, 0-RTT packets are likely to have
been lost or discarded by the server. A client [SHOULD]{.bcp14} attempt
to resend data in 0-RTT packets after it sends a new Initial packet. New
packet numbers [MUST]{.bcp14} be used for any new packets that are sent;
as described in [Section 17.2.5.3](#retry-continue){.xref}, reusing
packet numbers could compromise packet
protection.[¶](#section-17.2.3-5){.pilcrow}

A client only receives acknowledgments for its 0-RTT packets once the
handshake is complete, as defined in [Section
4.1.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-4.1.1){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2.3-6){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send 0-RTT packets once it starts processing
1-RTT packets from the server. This means that 0-RTT packets cannot
contain any response to frames from 1-RTT packets. For instance, a
client cannot send an ACK frame in a 0-RTT packet, because that can only
acknowledge a 1-RTT packet. An acknowledgment for a 1-RTT packet
[MUST]{.bcp14} be carried in a 1-RTT
packet.[¶](#section-17.2.3-7){.pilcrow}

A server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} treat a violation of remembered limits
([Section 7.4.1](#zerortt-parameters){.xref}) as a connection error of
an appropriate type (for instance, a FLOW_CONTROL_ERROR for exceeding
stream data limits).[¶](#section-17.2.3-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-handshake}
::: {#section-17.2.4 .section}
#### [17.2.4.](#section-17.2.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Handshake Packet](#name-handshake-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handshake-packet}

A Handshake packet uses long headers with a type value of 0x02, followed
by the Length and Packet Number fields; see [Section
17.2](#long-header){.xref}. The first byte contains the Reserved and
Packet Number Length bits; see [Section 17.2](#long-header){.xref}. It
is used to carry cryptographic handshake messages and acknowledgments
from the server and client.[¶](#section-17.2.4-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-handshake-protected-packet}

::: {#handshake-format}
::: {#section-17.2.4-2.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Handshake Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Fixed Bit (1) = 1,
      Long Packet Type (2) = 2,
      Reserved Bits (2),
      Packet Number Length (2),
      Version (32),
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..160),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..160),
      Length (i),
      Packet Number (8..32),
      Packet Payload (8..),
    }
:::

[Figure 17](#figure-17){.selfRef}: [Handshake Protected
Packet](#name-handshake-protected-packet){.selfRef}
:::

Once a client has received a Handshake packet from a server, it uses
Handshake packets to send subsequent cryptographic handshake messages
and acknowledgments to the server.[¶](#section-17.2.4-3){.pilcrow}

The Destination Connection ID field in a Handshake packet contains a
connection ID that is chosen by the recipient of the packet; the Source
Connection ID includes the connection ID that the sender of the packet
wishes to use; see [Section
7.2](#negotiating-connection-ids){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.4-4){.pilcrow}

Handshake packets have their own packet number space, and thus the first
Handshake packet sent by a server contains a packet number of
0.[¶](#section-17.2.4-5){.pilcrow}

The payload of this packet contains CRYPTO frames and could contain
PING, PADDING, or ACK frames. Handshake packets [MAY]{.bcp14} contain
CONNECTION_CLOSE frames of type 0x1c. Endpoints [MUST]{.bcp14} treat
receipt of Handshake packets with other frames as a connection error of
type PROTOCOL_VIOLATION.[¶](#section-17.2.4-6){.pilcrow}

Like Initial packets (see [Section 17.2.2.1](#discard-initial){.xref}),
data in CRYPTO frames for Handshake packets is discarded \-- and no
longer retransmitted \-- when Handshake protection keys are
discarded.[¶](#section-17.2.4-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#packet-retry}
::: {#section-17.2.5 .section}
#### [17.2.5.](#section-17.2.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Retry Packet](#name-retry-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-retry-packet}

As shown in [Figure 18](#retry-format){.xref}, a Retry packet uses a
long packet header with a type value of 0x03. It carries an address
validation token created by the server. It is used by a server that
wishes to perform a retry; see [Section
8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.5-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-retry-packet-2}

::: {#retry-format}
::: {#section-17.2.5-2.1 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
    Retry Packet {
      Header Form (1) = 1,
      Fixed Bit (1) = 1,
      Long Packet Type (2) = 3,
      Unused (4),
      Version (32),
      Destination Connection ID Length (8),
      Destination Connection ID (0..160),
      Source Connection ID Length (8),
      Source Connection ID (0..160),
      Retry Token (..),
      Retry Integrity Tag (128),
    }
:::

[Figure 18](#figure-18){.selfRef}: [Retry
Packet](#name-retry-packet-2){.selfRef}
:::

A Retry packet does not contain any protected fields. The value in the
Unused field is set to an arbitrary value by the server; a client
[MUST]{.bcp14} ignore these bits. In addition to the fields from the
long header, it contains these additional
fields:[¶](#section-17.2.5-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Retry Token:

:   An opaque token that the server can use to validate the client\'s
    address.[¶](#section-17.2.5-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Retry Integrity Tag:

:   Defined in Section
    [5.8](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.8){.relref}
    ([\"Retry Packet
    Integrity\"](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.8){.relref})
    of
    \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\].[¶](#section-17.2.5-4.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

::: {#sending-a-retry-packet}
::: {#section-17.2.5.1 .section}
##### [17.2.5.1.](#section-17.2.5.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Sending a Retry Packet](#name-sending-a-retry-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sending-a-retry-packet}

The server populates the Destination Connection ID with the connection
ID that the client included in the Source Connection ID of the Initial
packet.[¶](#section-17.2.5.1-1){.pilcrow}

The server includes a connection ID of its choice in the Source
Connection ID field. This value [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be equal to the
Destination Connection ID field of the packet sent by the client. A
client [MUST]{.bcp14} discard a Retry packet that contains a Source
Connection ID field that is identical to the Destination Connection ID
field of its Initial packet. The client [MUST]{.bcp14} use the value
from the Source Connection ID field of the Retry packet in the
Destination Connection ID field of subsequent packets that it
sends.[¶](#section-17.2.5.1-2){.pilcrow}

A server [MAY]{.bcp14} send Retry packets in response to Initial and
0-RTT packets. A server can either discard or buffer 0-RTT packets that
it receives. A server can send multiple Retry packets as it receives
Initial or 0-RTT packets. A server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send more than one
Retry packet in response to a single UDP
datagram.[¶](#section-17.2.5.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#handling-a-retry-packet}
::: {#section-17.2.5.2 .section}
##### [17.2.5.2.](#section-17.2.5.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Handling a Retry Packet](#name-handling-a-retry-packet){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handling-a-retry-packet}

A client [MUST]{.bcp14} accept and process at most one Retry packet for
each connection attempt. After the client has received and processed an
Initial or Retry packet from the server, it [MUST]{.bcp14} discard any
subsequent Retry packets that it
receives.[¶](#section-17.2.5.2-1){.pilcrow}

Clients [MUST]{.bcp14} discard Retry packets that have a Retry Integrity
Tag that cannot be validated; see [Section
5.8](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.8){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]. This diminishes an attacker\'s ability
to inject a Retry packet and protects against accidental corruption of
Retry packets. A client [MUST]{.bcp14} discard a Retry packet with a
zero-length Retry Token field.[¶](#section-17.2.5.2-2){.pilcrow}

The client responds to a Retry packet with an Initial packet that
includes the provided Retry token to continue connection
establishment.[¶](#section-17.2.5.2-3){.pilcrow}

A client sets the Destination Connection ID field of this Initial packet
to the value from the Source Connection ID field in the Retry packet.
Changing the Destination Connection ID field also results in a change to
the keys used to protect the Initial packet. It also sets the Token
field to the token provided in the Retry packet. The client [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} change the Source Connection ID because the server could
include the connection ID as part of its token validation logic; see
[Section
8.1.4](#token-integrity){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.5.2-4){.pilcrow}

A Retry packet does not include a packet number and cannot be explicitly
acknowledged by a client.[¶](#section-17.2.5.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#retry-continue}
::: {#section-17.2.5.3 .section}
##### [17.2.5.3.](#section-17.2.5.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Continuing a Handshake after Retry](#name-continuing-a-handshake-afte){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-continuing-a-handshake-afte}

Subsequent Initial packets from the client include the connection ID and
token values from the Retry packet. The client copies the Source
Connection ID field from the Retry packet to the Destination Connection
ID field and uses this value until an Initial packet with an updated
value is received; see [Section
7.2](#negotiating-connection-ids){.xref}. The value of the Token field
is copied to all subsequent Initial packets; see [Section
8.1.2](#validate-retry){.xref}.[¶](#section-17.2.5.3-1){.pilcrow}

Other than updating the Destination Connection ID and Token fields, the
Initial packet sent by the client is subject to the same restrictions as
the first Initial packet. A client [MUST]{.bcp14} use the same
cryptographic handshake message it included in this packet. A server
[MAY]{.bcp14} treat a packet that contains a different cryptographic
handshake message as a connection error or discard it. Note that
including a Token field reduces the available space for the
cryptographic handshake message, which might result in the client
needing to send multiple Initial
packets.[¶](#section-17.2.5.3-2){.pilcrow}

A client [MAY]{.bcp14} attempt 0-RTT after receiving a Retry packet by
sending 0-RTT packets to the connection ID provided by the
server.[¶](#section-17.2.5.3-3){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} reset the packet number for any packet
number space after processing a Retry packet. In particular, 0-RTT
packets contain confidential information that will most likely be
retransmitted on receiving a Retry packet. The keys used to protect
these new 0-RTT packets will not change as a result of responding to a
Retry packet. However, the data sent in these packets could be different
than what was sent earlier. Sending these new packets with the same
packet number is likely to compromise the packet protection for those
packets because the same key and nonce could be used to protect
