:::

::: {#app-error-codes}
::: {#section-20.2 .section}
### [20.2.](#section-20.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Application Protocol Error Codes](#name-application-protocol-error-){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-application-protocol-error-}

The management of application error codes is left to application
protocols. Application protocol error codes are used for the
RESET_STREAM frame ([Section 19.4](#frame-reset-stream){.xref}), the
STOP_SENDING frame ([Section 19.5](#frame-stop-sending){.xref}), and the
CONNECTION_CLOSE frame with a type of 0x1d ([Section
19.19](#frame-connection-close){.xref}).[¶](#section-20.2-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#security-considerations}
::: {#section-21 .section}
## [21.](#section-21){.section-number .selfRef} [Security Considerations](#name-security-considerations){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-security-considerations}

The goal of QUIC is to provide a secure transport connection. [Section
21.1](#security-properties){.xref} provides an overview of those
properties; subsequent sections discuss constraints and caveats
regarding these properties, including descriptions of known attacks and
countermeasures.[¶](#section-21-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#security-properties}
::: {#section-21.1 .section}
### [21.1.](#section-21.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Overview of Security Properties](#name-overview-of-security-proper){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-overview-of-security-proper}

A complete security analysis of QUIC is outside the scope of this
document. This section provides an informal description of the desired
security properties as an aid to implementers and to help guide protocol
analysis.[¶](#section-21.1-1){.pilcrow}

QUIC assumes the threat model described in
\[[SEC-CONS](#SEC-CONS){.xref}\] and provides protections against many
of the attacks that arise from that model.[¶](#section-21.1-2){.pilcrow}

For this purpose, attacks are divided into passive and active attacks.
Passive attackers have the ability to read packets from the network,
while active attackers also have the ability to write packets into the
network. However, a passive attack could involve an attacker with the
ability to cause a routing change or other modification in the path
taken by packets that comprise a
connection.[¶](#section-21.1-3){.pilcrow}

Attackers are additionally categorized as either on-path attackers or
off-path attackers. An on-path attacker can read, modify, or remove any
packet it observes such that the packet no longer reaches its
destination, while an off-path attacker observes the packets but cannot
prevent the original packet from reaching its intended destination. Both
types of attackers can also transmit arbitrary packets. This definition
differs from that of [Section
3.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3552#section-3.5){.relref} of
\[[SEC-CONS](#SEC-CONS){.xref}\] in that an off-path attacker is able to
observe packets.[¶](#section-21.1-4){.pilcrow}

Properties of the handshake, protected packets, and connection migration
are considered separately.[¶](#section-21.1-5){.pilcrow}

::: {#handshake-properties}
::: {#section-21.1.1 .section}
#### [21.1.1.](#section-21.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Handshake](#name-handshake){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handshake}

The QUIC handshake incorporates the TLS 1.3 handshake and inherits the
cryptographic properties described in [Appendix
E.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8446#appendix-E.1){.relref} of
\[[TLS13](#TLS13){.xref}\]. Many of the security properties of QUIC
depend on the TLS handshake providing these properties. Any attack on
the TLS handshake could affect QUIC.[¶](#section-21.1.1-1){.pilcrow}

Any attack on the TLS handshake that compromises the secrecy or
uniqueness of session keys, or the authentication of the participating
peers, affects other security guarantees provided by QUIC that depend on
those keys. For instance, migration ([Section 9](#migration){.xref})
depends on the efficacy of confidentiality protections, both for the
negotiation of keys using the TLS handshake and for QUIC packet
protection, to avoid linkability across network
paths.[¶](#section-21.1.1-2){.pilcrow}

An attack on the integrity of the TLS handshake might allow an attacker
to affect the selection of application protocol or QUIC
version.[¶](#section-21.1.1-3){.pilcrow}

In addition to the properties provided by TLS, the QUIC handshake
provides some defense against DoS attacks on the
handshake.[¶](#section-21.1.1-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#anti-amplification}
::: {#section-21.1.1.1 .section}
##### [21.1.1.1.](#section-21.1.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Anti-Amplification](#name-anti-amplification){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-anti-amplification}

Address validation ([Section 8](#address-validation){.xref}) is used to
verify that an entity that claims a given address is able to receive
packets at that address. Address validation limits amplification attack
targets to addresses for which an attacker can observe
packets.[¶](#section-21.1.1.1-1){.pilcrow}

Prior to address validation, endpoints are limited in what they are able
to send. Endpoints cannot send data toward an unvalidated address in
excess of three times the data received from that
address.[¶](#section-21.1.1.1-2){.pilcrow}

Note: The anti-amplification limit only applies when an endpoint
responds to packets received from an unvalidated address. The
anti-amplification limit does not apply to clients when establishing a
new connection or when initiating connection
migration.[¶](#section-21.1.1.1-3.1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#server-side-dos}
::: {#section-21.1.1.2 .section}
##### [21.1.1.2.](#section-21.1.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Server-Side DoS](#name-server-side-dos){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-side-dos}

Computing the server\'s first flight for a full handshake is potentially
expensive, requiring both a signature and a key exchange computation. In
order to prevent computational DoS attacks, the Retry packet provides a
cheap token exchange mechanism that allows servers to validate a
client\'s IP address prior to doing any expensive computations at the
cost of a single round trip. After a successful handshake, servers can
issue new tokens to a client, which will allow new connection
establishment without incurring this
cost.[¶](#section-21.1.1.2-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#on-path-handshake-termination}
::: {#section-21.1.1.3 .section}
##### [21.1.1.3.](#section-21.1.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [On-Path Handshake Termination](#name-on-path-handshake-terminati){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-on-path-handshake-terminati}

An on-path or off-path attacker can force a handshake to fail by
replacing or racing Initial packets. Once valid Initial packets have
been exchanged, subsequent Handshake packets are protected with the
Handshake keys, and an on-path attacker cannot force handshake failure
other than by dropping packets to cause endpoints to abandon the
attempt.[¶](#section-21.1.1.3-1){.pilcrow}

An on-path attacker can also replace the addresses of packets on either
side and therefore cause the client or server to have an incorrect view
of the remote addresses. Such an attack is indistinguishable from the
functions performed by a NAT.[¶](#section-21.1.1.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#parameter-negotiation}
::: {#section-21.1.1.4 .section}
##### [21.1.1.4.](#section-21.1.1.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Parameter Negotiation](#name-parameter-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-parameter-negotiation}

The entire handshake is cryptographically protected, with the Initial
packets being encrypted with per-version keys and the Handshake and
later packets being encrypted with keys derived from the TLS key
exchange. Further, parameter negotiation is folded into the TLS
transcript and thus provides the same integrity guarantees as ordinary
TLS negotiation. An attacker can observe the client\'s transport
parameters (as long as it knows the version-specific salt) but cannot
observe the server\'s transport parameters and cannot influence
parameter negotiation.[¶](#section-21.1.1.4-1){.pilcrow}

Connection IDs are unencrypted but integrity protected in all
packets.[¶](#section-21.1.1.4-2){.pilcrow}

This version of QUIC does not incorporate a version negotiation
mechanism; implementations of incompatible versions will simply fail to
establish a connection.[¶](#section-21.1.1.4-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#protected-packet-properties}
::: {#section-21.1.2 .section}
#### [21.1.2.](#section-21.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Protected Packets](#name-protected-packets-2){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-protected-packets-2}

Packet protection ([Section 12.1](#packet-protected){.xref}) applies
authenticated encryption to all packets except Version Negotiation
packets, though Initial and Retry packets have limited protection due to
the use of version-specific keying material; see
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\] for more details. This section
considers passive and active attacks against protected
packets.[¶](#section-21.1.2-1){.pilcrow}

Both on-path and off-path attackers can mount a passive attack in which
they save observed packets for an offline attack against packet
protection at a future time; this is true for any observer of any packet
on any network.[¶](#section-21.1.2-2){.pilcrow}

An attacker that injects packets without being able to observe valid
packets for a connection is unlikely to be successful, since packet
protection ensures that valid packets are only generated by endpoints
that possess the key material established during the handshake; see
Sections [7](#handshake){.xref} and
[21.1.1](#handshake-properties){.xref}. Similarly, any active attacker
that observes packets and attempts to insert new data or modify existing
data in those packets should not be able to generate packets deemed
valid by the receiving endpoint, other than Initial
packets.[¶](#section-21.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

A spoofing attack, in which an active attacker rewrites unprotected
parts of a packet that it forwards or injects, such as the source or
destination address, is only effective if the attacker can forward
packets to the original endpoint. Packet protection ensures that the
packet payloads can only be processed by the endpoints that completed
the handshake, and invalid packets are ignored by those
endpoints.[¶](#section-21.1.2-4){.pilcrow}

An attacker can also modify the boundaries between packets and UDP
datagrams, causing multiple packets to be coalesced into a single
datagram or splitting coalesced packets into multiple datagrams. Aside
from datagrams containing Initial packets, which require padding,
modification of how packets are arranged in datagrams has no functional
effect on a connection, although it might change some performance
characteristics.[¶](#section-21.1.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#migration-properties}
::: {#section-21.1.3 .section}
#### [21.1.3.](#section-21.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Connection Migration](#name-connection-migration-2){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-connection-migration-2}

Connection migration ([Section 9](#migration){.xref}) provides endpoints
with the ability to transition between IP addresses and ports on
multiple paths, using one path at a time for transmission and receipt of
non-probing frames. Path validation ([Section
8.2](#migrate-validate){.xref}) establishes that a peer is both willing
and able to receive packets sent on a particular path. This helps reduce
the effects of address spoofing by limiting the number of packets sent
to a spoofed address.[¶](#section-21.1.3-1){.pilcrow}

This section describes the intended security properties of connection
migration under various types of DoS
attacks.[¶](#section-21.1.3-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#on-path-active-attacks}
::: {#section-21.1.3.1 .section}
##### [21.1.3.1.](#section-21.1.3.1){.section-number .selfRef} [On-Path Active Attacks](#name-on-path-active-attacks){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-on-path-active-attacks}

An attacker that can cause a packet it observes to no longer reach its
intended destination is considered an on-path attacker. When an attacker
is present between a client and server, endpoints are required to send
packets through the attacker to establish connectivity on a given
path.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-1){.pilcrow}

An on-path attacker can:[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-2){.pilcrow}

-   [Inspect
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.1}
-   [Modify IP and UDP packet
    headers[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.2}
-   [Inject new
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.3}
-   [Delay
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.4}
-   [Reorder
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.5}
-   [Drop
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.6){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.6}
-   [Split and merge datagrams along packet
    boundaries[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-3.7){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-3.7}

An on-path attacker cannot:[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-4){.pilcrow}

-   [Modify an authenticated portion of a packet and cause the recipient
    to accept that
    packet[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-5.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-5.1}

An on-path attacker has the opportunity to modify the packets that it
observes; however, any modifications to an authenticated portion of a
packet will cause it to be dropped by the receiving endpoint as invalid,
as packet payloads are both authenticated and
encrypted.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-6){.pilcrow}

QUIC aims to constrain the capabilities of an on-path attacker as
follows:[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-7){.pilcrow}

1.  [An on-path attacker can prevent the use of a path for a connection,
    causing the connection to fail if it cannot use a different path
    that does not contain the attacker. This can be achieved by dropping
    all packets, modifying them so that they fail to decrypt, or other
    methods.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-8.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-8.1}
2.  [An on-path attacker can prevent migration to a new path for which
    the attacker is also on-path by causing path validation to fail on
    the new
    path.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-8.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-8.2}
3.  [An on-path attacker cannot prevent a client from migrating to a
    path for which the attacker is not
    on-path.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-8.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-8.3}
4.  [An on-path attacker can reduce the throughput of a connection by
    delaying packets or dropping
    them.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-8.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-8.4}
5.  [An on-path attacker cannot cause an endpoint to accept a packet for
    which it has modified an authenticated portion of that
    packet.[¶](#section-21.1.3.1-8.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.1-8.5}
:::
:::

::: {#off-path-active-attacks}
::: {#section-21.1.3.2 .section}
##### [21.1.3.2.](#section-21.1.3.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Off-Path Active Attacks](#name-off-path-active-attacks){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-off-path-active-attacks}

An off-path attacker is not directly on the path between a client and
server but could be able to obtain copies of some or all packets sent
between the client and the server. It is also able to send copies of
those packets to either endpoint.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-1){.pilcrow}

An off-path attacker can:[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-2){.pilcrow}

-   [Inspect
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-3.1}
-   [Inject new
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-3.2}
-   [Reorder injected
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-3.3}

An off-path attacker cannot:[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-4){.pilcrow}

-   [Modify packets sent by
    endpoints[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-5.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-5.1}
-   [Delay
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-5.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-5.2}
-   [Drop
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-5.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-5.3}
-   [Reorder original
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-5.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-5.4}

An off-path attacker can create modified copies of packets that it has
observed and inject those copies into the network, potentially with
spoofed source and destination
addresses.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-6){.pilcrow}

For the purposes of this discussion, it is assumed that an off-path
attacker has the ability to inject a modified copy of a packet into the
network that will reach the destination endpoint prior to the arrival of
the original packet observed by the attacker. In other words, an
attacker has the ability to consistently \"win\" a race with the
legitimate packets between the endpoints, potentially causing the
original packet to be ignored by the
recipient.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-7){.pilcrow}

It is also assumed that an attacker has the resources necessary to
affect NAT state. In particular, an attacker can cause an endpoint to
lose its NAT binding and then obtain the same port for use with its own
traffic.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-8){.pilcrow}

QUIC aims to constrain the capabilities of an off-path attacker as
follows:[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-9){.pilcrow}

1.  [An off-path attacker can race packets and attempt to become a
    \"limited\" on-path
    attacker.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.1}
2.  [An off-path attacker can cause path validation to succeed for
    forwarded packets with the source address listed as the off-path
    attacker as long as it can provide improved connectivity between the
    client and the
    server.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.2}
3.  [An off-path attacker cannot cause a connection to close once the
    handshake has
    completed.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.3}
4.  [An off-path attacker cannot cause migration to a new path to fail
    if it cannot observe the new
    path.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.4}
5.  [An off-path attacker can become a limited on-path attacker during
    migration to a new path for which it is also an off-path
    attacker.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.5){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.5}
6.  [An off-path attacker can become a limited on-path attacker by
    affecting shared NAT state such that it sends packets to the server
    from the same IP address and port that the client originally
    used.[¶](#section-21.1.3.2-10.6){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.2-10.6}
:::
:::

::: {#limited-on-path-active-attacks}
::: {#section-21.1.3.3 .section}
##### [21.1.3.3.](#section-21.1.3.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Limited On-Path Active Attacks](#name-limited-on-path-active-atta){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-limited-on-path-active-atta}

A limited on-path attacker is an off-path attacker that has offered
improved routing of packets by duplicating and forwarding original
packets between the server and the client, causing those packets to
arrive before the original copies such that the original packets are
dropped by the destination endpoint.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-1){.pilcrow}

A limited on-path attacker differs from an on-path attacker in that it
is not on the original path between endpoints, and therefore the
original packets sent by an endpoint are still reaching their
destination. This means that a future failure to route copied packets to
the destination faster than their original path will not prevent the
original packets from reaching the
destination.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-2){.pilcrow}

A limited on-path attacker can:[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-3){.pilcrow}

-   [Inspect
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-4.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-4.1}
-   [Inject new
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-4.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-4.2}
-   [Modify unencrypted packet
    headers[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-4.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-4.3}
-   [Reorder
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-4.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-4.4}

A limited on-path attacker cannot:[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-5){.pilcrow}

-   [Delay packets so that they arrive later than packets sent on the
    original
    path[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-6.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-6.1}
-   [Drop
    packets[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-6.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-6.2}
-   [Modify the authenticated and encrypted portion of a packet and
    cause the recipient to accept that
    packet[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-6.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-6.3}

A limited on-path attacker can only delay packets up to the point that
the original packets arrive before the duplicate packets, meaning that
it cannot offer routing with worse latency than the original path. If a
limited on-path attacker drops packets, the original copy will still
arrive at the destination endpoint.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-7){.pilcrow}

QUIC aims to constrain the capabilities of a limited off-path attacker
as follows:[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-8){.pilcrow}

1.  [A limited on-path attacker cannot cause a connection to close once
    the handshake has
    completed.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-9.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-9.1}
2.  [A limited on-path attacker cannot cause an idle connection to close
    if the client is first to resume
    activity.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-9.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-9.2}
3.  [A limited on-path attacker can cause an idle connection to be
    deemed lost if the server is the first to resume
    activity.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-9.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.1.3.3-9.3}

Note that these guarantees are the same guarantees provided for any NAT,
for the same reasons.[¶](#section-21.1.3.3-10){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#handshake-dos}
::: {#section-21.2 .section}
### [21.2.](#section-21.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Handshake Denial of Service](#name-handshake-denial-of-service){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-handshake-denial-of-service}

As an encrypted and authenticated transport, QUIC provides a range of
protections against denial of service. Once the cryptographic handshake
is complete, QUIC endpoints discard most packets that are not
authenticated, greatly limiting the ability of an attacker to interfere
with existing connections.[¶](#section-21.2-1){.pilcrow}

Once a connection is established, QUIC endpoints might accept some
unauthenticated ICMP packets (see [Section 14.2.1](#pmtud){.xref}), but
the use of these packets is extremely limited. The only other type of
packet that an endpoint might accept is a stateless reset ([Section
10.3](#stateless-reset){.xref}), which relies on the token being kept
secret until it is used.[¶](#section-21.2-2){.pilcrow}

During the creation of a connection, QUIC only provides protection
against attacks from off the network path. All QUIC packets contain
proof that the recipient saw a preceding packet from its
peer.[¶](#section-21.2-3){.pilcrow}

Addresses cannot change during the handshake, so endpoints can discard
packets that are received on a different network
path.[¶](#section-21.2-4){.pilcrow}

The Source and Destination Connection ID fields are the primary means of
protection against an off-path attack during the handshake; see [Section
8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref}. These are required to match those set
by a peer. Except for Initial and Stateless Resets, an endpoint only
accepts packets that include a Destination Connection ID field that
matches a value the endpoint previously chose. This is the only
protection offered for Version Negotiation
packets.[¶](#section-21.2-5){.pilcrow}

The Destination Connection ID field in an Initial packet is selected by
a client to be unpredictable, which serves an additional purpose. The
packets that carry the cryptographic handshake are protected with a key
that is derived from this connection ID and a salt specific to the QUIC
version. This allows endpoints to use the same process for
authenticating packets that they receive as they use after the
cryptographic handshake completes. Packets that cannot be authenticated
are discarded. Protecting packets in this fashion provides a strong
assurance that the sender of the packet saw the Initial packet and
understood it.[¶](#section-21.2-6){.pilcrow}

These protections are not intended to be effective against an attacker
that is able to receive QUIC packets prior to the connection being
established. Such an attacker can potentially send packets that will be
accepted by QUIC endpoints. This version of QUIC attempts to detect this
sort of attack, but it expects that endpoints will fail to establish a
connection rather than recovering. For the most part, the cryptographic
handshake protocol \[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\] is responsible for
detecting tampering during the handshake.[¶](#section-21.2-7){.pilcrow}

Endpoints are permitted to use other methods to detect and attempt to
recover from interference with the handshake. Invalid packets can be
identified and discarded using other methods, but no specific method is
mandated in this document.[¶](#section-21.2-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#amplification-attack}
::: {#section-21.3 .section}
### [21.3.](#section-21.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Amplification Attack](#name-amplification-attack){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-amplification-attack}

An attacker might be able to receive an address validation token
([Section 8](#address-validation){.xref}) from a server and then release
the IP address it used to acquire that token. At a later time, the
attacker can initiate a 0-RTT connection with a server by spoofing this
same address, which might now address a different (victim) endpoint. The
attacker can thus potentially cause the server to send an initial
congestion window\'s worth of data towards the
victim.[¶](#section-21.3-1){.pilcrow}

Servers [SHOULD]{.bcp14} provide mitigations for this attack by limiting
the usage and lifetime of address validation tokens; see [Section
8.1.3](#validate-future){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#optimistic-ack-attack}
::: {#section-21.4 .section}
### [21.4.](#section-21.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Optimistic ACK Attack](#name-optimistic-ack-attack){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-optimistic-ack-attack}

An endpoint that acknowledges packets it has not received might cause a
congestion controller to permit sending at rates beyond what the network
supports. An endpoint [MAY]{.bcp14} skip packet numbers when sending
packets to detect this behavior. An endpoint can then immediately close
the connection with a connection error of type PROTOCOL_VIOLATION; see
[Section 10.2](#immediate-close){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.4-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#request-forgery-attacks}
::: {#section-21.5 .section}
### [21.5.](#section-21.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Forgery Attacks](#name-request-forgery-attacks){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-forgery-attacks}

A request forgery attack occurs where an endpoint causes its peer to
issue a request towards a victim, with the request controlled by the
endpoint. Request forgery attacks aim to provide an attacker with access
to capabilities of its peer that might otherwise be unavailable to the
attacker. For a networking protocol, a request forgery attack is often
used to exploit any implicit authorization conferred on the peer by the
victim due to the peer\'s location in the
network.[¶](#section-21.5-1){.pilcrow}

For request forgery to be effective, an attacker needs to be able to
influence what packets the peer sends and where these packets are sent.
If an attacker can target a vulnerable service with a controlled
payload, that service might perform actions that are attributed to the
attacker\'s peer but are decided by the
attacker.[¶](#section-21.5-2){.pilcrow}

For example, cross-site request forgery \[[CSRF](#CSRF){.xref}\]
exploits on the Web cause a client to issue requests that include
authorization cookies \[[COOKIE](#COOKIE){.xref}\], allowing one site
access to information and actions that are intended to be restricted to
a different site.[¶](#section-21.5-3){.pilcrow}

As QUIC runs over UDP, the primary attack modality of concern is one
where an attacker can select the address to which its peer sends UDP
datagrams and can control some of the unprotected content of those
packets. As much of the data sent by QUIC endpoints is protected, this
includes control over ciphertext. An attack is successful if an attacker
can cause a peer to send a UDP datagram to a host that will perform some
action based on content in the datagram.[¶](#section-21.5-4){.pilcrow}

This section discusses ways in which QUIC might be used for request
forgery attacks.[¶](#section-21.5-5){.pilcrow}

This section also describes limited countermeasures that can be
implemented by QUIC endpoints. These mitigations can be employed
unilaterally by a QUIC implementation or deployment, without potential
targets for request forgery attacks taking action. However, these
countermeasures could be insufficient if UDP-based services do not
properly authorize requests.[¶](#section-21.5-6){.pilcrow}

Because the migration attack described in [Section
21.5.4](#request-forgery-with-spoofed-migration){.xref} is quite
powerful and does not have adequate countermeasures, QUIC server
implementations should assume that attackers can cause them to generate
arbitrary UDP payloads to arbitrary destinations. QUIC servers [SHOULD
NOT]{.bcp14} be deployed in networks that do not deploy ingress
filtering \[[BCP38](#BCP38){.xref}\] and also have inadequately secured
UDP endpoints.[¶](#section-21.5-7){.pilcrow}

Although it is not generally possible to ensure that clients are not
co-located with vulnerable endpoints, this version of QUIC does not
allow servers to migrate, thus preventing spoofed migration attacks on
clients. Any future extension that allows server migration
[MUST]{.bcp14} also define countermeasures for forgery
attacks.[¶](#section-21.5-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#control-options-for-endpoints}
::: {#section-21.5.1 .section}
#### [21.5.1.](#section-21.5.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Control Options for Endpoints](#name-control-options-for-endpoin){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-control-options-for-endpoin}

QUIC offers some opportunities for an attacker to influence or control
where its peer sends UDP datagrams:[¶](#section-21.5.1-1){.pilcrow}

-   [initial connection establishment ([Section 7](#handshake){.xref}),
    where a server is able to choose where a client sends datagrams \--
    for example, by populating DNS
    records;[¶](#section-21.5.1-2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.5.1-2.1}
-   [preferred addresses ([Section 9.6](#preferred-address){.xref}),
    where a server is able to choose where a client sends
    datagrams;[¶](#section-21.5.1-2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.5.1-2.2}
-   [spoofed connection migrations ([Section
    9.3.1](#address-spoofing){.xref}), where a client is able to use
    source address spoofing to select where a server sends subsequent
    datagrams;
    and[¶](#section-21.5.1-2.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.5.1-2.3}
-   [spoofed packets that cause a server to send a Version Negotiation
    packet ([Section
    21.5.5](#vn-spoofing){.xref}).[¶](#section-21.5.1-2.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-21.5.1-2.4}

In all cases, the attacker can cause its peer to send datagrams to a
victim that might not understand QUIC. That is, these packets are sent
by the peer prior to address validation; see [Section
8](#address-validation){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.5.1-3){.pilcrow}

Outside of the encrypted portion of packets, QUIC offers an endpoint
several options for controlling the content of UDP datagrams that its
peer sends. The Destination Connection ID field offers direct control
over bytes that appear early in packets sent by the peer; see [Section
5.1](#connection-id){.xref}. The Token field in Initial packets offers a
server control over other bytes of Initial packets; see [Section
17.2.2](#packet-initial){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.5.1-4){.pilcrow}

There are no measures in this version of QUIC to prevent indirect
control over the encrypted portions of packets. It is necessary to
assume that endpoints are able to control the contents of frames that a
peer sends, especially those frames that convey application data, such
as STREAM frames. Though this depends to some degree on details of the
application protocol, some control is possible in many protocol usage
contexts. As the attacker has access to packet protection keys, they are
likely to be capable of predicting how a peer will encrypt future
packets. Successful control over datagram content then only requires
that the attacker be able to predict the packet number and placement of
frames in packets with some amount of
reliability.[¶](#section-21.5.1-5){.pilcrow}

This section assumes that limiting control over datagram content is not
feasible. The focus of the mitigations in subsequent sections is on
limiting the ways in which datagrams that are sent prior to address
validation can be used for request
forgery.[¶](#section-21.5.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#request-forgery-with-client-initial-packets}
::: {#section-21.5.2 .section}
#### [21.5.2.](#section-21.5.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Forgery with Client Initial Packets](#name-request-forgery-with-client){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-forgery-with-client}

An attacker acting as a server can choose the IP address and port on
which it advertises its availability, so Initial packets from clients
are assumed to be available for use in this sort of attack. The address
validation implicit in the handshake ensures that \-- for a new
connection \-- a client will not send other types of packets to a
destination that does not understand QUIC or is not willing to accept a
QUIC connection.[¶](#section-21.5.2-1){.pilcrow}

Initial packet protection ([Section
5.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9001#section-5.2){.relref} of
\[[QUIC-TLS](#QUIC-TLS){.xref}\]) makes it difficult for servers to
control the content of Initial packets sent by clients. A client
choosing an unpredictable Destination Connection ID ensures that servers
are unable to control any of the encrypted portion of Initial packets
from clients.[¶](#section-21.5.2-2){.pilcrow}

However, the Token field is open to server control and does allow a
server to use clients to mount request forgery attacks. The use of
tokens provided with the NEW_TOKEN frame ([Section
8.1.3](#validate-future){.xref}) offers the only option for request
forgery during connection establishment.[¶](#section-21.5.2-3){.pilcrow}

Clients, however, are not obligated to use the NEW_TOKEN frame. Request
forgery attacks that rely on the Token field can be avoided if clients
send an empty Token field when the server address has changed from when
the NEW_TOKEN frame was received.[¶](#section-21.5.2-4){.pilcrow}

Clients could avoid using NEW_TOKEN if the server address changes.
However, not including a Token field could adversely affect performance.
Servers could rely on NEW_TOKEN to enable the sending of data in excess
of the three-times limit on sending data; see [Section
8.1](#validate-handshake){.xref}. In particular, this affects cases
where clients use 0-RTT to request data from
servers.[¶](#section-21.5.2-5){.pilcrow}

Sending a Retry packet ([Section 17.2.5](#packet-retry){.xref}) offers a
server the option to change the Token field. After sending a Retry, the
server can also control the Destination Connection ID field of
subsequent Initial packets from the client. This also might allow
indirect control over the encrypted content of Initial packets. However,
the exchange of a Retry packet validates the server\'s address, thereby
preventing the use of subsequent Initial packets for request
forgery.[¶](#section-21.5.2-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#forgery-spa}
::: {#section-21.5.3 .section}
#### [21.5.3.](#section-21.5.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Forgery with Preferred Addresses](#name-request-forgery-with-prefer){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-forgery-with-prefer}

Servers can specify a preferred address, which clients then migrate to
after confirming the handshake; see [Section
9.6](#preferred-address){.xref}. The Destination Connection ID field of
packets that the client sends to a preferred address can be used for
request forgery.[¶](#section-21.5.3-1){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send non-probing frames to a preferred
address prior to validating that address; see [Section
8](#address-validation){.xref}. This greatly reduces the options that a
server has to control the encrypted portion of
datagrams.[¶](#section-21.5.3-2){.pilcrow}

This document does not offer any additional countermeasures that are
specific to the use of preferred addresses and can be implemented by
endpoints. The generic measures described in [Section
21.5.6](#forgery-generic){.xref} could be used as further
mitigation.[¶](#section-21.5.3-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#request-forgery-with-spoofed-migration}
::: {#section-21.5.4 .section}
#### [21.5.4.](#section-21.5.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Forgery with Spoofed Migration](#name-request-forgery-with-spoofe){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-forgery-with-spoofe}

Clients are able to present a spoofed source address as part of an
apparent connection migration to cause a server to send datagrams to
that address.[¶](#section-21.5.4-1){.pilcrow}

The Destination Connection ID field in any packets that a server
subsequently sends to this spoofed address can be used for request
forgery. A client might also be able to influence the
ciphertext.[¶](#section-21.5.4-2){.pilcrow}

A server that only sends probing packets ([Section
9.1](#probing){.xref}) to an address prior to address validation
provides an attacker with only limited control over the encrypted
portion of datagrams. However, particularly for NAT rebinding, this can
adversely affect performance. If the server sends frames carrying
application data, an attacker might be able to control most of the
content of datagrams.[¶](#section-21.5.4-3){.pilcrow}

This document does not offer specific countermeasures that can be
implemented by endpoints, aside from the generic measures described in
[Section 21.5.6](#forgery-generic){.xref}. However, countermeasures for
address spoofing at the network level \-- in particular, ingress
filtering \[[BCP38](#BCP38){.xref}\] \-- are especially effective
against attacks that use spoofing and originate from an external
network.[¶](#section-21.5.4-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#vn-spoofing}
::: {#section-21.5.5 .section}
#### [21.5.5.](#section-21.5.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Forgery with Version Negotiation](#name-request-forgery-with-versio){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-forgery-with-versio}

Clients that are able to present a spoofed source address on a packet
can cause a server to send a Version Negotiation packet ([Section
17.2.1](#packet-version){.xref}) to that
address.[¶](#section-21.5.5-1){.pilcrow}

The absence of size restrictions on the connection ID fields for packets
of an unknown version increases the amount of data that the client
controls from the resulting datagram. The first byte of this packet is
not under client control and the next four bytes are zero, but the
client is able to control up to 512 bytes starting from the fifth
byte.[¶](#section-21.5.5-2){.pilcrow}

No specific countermeasures are provided for this attack, though generic
protections ([Section 21.5.6](#forgery-generic){.xref}) could apply. In
this case, ingress filtering \[[BCP38](#BCP38){.xref}\] is also
effective.[¶](#section-21.5.5-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#forgery-generic}
::: {#section-21.5.6 .section}
#### [21.5.6.](#section-21.5.6){.section-number .selfRef} [Generic Request Forgery Countermeasures](#name-generic-request-forgery-cou){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-generic-request-forgery-cou}

The most effective defense against request forgery attacks is to modify
vulnerable services to use strong authentication. However, this is not
always something that is within the control of a QUIC deployment. This
section outlines some other steps that QUIC endpoints could take
unilaterally. These additional steps are all discretionary because,
depending on circumstances, they could interfere with or prevent
legitimate uses.[¶](#section-21.5.6-1){.pilcrow}

Services offered over loopback interfaces often lack proper
authentication. Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} prevent connection attempts or
migration to a loopback address. Endpoints [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} allow
connections or migration to a loopback address if the same service was
previously available at a different interface or if the address was
provided by a service at a non-loopback address. Endpoints that depend
on these capabilities could offer an option to disable these
protections.[¶](#section-21.5.6-2){.pilcrow}

Similarly, endpoints could regard a change in address to a link-local
address \[[RFC4291](#RFC4291){.xref}\] or an address in a private-use
range \[[RFC1918](#RFC1918){.xref}\] from a global, unique-local
\[[RFC4193](#RFC4193){.xref}\], or non-private address as a potential
attempt at request forgery. Endpoints could refuse to use these
addresses entirely, but that carries a significant risk of interfering
with legitimate uses. Endpoints [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} refuse to use an
address unless they have specific knowledge about the network indicating
that sending datagrams to unvalidated addresses in a given range is not
safe.[¶](#section-21.5.6-3){.pilcrow}

Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} choose to reduce the risk of request forgery by
not including values from NEW_TOKEN frames in Initial packets or by only
sending probing frames in packets prior to completing address
validation. Note that this does not prevent an attacker from using the
Destination Connection ID field for an
attack.[¶](#section-21.5.6-4){.pilcrow}

Endpoints are not expected to have specific information about the
location of servers that could be vulnerable targets of a request
forgery attack. However, it might be possible over time to identify
specific UDP ports that are common targets of attacks or particular
patterns in datagrams that are used for attacks. Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14}
choose to avoid sending datagrams to these ports or not send datagrams
that match these patterns prior to validating the destination address.
Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} retire connection IDs containing patterns known
to be problematic without using them.[¶](#section-21.5.6-5){.pilcrow}

Note: Modifying endpoints to apply these protections is more efficient
than deploying network-based protections, as endpoints do not need to
perform any additional processing when sending to an address that has
been validated.[¶](#section-21.5.6-6.1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#slowloris-attacks}
::: {#section-21.6 .section}
### [21.6.](#section-21.6){.section-number .selfRef} [Slowloris Attacks](#name-slowloris-attacks){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-slowloris-attacks}

The attacks commonly known as Slowloris
\[[SLOWLORIS](#SLOWLORIS){.xref}\] try to keep many connections to the
target endpoint open and hold them open as long as possible. These
attacks can be executed against a QUIC endpoint by generating the
minimum amount of activity necessary to avoid being closed for
inactivity. This might involve sending small amounts of data, gradually
opening flow control windows in order to control the sender rate, or
manufacturing ACK frames that simulate a high loss
rate.[¶](#section-21.6-1){.pilcrow}

QUIC deployments [SHOULD]{.bcp14} provide mitigations for the Slowloris
attacks, such as increasing the maximum number of clients the server
will allow, limiting the number of connections a single IP address is
allowed to make, imposing restrictions on the minimum transfer speed a
connection is allowed to have, and restricting the length of time an
endpoint is allowed to stay connected.[¶](#section-21.6-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#stream-fragmentation-and-reassembly-attacks}
::: {#section-21.7 .section}
### [21.7.](#section-21.7){.section-number .selfRef} [Stream Fragmentation and Reassembly Attacks](#name-stream-fragmentation-and-re){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-stream-fragmentation-and-re}

An adversarial sender might intentionally not send portions of the
stream data, causing the receiver to commit resources for the unsent
data. This could cause a disproportionate receive buffer memory
commitment and/or the creation of a large and inefficient data structure
at the receiver.[¶](#section-21.7-1){.pilcrow}

An adversarial receiver might intentionally not acknowledge packets
containing stream data in an attempt to force the sender to store the
unacknowledged stream data for
retransmission.[¶](#section-21.7-2){.pilcrow}

The attack on receivers is mitigated if flow control windows correspond
to available memory. However, some receivers will overcommit memory and
advertise flow control offsets in the aggregate that exceed actual
available memory. The overcommitment strategy can lead to better
performance when endpoints are well behaved, but renders endpoints
vulnerable to the stream fragmentation
attack.[¶](#section-21.7-3){.pilcrow}

QUIC deployments [SHOULD]{.bcp14} provide mitigations for stream
fragmentation attacks. Mitigations could consist of avoiding
overcommitting memory, limiting the size of tracking data structures,
delaying reassembly of STREAM frames, implementing heuristics based on
the age and duration of reassembly holes, or some combination of
these.[¶](#section-21.7-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#stream-commitment-attack}
::: {#section-21.8 .section}
### [21.8.](#section-21.8){.section-number .selfRef} [Stream Commitment Attack](#name-stream-commitment-attack){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-stream-commitment-attack}

An adversarial endpoint can open a large number of streams, exhausting
state on an endpoint. The adversarial endpoint could repeat the process
on a large number of connections, in a manner similar to SYN flooding
attacks in TCP.[¶](#section-21.8-1){.pilcrow}

Normally, clients will open streams sequentially, as explained in
[Section 2.1](#stream-id){.xref}. However, when several streams are
initiated at short intervals, loss or reordering can cause STREAM frames
that open streams to be received out of sequence. On receiving a
higher-numbered stream ID, a receiver is required to open all
intervening streams of the same type; see [Section
3.2](#stream-recv-states){.xref}. Thus, on a new connection, opening
stream 4000000 opens 1 million and 1 client-initiated bidirectional
streams.[¶](#section-21.8-2){.pilcrow}

The number of active streams is limited by the initial_max_streams_bidi
and initial_max_streams_uni transport parameters as updated by any
received MAX_STREAMS frames, as explained in [Section
4.6](#controlling-concurrency){.xref}. If chosen judiciously, these
limits mitigate the effect of the stream commitment attack. However,
setting the limit too low could affect performance when applications
expect to open a large number of streams.[¶](#section-21.8-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#useless}
::: {#section-21.9 .section}
### [21.9.](#section-21.9){.section-number .selfRef} [Peer Denial of Service](#name-peer-denial-of-service){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-peer-denial-of-service}

QUIC and TLS both contain frames or messages that have legitimate uses
in some contexts, but these frames or messages can be abused to cause a
peer to expend processing resources without having any observable impact
on the state of the connection.[¶](#section-21.9-1){.pilcrow}

Messages can also be used to change and revert state in small or
inconsequential ways, such as by sending small increments to flow
control limits.[¶](#section-21.9-2){.pilcrow}

If processing costs are disproportionately large in comparison to
bandwidth consumption or effect on state, then this could allow a
malicious peer to exhaust processing
capacity.[¶](#section-21.9-3){.pilcrow}

While there are legitimate uses for all messages, implementations
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} track cost of processing relative to progress and treat
excessive quantities of any non-productive packets as indicative of an
attack. Endpoints [MAY]{.bcp14} respond to this condition with a
connection error or by dropping packets.[¶](#section-21.9-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#security-ecn}
::: {#section-21.10 .section}
### [21.10.](#section-21.10){.section-number .selfRef} [Explicit Congestion Notification Attacks](#name-explicit-congestion-notifica){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-explicit-congestion-notifica}

An on-path attacker could manipulate the value of ECN fields in the IP
header to influence the sender\'s rate. \[[RFC3168](#RFC3168){.xref}\]
discusses manipulations and their effects in more
detail.[¶](#section-21.10-1){.pilcrow}

A limited on-path attacker can duplicate and send packets with modified
ECN fields to affect the sender\'s rate. If duplicate packets are
discarded by a receiver, an attacker will need to race the duplicate
packet against the original to be successful in this attack. Therefore,
QUIC endpoints ignore the ECN field in an IP packet unless at least one
QUIC packet in that IP packet is successfully processed; see [Section
13.4](#ecn){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.10-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reset-oracle}
::: {#section-21.11 .section}
### [21.11.](#section-21.11){.section-number .selfRef} [Stateless Reset Oracle](#name-stateless-reset-oracle){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-stateless-reset-oracle}

Stateless resets create a possible denial-of-service attack analogous to
a TCP reset injection. This attack is possible if an attacker is able to
cause a stateless reset token to be generated for a connection with a
selected connection ID. An attacker that can cause this token to be
generated can reset an active connection with the same connection
ID.[¶](#section-21.11-1){.pilcrow}

If a packet can be routed to different instances that share a static key
\-- for example, by changing an IP address or port \-- then an attacker
can cause the server to send a stateless reset. To defend against this
style of denial of service, endpoints that share a static key for
stateless resets (see [Section 10.3.2](#reset-token){.xref})
[MUST]{.bcp14} be arranged so that packets with a given connection ID
always arrive at an instance that has connection state, unless that
connection is no longer active.[¶](#section-21.11-2){.pilcrow}

More generally, servers [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} generate a stateless reset if
a connection with the corresponding connection ID could be active on any
endpoint using the same static key.[¶](#section-21.11-3){.pilcrow}

In the case of a cluster that uses dynamic load balancing, it is
possible that a change in load-balancer configuration could occur while
an active instance retains connection state. Even if an instance retains
connection state, the change in routing and resulting stateless reset
will result in the connection being terminated. If there is no chance of
the packet being routed to the correct instance, it is better to send a
stateless reset than wait for the connection to time out. However, this
is acceptable only if the routing cannot be influenced by an
attacker.[¶](#section-21.11-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#version-downgrade}
::: {#section-21.12 .section}
### [21.12.](#section-21.12){.section-number .selfRef} [Version Downgrade](#name-version-downgrade){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-version-downgrade}

This document defines QUIC Version Negotiation packets ([Section
6](#version-negotiation){.xref}), which can be used to negotiate the
QUIC version used between two endpoints. However, this document does not
specify how this negotiation will be performed between this version and
subsequent future versions. In particular, Version Negotiation packets
do not contain any mechanism to prevent version downgrade attacks.
Future versions of QUIC that use Version Negotiation packets
[MUST]{.bcp14} define a mechanism that is robust against version
downgrade attacks.[¶](#section-21.12-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#targeted-attacks-by-routing}
::: {#section-21.13 .section}
### [21.13.](#section-21.13){.section-number .selfRef} [Targeted Attacks by Routing](#name-targeted-attacks-by-routing){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-targeted-attacks-by-routing}

Deployments should limit the ability of an attacker to target a new
connection to a particular server instance. Ideally, routing decisions
are made independently of client-selected values, including addresses.
Once an instance is selected, a connection ID can be selected so that
later packets are routed to the same
instance.[¶](#section-21.13-1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#traffic-analysis}
::: {#section-21.14 .section}
### [21.14.](#section-21.14){.section-number .selfRef} [Traffic Analysis](#name-traffic-analysis){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-traffic-analysis}

The length of QUIC packets can reveal information about the length of
the content of those packets. The PADDING frame is provided so that
endpoints have some ability to obscure the length of packet content; see
[Section 19.1](#frame-padding){.xref}.[¶](#section-21.14-1){.pilcrow}

Defeating traffic analysis is challenging and the subject of active
research. Length is not the only way that information might leak.
Endpoints might also reveal sensitive information through other side
channels, such as the timing of packets.[¶](#section-21.14-2){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#iana}
::: {#section-22 .section}
## [22.](#section-22){.section-number .selfRef} [IANA Considerations](#name-iana-considerations){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-iana-considerations}

This document establishes several registries for the management of
codepoints in QUIC. These registries operate on a common set of policies
as defined in [Section
22.1](#iana-policy){.xref}.[¶](#section-22-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#iana-policy}
::: {#section-22.1 .section}
### [22.1.](#section-22.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Registration Policies for QUIC Registries](#name-registration-policies-for-q){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-registration-policies-for-q}

All QUIC registries allow for both provisional and permanent
registration of codepoints. This section documents policies that are
common to these registries.[¶](#section-22.1-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#iana-provisional}
::: {#section-22.1.1 .section}
#### [22.1.1.](#section-22.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Provisional Registrations](#name-provisional-registrations){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-provisional-registrations}

Provisional registrations of codepoints are intended to allow for
private use and experimentation with extensions to QUIC. Provisional
registrations only require the inclusion of the codepoint value and
contact information. However, provisional registrations could be
reclaimed and reassigned for another
purpose.[¶](#section-22.1.1-1){.pilcrow}

Provisional registrations require Expert Review, as defined in [Section
4.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.5){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]. The designated expert or experts are
advised that only registrations for an excessive proportion of remaining
codepoint space or the very first unassigned value (see [Section
22.1.2](#iana-random){.xref}) can be
rejected.[¶](#section-22.1.1-2){.pilcrow}

Provisional registrations will include a Date field that indicates when
the registration was last updated. A request to update the date on any
provisional registration can be made without review from the designated
expert(s).[¶](#section-22.1.1-3){.pilcrow}

All QUIC registries include the following fields to support provisional
registration:[¶](#section-22.1.1-4){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Value:

:   The assigned codepoint.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Status:

:   \"permanent\" or
    \"provisional\".[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Specification:

:   A reference to a publicly available specification for the
    value.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.6.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Date:

:   The date of the last update to the
    registration.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.8.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Change Controller:

:   The entity that is responsible for the definition of the
    registration.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.10.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Contact:

:   Contact details for the
    registrant.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.12.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Notes:

:   Supplementary notes about the
    registration.[¶](#section-22.1.1-5.14.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Provisional registrations [MAY]{.bcp14} omit the Specification and Notes
fields, plus any additional fields that might be required for a
permanent registration. The Date field is not required as part of
requesting a registration, as it is set to the date the registration is
created or updated.[¶](#section-22.1.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#iana-random}
::: {#section-22.1.2 .section}
#### [22.1.2.](#section-22.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Selecting Codepoints](#name-selecting-codepoints){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-selecting-codepoints}

New requests for codepoints from QUIC registries [SHOULD]{.bcp14} use a
randomly selected codepoint that excludes both existing allocations and
the first unallocated codepoint in the selected space. Requests for
multiple codepoints [MAY]{.bcp14} use a contiguous range. This minimizes
the risk that differing semantics are attributed to the same codepoint
by different implementations.[¶](#section-22.1.2-1){.pilcrow}

The use of the first unassigned codepoint is reserved for allocation
using the Standards Action policy; see [Section
4.9](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.9){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]. The early codepoint assignment process
\[[EARLY-ASSIGN](#EARLY-ASSIGN){.xref}\] can be used for these
values.[¶](#section-22.1.2-2){.pilcrow}

For codepoints that are encoded in variable-length integers ([Section
16](#integer-encoding){.xref}), such as frame types, codepoints that
encode to four or eight bytes (that is, values 2^14^ and above)
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be used unless the usage is especially sensitive to
having a longer encoding.[¶](#section-22.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

Applications to register codepoints in QUIC registries [MAY]{.bcp14}
include a requested codepoint as part of the registration. IANA
[MUST]{.bcp14} allocate the selected codepoint if the codepoint is
unassigned and the requirements of the registration policy are
met.[¶](#section-22.1.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reclaiming-provisional-codepoints}
::: {#section-22.1.3 .section}
#### [22.1.3.](#section-22.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Reclaiming Provisional Codepoints](#name-reclaiming-provisional-code){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-reclaiming-provisional-code}

A request might be made to remove an unused provisional registration
from the registry to reclaim space in a registry, or a portion of the
registry (such as the 64-16383 range for codepoints that use
variable-length encodings). This [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be done only for the
codepoints with the earliest recorded date, and entries that have been
updated less than a year prior [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} be
reclaimed.[¶](#section-22.1.3-1){.pilcrow}

A request to remove a codepoint [MUST]{.bcp14} be reviewed by the
designated experts. The experts [MUST]{.bcp14} attempt to determine
whether the codepoint is still in use. Experts are advised to contact
the listed contacts for the registration, plus as wide a set of protocol
implementers as possible in order to determine whether any use of the
codepoint is known. The experts are also advised to allow at least four
weeks for responses.[¶](#section-22.1.3-2){.pilcrow}

If any use of the codepoints is identified by this search or a request
to update the registration is made, the codepoint [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be
reclaimed. Instead, the date on the registration is updated. A note
might be added for the registration recording relevant information that
was learned.[¶](#section-22.1.3-3){.pilcrow}

If no use of the codepoint was identified and no request was made to
update the registration, the codepoint [MAY]{.bcp14} be removed from the
registry.[¶](#section-22.1.3-4){.pilcrow}

This review and consultation process also applies to requests to change
a provisional registration into a permanent registration, except that
the goal is not to determine whether there is no use of the codepoint
but to determine that the registration is an accurate representation of
any deployed usage.[¶](#section-22.1.3-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#iana-permanent}
::: {#section-22.1.4 .section}
#### [22.1.4.](#section-22.1.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Permanent Registrations](#name-permanent-registrations){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-permanent-registrations}

Permanent registrations in QUIC registries use the Specification
Required policy ([Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]), unless otherwise specified. The
designated expert or experts verify that a specification exists and is
readily accessible. Experts are encouraged to be biased towards
approving registrations unless they are abusive, frivolous, or actively
harmful (not merely aesthetically displeasing or architecturally
dubious). The creation of a registry [MAY]{.bcp14} specify additional
constraints on permanent registrations.[¶](#section-22.1.4-1){.pilcrow}

The creation of a registry [MAY]{.bcp14} identify a range of codepoints
where registrations are governed by a different registration policy. For
instance, the \"QUIC Frame Types\" registry ([Section
22.4](#iana-frames){.xref}) has a stricter policy for codepoints in the
range from 0 to 63.[¶](#section-22.1.4-2){.pilcrow}

Any stricter requirements for permanent registrations do not prevent
provisional registrations for affected codepoints. For instance, a
provisional registration for a frame type of 61 could be
requested.[¶](#section-22.1.4-3){.pilcrow}

All registrations made by Standards Track publications [MUST]{.bcp14} be
permanent.[¶](#section-22.1.4-4){.pilcrow}

All registrations in this document are assigned a permanent status and
list a change controller of the IETF and a contact of the QUIC Working
Group (quic\@ietf.org).[¶](#section-22.1.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#iana-version}
::: {#section-22.2 .section}
### [22.2.](#section-22.2){.section-number .selfRef} [QUIC Versions Registry](#name-quic-versions-registry){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-quic-versions-registry}

IANA has added a registry for \"QUIC Versions\" under a \"QUIC\"
heading.[¶](#section-22.2-1){.pilcrow}

The \"QUIC Versions\" registry governs a 32-bit space; see [Section
15](#versions){.xref}. This registry follows the registration policy
from [Section 22.1](#iana-policy){.xref}. Permanent registrations in
this registry are assigned using the Specification Required policy
([Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-22.2-2){.pilcrow}

The codepoint of 0x00000001 for the protocol is assigned with permanent
status to the protocol defined in this document. The codepoint of
0x00000000 is permanently reserved; the note for this codepoint
indicates that this version is reserved for version
negotiation.[¶](#section-22.2-3){.pilcrow}

All codepoints that follow the pattern 0x?a?a?a?a are reserved, [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} be assigned by IANA, and [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} appear in the
listing of assigned values.[¶](#section-22.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#iana-transport-parameters}
::: {#section-22.3 .section}
### [22.3.](#section-22.3){.section-number .selfRef} [QUIC Transport Parameters Registry](#name-quic-transport-parameters-r){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-quic-transport-parameters-r}

IANA has added a registry for \"QUIC Transport Parameters\" under a
\"QUIC\" heading.[¶](#section-22.3-1){.pilcrow}

The \"QUIC Transport Parameters\" registry governs a 62-bit space. This
registry follows the registration policy from [Section
22.1](#iana-policy){.xref}. Permanent registrations in this registry are
assigned using the Specification Required policy ([Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]), except for values between 0x00 and 0x3f
(in hexadecimal), inclusive, which are assigned using Standards Action
or IESG Approval as defined in Sections
[4.9](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.9){.relref} and
[4.10](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.10){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\].[¶](#section-22.3-2){.pilcrow}

In addition to the fields listed in [Section
22.1.1](#iana-provisional){.xref}, permanent registrations in this
registry [MUST]{.bcp14} include the following
field:[¶](#section-22.3-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Parameter Name:

:   A short mnemonic for the
    parameter.[¶](#section-22.3-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

The initial contents of this registry are shown in [Table
6](#iana-tp-table){.xref}.[¶](#section-22.3-5){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-initial-quic-transport-para}

::: {#iana-tp-table}
  Value   Parameter Name                        Specification
  ------- ------------------------------------- ---------------------------------------------------------
  0x00    original_destination_connection_id    [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x01    max_idle_timeout                      [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x02    stateless_reset_token                 [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x03    max_udp_payload_size                  [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x04    initial_max_data                      [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x05    initial_max_stream_data_bidi_local    [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x06    initial_max_stream_data_bidi_remote   [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x07    initial_max_stream_data_uni           [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x08    initial_max_streams_bidi              [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x09    initial_max_streams_uni               [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0a    ack_delay_exponent                    [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0b    max_ack_delay                         [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0c    disable_active_migration              [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0d    preferred_address                     [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0e    active_connection_id_limit            [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x0f    initial_source_connection_id          [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}
  0x10    retry_source_connection_id            [Section 18.2](#transport-parameter-definitions){.xref}

  : [Table 6](#table-6){.selfRef}: [Initial QUIC Transport Parameters
  Registry Entries](#name-initial-quic-transport-para){.selfRef}
:::

Each value of the form `31 * N + 27` for integer values of N (that is,
27, 58, 89, \...) are reserved; these values [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be
assigned by IANA and [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} appear in the listing of
assigned values.[¶](#section-22.3-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#iana-frames}
::: {#section-22.4 .section}
### [22.4.](#section-22.4){.section-number .selfRef} [QUIC Frame Types Registry](#name-quic-frame-types-registry){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-quic-frame-types-registry}

IANA has added a registry for \"QUIC Frame Types\" under a \"QUIC\"
heading.[¶](#section-22.4-1){.pilcrow}

The \"QUIC Frame Types\" registry governs a 62-bit space. This registry
follows the registration policy from [Section
22.1](#iana-policy){.xref}. Permanent registrations in this registry are
assigned using the Specification Required policy ([Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]), except for values between 0x00 and 0x3f
(in hexadecimal), inclusive, which are assigned using Standards Action
or IESG Approval as defined in Sections
[4.9](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.9){.relref} and
[4.10](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.10){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\].[¶](#section-22.4-2){.pilcrow}

In addition to the fields listed in [Section
22.1.1](#iana-provisional){.xref}, permanent registrations in this
registry [MUST]{.bcp14} include the following
field:[¶](#section-22.4-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Frame Type Name:

:   A short mnemonic for the frame
    type.[¶](#section-22.4-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

In addition to the advice in [Section 22.1](#iana-policy){.xref},
specifications for new permanent registrations [SHOULD]{.bcp14} describe
the means by which an endpoint might determine that it can send the
identified type of frame. An accompanying transport parameter
registration is expected for most registrations; see [Section
22.3](#iana-transport-parameters){.xref}. Specifications for permanent
registrations also need to describe the format and assigned semantics of
any fields in the frame.[¶](#section-22.4-5){.pilcrow}

The initial contents of this registry are tabulated in [Table
3](#frame-types){.xref}. Note that the registry does not include the
\"Pkts\" and \"Spec\" columns from [Table
3](#frame-types){.xref}.[¶](#section-22.4-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#iana-error-codes}
::: {#section-22.5 .section}
### [22.5.](#section-22.5){.section-number .selfRef} [QUIC Transport Error Codes Registry](#name-quic-transport-error-codes-){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-quic-transport-error-codes-}

IANA has added a registry for \"QUIC Transport Error Codes\" under a
\"QUIC\" heading.[¶](#section-22.5-1){.pilcrow}

The \"QUIC Transport Error Codes\" registry governs a 62-bit space. This
space is split into three ranges that are governed by different
policies. Permanent registrations in this registry are assigned using
the Specification Required policy ([Section
4.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.6){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]), except for values between 0x00 and 0x3f
(in hexadecimal), inclusive, which are assigned using Standards Action
or IESG Approval as defined in Sections
[4.9](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.9){.relref} and
[4.10](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8126#section-4.10){.relref} of
\[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\].[¶](#section-22.5-2){.pilcrow}

In addition to the fields listed in [Section
22.1.1](#iana-provisional){.xref}, permanent registrations in this
registry [MUST]{.bcp14} include the following
fields:[¶](#section-22.5-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

Code:

:   A short mnemonic for the
    parameter.[¶](#section-22.5-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

:   

Description:

:   A brief description of the error code semantics, which [MAY]{.bcp14}
    be a summary if a specification reference is
    provided.[¶](#section-22.5-4.4.1){.pilcrow}

:   

The initial contents of this registry are shown in [Table
7](#iana-error-table){.xref}.[¶](#section-22.5-5){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-initial-quic-transport-erro}

::: {#iana-error-table}
  Value            Code                        Description                               Specification
  ---------------- --------------------------- ----------------------------------------- -----------------------------------
  0x00             NO_ERROR                    No error                                  [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x01             INTERNAL_ERROR              Implementation error                      [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x02             CONNECTION_REFUSED          Server refuses a connection               [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x03             FLOW_CONTROL_ERROR          Flow control error                        [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x04             STREAM_LIMIT_ERROR          Too many streams opened                   [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x05             STREAM_STATE_ERROR          Frame received in invalid stream state    [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x06             FINAL_SIZE_ERROR            Change to final size                      [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x07             FRAME_ENCODING_ERROR        Frame encoding error                      [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x08             TRANSPORT_PARAMETER_ERROR   Error in transport parameters             [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x09             CONNECTION_ID_LIMIT_ERROR   Too many connection IDs received          [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0a             PROTOCOL_VIOLATION          Generic protocol violation                [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0b             INVALID_TOKEN               Invalid Token received                    [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0c             APPLICATION_ERROR           Application error                         [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0d             CRYPTO_BUFFER_EXCEEDED      CRYPTO data buffer overflowed             [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0e             KEY_UPDATE_ERROR            Invalid packet protection update          [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0f             AEAD_LIMIT_REACHED          Excessive use of packet protection keys   [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x10             NO_VIABLE_PATH              No viable network path exists             [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}
  0x0100-​0x01ff   CRYPTO_ERROR                TLS alert code                            [Section 20](#error-codes){.xref}

  : [Table 7](#table-7){.selfRef}: [Initial QUIC Transport Error Codes
  Registry Entries](#name-initial-quic-transport-erro){.selfRef}
:::
:::
:::
:::
