::: {#pseudocode}
::: {#section-appendix.a .section}
## [Appendix A.](#section-appendix.a){.section-number .selfRef} [Pseudocode](#name-pseudocode){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-pseudocode}

The pseudocode in this section describes sample algorithms. These
algorithms are intended to be correct and clear, rather than being
optimally performant.[¶](#section-appendix.a-1){.pilcrow}

The pseudocode segments in this section are licensed as Code Components;
see the Copyright Notice.[¶](#section-appendix.a-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#sample-varint}
::: {#section-a.1 .section}
## [A.1.](#section-a.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Sample Variable-Length Integer Decoding](#name-sample-variable-length-inte){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sample-variable-length-inte}

The pseudocode in [Figure 45](#alg-varint){.xref} shows how a
variable-length integer can be read from a stream of bytes. The function
ReadVarint takes a single argument \-- a sequence of bytes, which can be
read in network byte order.[¶](#section-a.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#name-sample-variable-length-integ}

::: {#alg-varint}
::: {#section-a.1-2.1}
``` {.sourcecode .lang-pseudocode}
ReadVarint(data):
  // The length of variable-length integers is encoded in the
  // first two bits of the first byte.
  v = data.next_byte()
  prefix = v >> 6
  length = 1 << prefix

  // Once the length is known, remove these bits and read any
  // remaining bytes.
  v = v & 0x3f
  repeat length-1 times:
    v = (v << 8) + data.next_byte()
  return v
```
:::

[Figure 45](#figure-45){.selfRef}: [Sample Variable-Length Integer
Decoding Algorithm](#name-sample-variable-length-integ){.selfRef}
:::

For example, the eight-byte sequence 0xc2197c5eff14e88c decodes to the
decimal value 151,288,809,941,952,652; the four-byte sequence 0x9d7f3e7d
decodes to 494,878,333; the two-byte sequence 0x7bbd decodes to 15,293;
and the single byte 0x25 decodes to 37 (as does the two-byte sequence
0x4025).[¶](#section-a.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#sample-packet-number-encoding}
::: {#section-a.2 .section}
## [A.2.](#section-a.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Sample Packet Number Encoding Algorithm](#name-sample-packet-number-encodi){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sample-packet-number-encodi}

The pseudocode in [Figure 46](#alg-encode-pn){.xref} shows how an
implementation can select an appropriate size for packet number
encodings.[¶](#section-a.2-1){.pilcrow}

The EncodePacketNumber function takes two
arguments:[¶](#section-a.2-2){.pilcrow}

-   [full_pn is the full packet number of the packet being
    sent.[¶](#section-a.2-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-a.2-3.1}
-   [largest_acked is the largest packet number that has been
    acknowledged by the peer in the current packet number space, if
    any.[¶](#section-a.2-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-a.2-3.2}

[]{#name-sample-packet-number-encodin}

::: {#alg-encode-pn}
::: {#section-a.2-4.1}
``` {.sourcecode .lang-pseudocode}
EncodePacketNumber(full_pn, largest_acked):

  // The number of bits must be at least one more
  // than the base-2 logarithm of the number of contiguous
  // unacknowledged packet numbers, including the new packet.
  if largest_acked is None:
    num_unacked = full_pn + 1
  else:
    num_unacked = full_pn - largest_acked

  min_bits = log(num_unacked, 2) + 1
  num_bytes = ceil(min_bits / 8)

  // Encode the integer value and truncate to
  // the num_bytes least significant bytes.
  return encode(full_pn, num_bytes)
```
:::

[Figure 46](#figure-46){.selfRef}: [Sample Packet Number Encoding
Algorithm](#name-sample-packet-number-encodin){.selfRef}
:::

For example, if an endpoint has received an acknowledgment for packet
0xabe8b3 and is sending a packet with a number of 0xac5c02, there are
29,519 (0x734f) outstanding packet numbers. In order to represent at
least twice this range (59,038 packets, or 0xe69e), 16 bits are
required.[¶](#section-a.2-5){.pilcrow}

In the same state, sending a packet with a number of 0xace8fe uses the
24-bit encoding, because at least 18 bits are required to represent
twice the range (131,222 packets, or
0x020096).[¶](#section-a.2-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#sample-packet-number-decoding}
::: {#section-a.3 .section}
## [A.3.](#section-a.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Sample Packet Number Decoding Algorithm](#name-sample-packet-number-decodi){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sample-packet-number-decodi}

The pseudocode in [Figure 47](#alg-decode-pn){.xref} includes an example
algorithm for decoding packet numbers after header protection has been
removed.[¶](#section-a.3-1){.pilcrow}

The DecodePacketNumber function takes three
arguments:[¶](#section-a.3-2){.pilcrow}

-   [largest_pn is the largest packet number that has been successfully
    processed in the current packet number
    space.[¶](#section-a.3-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-a.3-3.1}
-   [truncated_pn is the value of the Packet Number
    field.[¶](#section-a.3-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-a.3-3.2}
-   [pn_nbits is the number of bits in the Packet Number field (8, 16,
    24, or 32).[¶](#section-a.3-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-a.3-3.3}

[]{#name-sample-packet-number-decodin}

::: {#alg-decode-pn}
::: {#section-a.3-4.1}
``` {.sourcecode .lang-pseudocode}
DecodePacketNumber(largest_pn, truncated_pn, pn_nbits):
   expected_pn  = largest_pn + 1
   pn_win       = 1 << pn_nbits
   pn_hwin      = pn_win / 2
   pn_mask      = pn_win - 1
   // The incoming packet number should be greater than
   // expected_pn - pn_hwin and less than or equal to
   // expected_pn + pn_hwin
   //
   // This means we cannot just strip the trailing bits from
   // expected_pn and add the truncated_pn because that might
   // yield a value outside the window.
   //
   // The following code calculates a candidate value and
   // makes sure it's within the packet number window.
   // Note the extra checks to prevent overflow and underflow.
   candidate_pn = (expected_pn & ~pn_mask) | truncated_pn
   if candidate_pn <= expected_pn - pn_hwin and
      candidate_pn < (1 << 62) - pn_win:
      return candidate_pn + pn_win
   if candidate_pn > expected_pn + pn_hwin and
      candidate_pn >= pn_win:
      return candidate_pn - pn_win
   return candidate_pn
```
:::

[Figure 47](#figure-47){.selfRef}: [Sample Packet Number Decoding
Algorithm](#name-sample-packet-number-decodin){.selfRef}
:::

For example, if the highest successfully authenticated packet had a
packet number of 0xa82f30ea, then a packet containing a 16-bit value of
0x9b32 will be decoded as 0xa82f9b32.[¶](#section-a.3-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#ecn-alg}
::: {#section-a.4 .section}
## [A.4.](#section-a.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Sample ECN Validation Algorithm](#name-sample-ecn-validation-algor){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-sample-ecn-validation-algor}

Each time an endpoint commences sending on a new network path, it
determines whether the path supports ECN; see [Section
13.4](#ecn){.xref}. If the path supports ECN, the goal is to use ECN.
Endpoints might also periodically reassess a path that was determined to
not support ECN.[¶](#section-a.4-1){.pilcrow}

This section describes one method for testing new paths. This algorithm
is intended to show how a path might be tested for ECN support.
Endpoints can implement different methods.[¶](#section-a.4-2){.pilcrow}

The path is assigned an ECN state that is one of \"testing\",
\"unknown\", \"failed\", or \"capable\". On paths with a \"testing\" or
\"capable\" state, the endpoint sends packets with an ECT marking \--
ECT(0) by default; otherwise, the endpoint sends unmarked
packets.[¶](#section-a.4-3){.pilcrow}

To start testing a path, the ECN state is set to \"testing\", and
existing ECN counts are remembered as a
baseline.[¶](#section-a.4-4){.pilcrow}

The testing period runs for a number of packets or a limited time, as
determined by the endpoint. The goal is not to limit the duration of the
testing period but to ensure that enough marked packets are sent for
received ECN counts to provide a clear indication of how the path treats
marked packets. [Section 13.4.2](#ecn-validation){.xref} suggests
limiting this to ten packets or three times the
PTO.[¶](#section-a.4-5){.pilcrow}

After the testing period ends, the ECN state for the path becomes
\"unknown\". From the \"unknown\" state, successful validation of the
ECN counts in an ACK frame (see [Section 13.4.2.1](#ecn-ack){.xref})
causes the ECN state for the path to become \"capable\", unless no
marked packet has been acknowledged.[¶](#section-a.4-6){.pilcrow}

If validation of ECN counts fails at any time, the ECN state for the
affected path becomes \"failed\". An endpoint can also mark the ECN
state for a path as \"failed\" if marked packets are all declared lost
or if they are all ECN-CE marked.[¶](#section-a.4-7){.pilcrow}

Following this algorithm ensures that ECN is rarely disabled for paths
that properly support ECN. Any path that incorrectly modifies markings
will cause ECN to be disabled. For those rare cases where marked packets
are discarded by the path, the short duration of the testing period
limits the number of losses incurred.[¶](#section-a.4-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

## [Contributors](#name-contributors){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-contributors}

The original design and rationale behind this protocol draw
significantly from work by [Jim Roskind]{.contact-name}
\[[EARLY-DESIGN](#EARLY-DESIGN){.xref}\].[¶](#section-appendix.b-1){.pilcrow}

The IETF QUIC Working Group received an enormous amount of support from
many people. The following people provided substantive contributions to
